THE RED PAGE.
I FI DELIS.
[For The Bulletin.]
Fidelia was the word ,
A rosebud smile the wand
To touch my soul, that stirred
An ecstasy beyond,
Like a soaring bird.
The bird is in the skies ;
My heart was even there,
Where Summer's cradle lies,
Rocked by a secret air
Slipped from Paradise.
The Summer light it goes,
The bird away it flies,
And Love is one with those —■
The rose that never dies
Never was a rose !
Maoriland. Hubekt Church.
Turn over pictures of bygone fashions in dress,
and note how strange, even ludicrous, may now
appear the costumes of our grandfathers and
grandmothers ! The patches and hoops, the
cravats and buckled shoes, are as quaint to the
eye of this generation as this generation’s dress
might seem to the eyes of a gentleman of the court
of Queen Anne. La Mode has changed even since
the day of our fathers. But our fathers for the
most part have been content to change with the
ifiode ; and if here and there one clings to the
garb of his boyhood he doesn’t dream of insisting
that his sons shall imitate him. Even when justi-
fying his preference he will rarely venture to
declare all other preferences unjustifiable.
* * * *
Fashions in poetry have been almost as numer-
ous as fashions in dress; but in poetry the dead
hand of our ancestors is heavy upon us. Nowadays
we neither wear slashed breeches nor write inter-
minable epics to exalt religious dogma ; but though
it is commonly agreed that gOod taste may be dis-
played in trousers, a considerable body of opinion
still holds that only very bad taste can refuse to re-
cognise “Paradise Lost” and “ Paradise Regained
as two of the highest poetical achievements of the
human mind. In reality, according to the stan-
dards set by present fashion, there is little more
essential poetry in the “ Paradises ” than there is
in Queen Victoria. Like Queen Victoria, Milton s
epics are heavy, and faded, and invested with a
fatiguing magnificence : and they continue to be
revered as Queen Victoria is revered—beoause we
have inherited the tradition of reverence from our
fathers, and are not strong enough to shake it off.
• *
Similarly, though Homer and Dante directly
provide but an infinitesimal portion of the poetioal
stimulus which this generation is receiving, it is
continually declared ex cathedra that Homer and
Dante are the world’s two greatest poets, while
modern writers of verse are little more than the
dust which drifts about their large historic feet.
To argue this question with the (other) dogmatists
it would be necessary to agree upon a definition of
poetry. But such agreement is not necessary to
the affirmation that most Australian readers of
verse know and care little whether Homer and
Dante were poets or palmists. They accept the
I tradition, of course; bow to the printed name of
Dante, and believe that Homer is something vast
and venerable in the verse-line; but the poetical
areas of their minds are occupied, not with
Achilles or Beatrice, but with the Man from Iron-
bark or the ltiver Maiden. Even if you ask of
those who may be supposed to possess cultured or
classical taste, you find they are reading Keats, or
Heine, or Stephen Phillips ; if ycpu notice whom
they take pains to write about, it is Browning, or
Mallarine, or Kipling. Dante and Homer are
very fine fellows, no doubt; but they are on the
shelf with the old maids and Reid s Local Grovern-
ment Bill.
* * • *
What is true of Australia is true of other
countries: people are everywhere occupied for the
most part with present-day poets, and Dante ana
Homer receive far more lip-service than heart-
incense. It is here contended, indeed, that this is
just and right. Homer was no doubt a great poet or
several great poets in his time ; but that is not our
time. We have other minds, other tastes, other
needs. Homer’s beauty and humanity wil 1 always
have their value ; but the plain emotions, the
simple scenes, which may be presumed to have
intoxicated the Greeks of 2700 years ago, are not the
highest ■ poetical boon which modern readers
can desire. Nor, in spite of tragic passion and
picturesque phrasing, are the imaginative vagaries
of Dante more sufficing. According to present
lights, the Dante and Homer of tradition are
25 per cent, genuine poetry and 7o per cent,
academic humbug—Brocken -spectres whose huge
apparent bulk terrifies the average person because
he never dares stop to scrutinise closely the homely
mortal who casts the shadow. Some collateral
proof of this, in Dante’s case, has been recently
furnished by Q. Chief-Justice Griffith. This gen-
tleman has published a translation of some frag-
ments of the Inferno, and the Queenslander gives
a sample of his results. Led into the doleful
I lost souls, Dante is supposed to have
red Francesca and her lover”—

* * * *
be argued, indeed, that much modern
s essentially a parasitic growth upon the
f Homer and Dante and Milton. Doubt-
y treasured ideas and images have been
from their store. Not only our daws, but
cocks, often strut in borrowed feathers,
whose feathers did the peacock of anti-
rut ? Homer, it is admitted, collected the
of a generation, probably of an era : he
,ve been merely the editor of a ballad
gy : when.all the guesses are sifted we

There is evidence to support the theory
eking the basis of Vondel's “ Lucifer ”
would never have had a “ Paradise ” to
We all stand on the shoulders of the I ast.
iile it is comparatively easy to detect the
isms of modern writers, the geese plucked
ish ancient quills have flown out of sight
he vista of the centuries. The one thing
is that between ancient and modern dis-
•s are easy.
* * * * .
worship of names illustrates the familiar
if habit. Homage to “classic” writers is m
lersons partly or wholly automatic. When
ant learns to walk you see it devoting its
mind to taking one step and another. It
its mouth, fixes its gaze, wrinkles its fore-
Its elders encourage if by examples which
jriously imitates. It works with its whole
and every hesitating advance is a reasoned
But by degrees the act' becomes a habit:
x path is worn in the cerebrum, and the auto-
centres take charge of the legs. Upon an
se from consciousness walking continues un-
ously. By-and-by even the impulse becomes
scious ; the limbs obey a hint so impercepti-
iat it passes unrealised. We walk without
ng it, as Monsieur Jourdain spoke prose.
io the schoolboy, learning from his master or
xt-book that Milton was a great poet, begins
Ik mentally in the path his elders have trod.
> more asks why than as an infant he asked
he should toddle : he imitates the belief as
litated the act. As he grows, the first
impression on his brain is repeated until
is a fixed- idea from which it is hardly
>le to escape. The din of tradition has
, permanent dint. His mind repeats its
automatically—“ Milton is a great poet.
f he would unlearn the lesson, he is met by
heal obstacle .as real as if he tried to unlearn
ng. So, as a rule, his prepossession triumphs
eeble doubts : you can no more persuade him
ive off Milton than to leave off drink or
>l. „ »
* * * *
s argument drifts round to Brunton Stephens
fennyson. In verse and prose Brunton
iens has made his mark upon Australian
s: there is no man in Australia whose
ry judgment is more to be respected. But
ton Stephens is a man of Tennyson s
ation : his mind was m youthful
ty when Tennyson was establishing
jry over that generation: lie has assi-
ed all the plaudits of the crowd which
red in Tennyson’s heyday that the age had
no greater poet, doubtfully a poet as great,
mid be astonishing if the Tennyson impress
m Brunton Stephens had not become a source
itomatic opinion partly independent of ms
ir judgment. Every mans ideas are per-
,ntly coloured by celebrated contemporaries:
i is an infection of fame winch pits brains
mmstakably as smallpox pits bodies We
l prevalent authors just as we catch the pre-
it influenza. Of course Brunton Stephens
ht Tennyson:' it was the obvious thing tor
tton Stephens to do. But there is a valuable
ical law which makes the toxins of one gene-
n provide the anti-toxins of the next: the
s which our fathers endure gradually earn us
unity from our fathers’ diseases. I or example,
as a result? of the historic ravages of syphilis, there
is ground for belief that syphilis is dying out, be-
cause in centuries of struggle the race has learnt
the noble art of self-defence. The poetic analogy
is plain. We can defy the microbesof Tupper and
Bailey. The microbe of Pope finds us, on the
whole, an unsuitable culture-medium. And the
microbe of Tennyson is not half as dangerous as it
was fifty years ago, when it came down like a
dingo on the unvaccinated flock.
Brunton Stephens’s poetical work has been
influenced by many poets ; but notably by Tenny-
son. In his book of collected verses "The
Squatter’s Baccy Famine” parodies “Mariana” ;
“ What does little Joey say,” in “ Marsupial
Bill, ” is another obvious imitation ; and through-
out the book there fere frequent hints in phrase
and thought to show how the elder poet has
swayed the younger.' For example, the lines in
“ Mute Discourse ”
Oh, Sister, would I bruise
The snowy petals of thy prayerful faith.
Or chill the of thy hope
With evil influence of wintry scorn?
are a simple repetition of
„ Leave thou thy sister when she prays,
Hef early Heaven, her happy views ;
Nbr-thou with shadowed hint confuse
A life that leads melodious days.
—ln Memoriam, xxxiii.
» ■* * 0
“ In Memoriam ” wap published in 18'>0, when
Brunton Stephens was a lad of 15 ; and to this
day he is occupied with the effort to reconcile
faith and reason which is “In Memoriam’s”
staple. The inspiration of lines like
We have but faith : we cannot know ;
For knowledge is of things we see ;
And yet we trust it comes from thee,
A beam in darkness: let it grow,
and many similar lines in “In Memoriam ” is
precisely the inspiration seen 47 years later m
Brunton Stephons’s “The Chamber of l'aith —-
And Who knows hut beyond the narrow scope
Of these chamber walls [Faith 1 lives again,
A transmuted force unnamed of men,
One wave whereof is this trembling hope,
That beyond the beyond and above the above
There yet may be things we know not of.
Nothing is more probable than that there is an
Unknown beyond the Known; but poetical
discussion of such matters is out-of-date in a
generation which takes its limitations for granted.
Brunton Stephens, in fact, has preserved nud-
century doubts and problems till they are as
unsuited to their surroundings as mid-century
dress-fashions would be. So mighty an influence
did Tennyson wield upon one youthful bram .
* * * *
Now we begin to see where Brunton Stephens
stands in relation to .Tennyson. 'I he thing that
detracts most from Tennyson’s claim to tlm highest
poetic rank is the very thing which seemed to the
men of his generation the chief support of Ills
claim. Beyond all other verse-writers lie em-
bodied the spirit of his time. But his time lias
gone, and the spirit of his time has gone, and with
them ha- «ono the {ovo* of much yf Tennyson s
poetry. He wrote for the men of Ins day, and lie
was rewarded by the unqualified admirationof
the men of his day, and the chief cause of his
temporary success is the seed of his (by comiuni-
son) permanent failure. To Brunton Stephens
Tennyson is still the Tennyson of the fifties and
sixties, because Brunton Stephens is the Stephens
of the fifties and sixties. because
we are leaving the fane where he is still an ardent
worshipper. But the fane is a fixture, and the
world moves. Possibly it is moving in the wrong
direction : anyway, it moves.
* * * *
Almost of necessity the ancient disciple fails
to perceive the rents which younger eyes-
see in the garment of the master. Why
was it that Huxley and Tyndall revered
Tennyson? Because he made them feel what
they had hitherto only known.’ Precisely.
Huxley and Tyndall and Tennyson were corks
which floated at the same speed in the same place
on the same current, and naturally they attracted
each other. But we have, floated past lennyson
and Huxley and Tyndall. If Tennyson had con-
fined himself to the creation of beauty, or the
decoration of permanent truths, he tnighfc_be h-Id
as great a poet to-day as he was in 1870. Bit
when he set himself to inculcate philosophic d
dogmas which appealed to Huxley and Tynda ,
he was destroying his power over the poetical sense
of a generation which has ceased to believe in the
philosophical dogmas that appealed to Huxley or
Tvndall. He “invested their knowledge with
spiritual attributes’’—and the “ spiritual attri-
butes ” have gone out-of-date with the know-
ledge.” , . .
When it is alleged that Tennyson had a knack
of glorifying commonplace, it is meant that the
result often remains commonplace in spite of the
glorification. When it is alleged that Tennjsqn
lacked passion, it is not meant that he failed to
“ let himself go ” in exaltation of a fleshly Dolol '® s '
but that his nature was cold and selfish, and the
poetical development of his nature was cold and
artificial. When it is alleged that lennysons
ideas were for the most part conventional ideas, it
is meant that he was essentially sheepish, a
follower not a leader of thought, the mate
who always stayed at home and did the
cooking, not the mate who hunted round and
got the tucker. Discussion as to whether it is a
poet’s “business” or “.duty to possess this or
that quality, or to exercise this or that function,
can proceed till the sun freezes. It is
the present if something been done ~~u ses
why, given that Tennyson is a poet, , ■
which com pel Bruntou Stephens to cons
“high among the great poets ” are almost oi
necessity beyond Brunton Stephenss control.
A WISH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dear Love, a brief hour's brilliant bliss,
A flood of light divine,
Then darkness, closing with a kiss
From colder lips than thine.
A song as of a thousand men,
Foiling from heights above,
A cadence in the strain—and then
Sweet Death upon sweet Love.

existence. Members are invited to make this a RECORD YEAR by taking out additional policies themselves and bv
inducing their friends who are not already assured to become members. ALL POLICIES ISSUED before
the end of the year will share in the division of profits to be made as at 31st December next Amount of Cash Surplus
divided amongst the members during the 49 years ending 1897 was £7,726,170. The Cash Bonus distributed amongst
the Policyholders for the single year 1897 was £445,235. EVERY YEAR A BONUS YEAR.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To-Night!
The lamps will be lit over-seas to-night,
And the feast of the year be spread,
And the girls will gather with faces bright,
And the wine will sparkle red,
And hands will close on the glass’s stem,
And over the Christmas cheer
The boys will be drinking ‘ Long life to them ! ”
On the happiest day in the year.
And, spite of the sorrow that hides for shame
In the brown locks streaked with gray,
Though a father may frown at a whispered name,
Yet a mother will have her way ;
For a son’s disgrace is a sword to smite,
But Time is a balm to heal,
And in many a home in the North to-night
They will drink to their Ne’er-do-weel.
The township streets will be full to-night
With the bushmen from far and near
Who have ridden to share in the wild delight
Of the merriest day in the year,
And men will come from the dusty street
And stand at the crowded bar,
And maybe a memory soft and sweet
Will float to some heart from far —
A flashing of lights in a lordly home,
And a glitter of lifted hands,
As they drink to the health of the boys who roam
In those different distant lands;
And there in the midst of the noisy host,
In a sorrow that none can feel,
Will be fashioned, it may be, a silent toast
In the heart of some Ne’er-do-weel.
Deo. 25, 1898.
WILL H. OCILVIE.
The Sting of the Sea.
The dawning falls on thy sibilant breast,
And fisher boats float like gulls in the West,
And the cliffs are silent, and all is still
Save the north-wind’s murmur over the hill.
O, the morning passes, and the day is long,
And the night is sad with thy grievous song 1
I cannot look on thee, luminous Sea—
Thy voice is a pain that withereth ine ;
The beat of thy billows is like my heart
In ceaseless passion and in mournful part:
O, the long day drags, and the night is dree
With thy hungry voices, thou dour grey Sea I
I cannot look on thee, treacherous Sea,
Till thou givest back my boy unto me,
And Hosethe sight of his wild wet eyes,
And I hear not his voice in thy sobbing cries :
O, the long wave heaves, and the red kelp strains,
And the day drags under its load of pains !
O, the long wave heaves, and the shorecoming roll
Hath foam on its lips like the snow in my soul,
And the black rocks weep where the mourner
stands
’Mid the cold despair of the desolate sands.
And the long day drags, and the night is dree
With the sob of my heart and the sob of the Sea.
Tas.
BARKIS.
The Hermit’s Bed.
Ah, leave to worn and palsied age
The horrors of a hermitage,
For prayer is sweeter to the breath
If Love in glory entereth !
While eyes are blue and lips are red,
I shall forswear a Hermit Bed.
But when the winter’s creeping in,
And this gold hair is dull and thin,
When rounded limbs have lost their grace,
Then lead me to the loneliest place:
When all of Beauty’s charms are sped,
Then be the grave my Hermit Bed.
THE PAGAN.
Sydney Nocturnes.
FROM THE NORTH SHORE.
Dimly across the evening calms
Her faint, sweet face beseeches;
A white reproach of wistful arms
Over the bay she reaches.
Upon her gleaming bosom, wet
With tears and quivering,
In ropes of golden beauty set
Her vivid jewels swing.
Upon the pathway of the night
She, pausing often, paces ;
About her body waves gleam white
Like froth of filmy laces.
And to her pleasure hurrying,
Their torches holding high,
On molten waters smouldering
The ferry boats flame by.
IN HYDE PARK.
The white mist walks between the trees
In silver gown ;
Her mystic floating draperies
The branches drown.
And lurking there with eager leer
And wonder new,
The lamps inquisitively peer
Their fingers through.
The city weary of the day
Draws tired breath.
A ruddy glow above the grey,
Far down beneath
On Night’s smooth garment running o’er
In sullen flood,
The city, like a festering sore,
Oozes warm blood.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.

HISTORY or
YANKEE DOODLE
SEE
DIXSON’S TOBACCO HOARDINGS.
Iq America.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
General, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
through New Jersey on New York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Fayette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Fayette’s soldiers
were at a low ebb, a strange sight
aroused them. Marching with drum
and fife was an old man, Jeremiah Wyn-
ton, and his son and grandson. The
old father, with silvery flowing locks,
sturdily strode through the field. The
son with bandaged head fifing lustily,
the youth, catching the spirit of his
grandsire, rolled out with him the soul-
inspiring rattle of the drum. Father, son,
and grandson had accompanied the
troops as volunteers, and were ever in
the forefront of the fight. The fierce
light of battle was in their eyes, they
knew no fear, and many a wild cheer
arose, as on that fateful day Yankee
Doodle was heard as never was heard
before or since.
Ii) Australia.
ANKEE Doodle as a Trade Mark
■ is admitted by all to have no equal
on this side of the World, and, as
a Tobacco, Dixson and Sons determined
that it should enjoy an equal reputation,
consequently the resources of their great
establishment, and patient trials and ex-
periments (involving several visits to
America in search of suitable leaf with
which to produce the tobacco sought
after) resulted in a Dark and Bright
Tobacco, which to-day CHALLENGES
THE WHOLE WORLD IN COMPE-
TITION, and that it is appreciated may
be gathered from the fact that the sales
for New South Wales alone now reach
Tons per Dat.
The objection of many smokers to
Dark Tobacco is its strength. Dixson’s
have overcome this, and Dark Yankee
Doodle, while possessing a delightful
flavor, is not strong. Then again, Light
or Bright Tobacco, although mild, burns
the tongue. Bright Yankee Doodle is
equally mild, richer than other tobaccos
in aroma, and DOES NOT PARCH
THE TONGUE. Every smoker should
TRY FOR HIMSELF.'

SATURDAY, JANUARY 7, 1899.
The Federal Deadlock.
Sometime this or next month five Premiers
will probably meet, at the invitation of
George Reid, of New South Wales, to con-
sider amendments, and tinker with the
Federal Constitution Bill, and undo the
work which was done by the people’s elected
representatives and endorsed by the electors
of New South Wales, Victoria, Tasmania,
and South Austi'alia. And even now, at
the eleventh hour, the New South Wales
Premier has no new financial scheme to offer
so far as the country is aware, and has no
Parliamentary or other authority to offer
one. The financial clauses are, admittedly,
the biggest part of the whole Federal pro-
blem, and the making of a workable plan of
Federal finance has all along been the
great difficulty. The parochial-souled poli-
ticians of the Gon-fearing Ministry urged
New South Wales to reject the late Federal
Constitution on the ground mainly that the
financial provisions were unsatisfactory, and
it advanced many reasons—most of them
glaringly untrue—in proof thereof. And
now it is apparently quite unable to say
what is jvrong with these provisions—barring
the fact that they were devised by a Conven-
tion of which Edmund Barton was leader.
The Government of New South Wales appeals
to the other provinces to hold “further
inquiry into, and a more thorough considera-
tion of, the financial clauses.” It doesn’t
say what it wants done to them ; in fact, it
evidently doesn’t know what it wants, only
it wants it immediately, whatever it is. In
this matter Premier Reid approaches the
Conference as a weary, wandering bagman
without credentials of any kind. He has to
start by stating that New South Wales
objects to the money clauses in the present
Bill, which is a stupid and clumsy lie to
begin with, for New South Wales accepted
them by a considerable majority. Then he
has to state that he didn’t submit any new
financial scheme either to the Parliament or
the country, so that neither of these authori-
ties have demanded any new scheme in
particular. And as regards any one which
he may offer himself he can’t say whether
the country or the New South Wales Par-
liament will accept it, as they haven’t been
consulted; and in submitting it he may or
may not be fooling away the time of the
other Premier's—he doesn’t know. In his
position as a Premier who goes to a Confer-
ence to state that the greatest province of
Australasia wants something and doesn’t
know what—that it has wakened up in the
night and is crying for an object unspecified
—George Reid looks rather foolish.
* * * •
The old scheme—the one which the God-
fearing Ministry objects to—was (barring
certain small temporary concessions to West-
ralia) reasonably fair all round. The pro-
vinces were all to pay the same customs and
excise duties. They were to pay the same
amount per inhabitant towards the support
of the Federal Government. And each pro-
vince was to receive back as much of its own
customs and excise revenue as was left after
its share of the cost of the Federal Govern-
ment was paid. At the end of five years,
when experience may possibly have thrown
a lot of light on things that are now dark,
the financial scheme was to be reconsidered
by the people’s representatives, to see if the
machinery could be simplified in any way,
but the basis of it was that each province
was to get what it was entitled to down to
the last penny.
• » • •
There are difficulties in the way, of course
—there always are. New South Wales has
more than twice as much land-revenue as
Victoria, South Australia, Tasmania and
Westralia put together, therefore if they all
run the borrowing swindle in about the
same degree it can do with less customs
revenue per head of population than any of
them. Yet the necessity for making the
tariff uniform involves the further necessity
that the revenue per head shall be about the
same all round. In 1898 the five eastern
provinces kept just about solvent on paper
by raising customs and excise revenue as
follows :
per inhabitant. per inhabitant.
N.S.W. ... £l3 1 I Tasmania .. £2 311
S-A. ... 113 5 | Queensland ... 214 7
Victoria ... 115 11 |
Therefore the situation is this : If enough
customs and excise revenue is raised to keep
Queensland solvent the other four provinces
will have a lot more than they have now,
and are confronted by the horrifying fact
that they will be able to do with less borrow-
ing. If enough is raised to just keep
Tasmania solvent then New South Wales,
Victoria and S.A. will have a large sur-
plus, and Queensland will go broke. If
just enough is raised to keep Victoria sol-
vent, then S.A. and New South Wales will
have a surplus, while Tasmania and Queens-
land will be left in a state of bankruptcy. If
the Federal customs and excise revenue is
fixed at the figure which will keep South
Australia afloat and no more, then New
South Wales will have a surplus, while Vic-
toria, Tasmania, and Queensland will all be
bankrupt together. And if the Common-
wealth fixes its tariff at a rate which will
leave New South Wales exactly as it is, then
all the other four provinces will be stony-
broke. Westralia, which wants about £5 or
£6 per head of customs revenue, was covered
temporarily by a special arrangement, so it
is left out of the calculation.
•_ • *
Therefore, among the four provinces (New
South Wales, Victoria, Tasmania, and S.A.)
which accepted the Federal Constitution,
New South Wales is the only one which had
no serious risks to face under the Conven-
tion’s arrangement. When the Common-
wealth Parliament sets to work to build the
new tariff for all Australia, each province
will probably want to put the tariff high
enough to cover its own needs, and won’t
worry much about the needs of its neigh-
bors. If all the five provinces above-men-
tioned enter the Federation, then New South
Wales takes the risk of increased taxation
only. Queensland takes the risk of being
made insolvent through the establishment of
a tariff not large enough to cover its neces-
sities. South Australia, Victoria and Tas-
mania take two risks ; they are liable, on
one hand, to extra taxation if the tariff is
made high enough to keep Queensland sol-
vent ; and they are liable, on the other, to
be financially wrecked and left bankrupt if
it is made low enough to suit the ideas of
New South Wales. The latter province is
only in danger of having some extra money
on hand, so that it can build its roads and
bridges without further piling up its ghastly
heap of unproductive debt. All the others
take the chance of having their principal
financial prop knocked from under them.
And so far these provinces have cheerfully
accepted their chances as a fair price to pay
for Australian unity. New South Wales, the
only one of the crowd which is absolutely
secured against financial disaster, is the one
which has done all the lamentation up to
date.
* * * *
There are two visible ways out of the diffi-
culty. One is to hand the debts and rail-
ways over to the Commonwealth, and then
the provinces, being relieved of a great part
of their liabilities, won’t need any refund of
Customs revenue, and won’t need to trouble
about how much the tariff yields. That
would be the shortest and simplest way out,
but so far New South Wales flatly refuses to
consider any such idea. It wasn’t the only
objector, certainly, but it was the loudest
one. Another is that the wealthier pro-
vinces—New South Wales and Victoria—
shall agree to help their poorer neighbors.
So far, New South Wales isn’t taking any
of that either. What the Reid Government
has demanded up to date is practically that
the Federal tariff shall be fixed to yield just
enough to keep its province afloat, and that
the others shall get through any way they
can, or not get through at all, just as things
may happen. Of the three possible arrange-
ments the New South Wales delegates—
particularly those who were members of the
Ministry—declined to accede to two. They
accepted the third, and the people of New
South Wales accepted it also, but now the
Ministry goes back on it. And it offers no
fourth alternative.
• • * •
Australia is waiting for the production of
the new unauthorised financial scheme de-
vised by Reid and Sunday Brunker—
if they have any scheme, which ’ is doubtful.
At present New South Wales cuts a lament-
ably poor figure in the Federal business.
It was by the invitation of its Government
that the present movement started. Its
Government knew—if it was capable of
knowing anything at all—that there was a
financial difficulty in the road. The fact that
the Federal customs revenue must be high
enough to keep the poorer provinces afloat,
or else that the poorer provinces must simply
be thrown overboard, was visible all along
to the commonest intelligence—even the
intelligence of Brunker. The Convention ac-
cepted a scheme which was probably the best
that circumstances admitted of, and the New
South Wales Government delegates con-
curred in it. Then the majority of these
same Government delegates denounced it
over the length and breadth of the country
as an infamous fraud on the Cornstalk
Treasury. The people of New South Wales
endorsed the scheme at a popular election,
and allowed their servants in Parliament to
cancel their decision. The financial system
which was accepted contained certain un-
avoidable risks for all the provinces con-
cerned ; the other Governments took their
share of the risk as a matter of course ; the
New South Wales Ministry took its share
howling, and filled the air with a lying
lamentation that all the possible losses fell
on its State alone. The New South Wales
Parliament hasn’t offered to bear a penny of
the burdens of the other provinces, yet it
demands possession of the capital as a fair
equivalent for the sacrifices it isn’t going to
make. It has wept with a great dampness
about the fact that it hazards its fiscal sys-
tem by federating with the other provinces ;
the other provinces hazard their fiscal sys-
tems also, but none of them has shed tears
of woe over it, or demanded possession of
the capital as compensation. And now New
South Wales’s self-appointed Federal repre-
sentative goes to a conference to state that
his province refuses to accept the financial
scheme embodied in the last Bill (which is
glaringly false) and wants something instead
of it, only it doesn’t really know what it does
want.
The Late Demented.
The above term is used advisedly as regards
the defunct YVestralian boom. About its late-
ness there is no possible shadow of doubt. It
is probably the most completely dead thing to
be found between the Leeuwin, which looks out
to the west, and Thursday Island, that keeps
its eye on the leprous east. There may be a lot
of decay still to be attended to before the pro-
vince reaches bottom, but the boom couldn’t
be more completely deceased even if it tried.
And, as regards the other adjective, it requires
no explanation. The Westralian boom was, in
its way, the most remarkable that this country
has yet seen. In proportion to the size of the
community in which the disease broke out, it
was more violent than any of its predecessors,
with the doubtful exception of the early gold
outbreak in Victoria. It was the most sudden
of all the Australian booms. The other memor-
able bubbles, as a rule, grew slowly; the
first beginning and the last burst were so
wide apart that a large proportion of the
community wasn’t there at both events, and
didn’t enjoy the privilege of seeing a whole
boom and studying the natural history of the
animal in its entirety. The W.A. boom rose
almost in a day, like Jonah’s alleged gourd,
and fell almost as suddenly, and its life was so
short that almost all the notable pioneers of the
movement are still there to look (over the fence
of the Benevolent Asylum or otherwise) at its
ruins.
• * • .
Westralia, like every other province which
has possessed a boom, refused to the very lasi
to admit that it had such a thing. No matter
how many similar affairs have risen and glit-
tered and burst, the province which is having a
boom always denies that its boom is a boom at
all, and maintains that it is merely the natural
development of the country. In this respect
the province is merely like any other drunk
who hangs on to a lamp-post, and asserts his
perfect sobriety, and then falls down. Now
that the vast episode is over, it looks
quite probable that it would have been
a great deal better for WesLralia if it
had never had any boom at all. It is quite
probable that it would have been better for the
province if gold had never been found in it at
all, considering that the main result was to
fill the land with British shindykits and cover
it with British mortgages. Westralia has
gained greatly in population, certainly; that is
to say it has 17 tolerably miserable people where
it used to have four who were placidly comfort-
able. It owes a vast amount more money
than it did. It has found large gold deposits,
and it has emerged from its great dazzle to find
that they mostly belong to that übiquitous
foreign capitalist, J. Bull, and to people in the
other provinces. Before the boom, the gold—
though it was not then discovered—belonged to
the local sandgroper ; during the time of great
joy, it somehow mostly dropped through his
fingers. There was a great influx of foreign
capital, but it mostly went away again, and
the interest keeps going after it; and a lot of
land which was the sandgroper’s own property
before the golden days now belongs to foreign
syndicates. When everything is cleaned up
the sandgroper will probably find himself, on
the average, no richer in actual cash than he
was before—possibly a great deal poorer. Also
his great golden asset will be very largely
foreign property, and the great fortune he might
have made out of it some day, if he had found
it when he was older and had more sense, will
have mostly slipped away. Likewise, his liabili-
ties w'ill have enormously increased, and he will
have to pay much heavier taxes to meet theinter-
est on them ; and he will find himself shaken out
of the old placid rut, and his head will be very
sore and very much enlarged. The peculiarity
of every boom is that assets evaporate like a
snowball in Gehenna, and, in the end, out of
every thousand people, one man doesn’t seem
to be much richer, and 999 are a lot poorer.
• • » *
The Westralian Government is deserving of
no special blame for not profiting by the experi-
ence of the other provinces, for none of them
ever profited by the experience of any other
province either. But if it had possessed the
unusual wisdom to do so, it would have started
about ’92, when the boom began to be really
visible, by setting apart a day for humiliation
and prayer—prayer to be delivered from large
syndicates and from the influx of British capi-
tal. And it would not have depended on prayer
alone, but would have added works thereunto.
It was commonly regarded as an oppressive
Government—certainly it managed, in the
height of the riot, to raise a gross revenue equal
to £2O per inhabitant per annum, which was
three times the average of the other provinces.
Yet its preliminary mistake was that it wasn’t
nearly oppressive enough. It should have
screwed out a revenue of £3O per head,
while the chance was there, and built its own
public works solely out of revenue, or else let
many of the works wait. The new shindykits
and the new capital that flocked into Westralia
created a demand for new railways, and they
were built—with borrowed money. Now the
foreign capital has mostly evaporated and some
of the new people are leaving, and many are
stone-broke, and the struggle to pav the in-
terest bill is already something terrible. The
new railways were built in frantic haste with
loan moneys because British capital was coming
into the country, and it was feared that it
would stop coming if it wasn’t encouraged—
just as if foreign capital, unless its owners
come with it and settle in the country, isn’t a
disease, and a disastrous mortgage, and a thing
for whose scalp a liberal reward should he
offered; The Government piled up hideous
liabilities to keep the boom going—as if any
sane community can possibly want to keep the
boom going. The first object should have been
to get the boom down, and smash it out of all
human recognition, and bury it at the dead of
night where no one would ever find it again
* • * .
Next time there is a great gold discovery in
Australia—if there ever is another—the local
Government should immediately put ice on its
head in order to keep cool. Then it should
write out these seven propositions and paste
them on the decanter where they will be always
visible and handy : (1) There is no haste ; the
gold won t rise up and go away, neither will it
lose its value. (2) The sudden “ development
of the country ” only means that the new
wealth will be sooner exhausted. Also, it
means a great influx of population to dig it out
all at once, and when it begins to fail these
people will take a long time to find other
means of support, therefore the country will
have to build benevolent asylums to put a lot
of them into, and find victuals for them. (3)
No human being who isn’t resident in the
country should be allowed to hold any interest
in any mine under any pretext whatever. The
influx of absentee capital only means that
somebody brings £20,000. into the country in
the hope of taking £2,000,000 out. Also the
absentee capitalist generally waters his stock
to such an alarming degree that he has to screw
the last possible copper out of his mine to pay
dividends, and to do that he cuts down wages
and creates poverty. The poverty is often
the only thing the country gets out of the
deal. (4) The only goldfield where the absen-
tee capitalist should be treated otherwise
than as a public enemy is the one where there
is no gold. (5) To borrow money for public
works when there is a boom only means that
the boom will break some day and throw a
multitude of people out of employment, and the
borrowing will stop about the same time for
want of credit and throw another multitude out
of employment, and there will be two horrors
instead of one. If there is any time when bor-
rowing is justifiable it is when there is no boom.
(6) Rapid increase of population isn’t neces-
sarily a blessing, any more than rapid increase
of tumor. There are times when population is
a disease. (7) Rapid increase of commerce is
often a disease also. In Australia it frequently
means the increased importation of unpaid-for
material to build public works that will be a
dead loss, and the increased export of money to
pay the interest.
* • • •
Therefore, the moral of this tirade is as fol-
lows : Any sensible Government (there are no
sensible Governments, but that doesn’t make
any difference in the argument) will recognise
that a boom is a disaster and treat it as such.
It will also recognise that the influx of capital
is a curse unless the owners come to live
in the country ; the money of the absentee
capitalist is merely a debt owing by the country,
and no sane community whoops over an in-
crease in its debts. If the W.A. Government
had recognised these principles even when the
arrival of much new debt had already created
the Coolgardie boom, it would have been a lot
more oppressive in its taxation than it was, and
would have built its goldfields railways and
other works out of the proceeds, even if it
wrecked the absentee capitalist in the process.


If he was wrecked, W. A. would have made him
pay for aome valuable railways before he left,
and then it would have had the gold for its own
people. At present, Bull has most of the gold,
and W.A. has the debt and the prospective
railway deficit, which is how the boom policy
always pans out sooner or later. This complaint
ends abruptly right here.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Terrorised Bench.
Dear Bulletin, — The unpaid magistrates at
Carlton (Melb.) sit in fear and trembling when
a member of a local “ push ” (Carlton being the
“ pushiest” suburb thereabouts) is brought up
for punishment. Recent testimonials to the
Bench’s mortal funkiness are as follows. For
ringing up a harmless citizen in the middle of
the night, dragging him into the roadway by
his hair and fracturing his leg, the assailant (a
perfect stranger to the prosecutor) was fined
40s. ! For an unprovoked assault, including
breakage of the victim’s leg—a £5 fine. For
knocking a man down and splitting his head
with the sharp edge of an iron bar as he lay on
the ground, the prisoner’s doom was a JBIO fine,
payable within a month, on the understanding
that he would bolt from the province ere the
money fell due. For casually tiring a revolver-
bullet at a hotel-keeper and missing a fatal spot
by half an inch the youthful marksman
received a severe lecture and three days’ im-
prisonment, in default of £1 line, “ the police
being directed to return the revolver to the
prisoner on his completing his sentence ”111
Also, at the same court of justice one Ellis
was charged with stabbing his much-bat-
tered “ woman ” in two places, and the re-
ward of merit was a rather severe lecture and
the same old £1 fine. Popular ignorance
blames the “ beaks ” for these sinfully inade-
quate sentences, but the magistrates, if called
to account, probably pass the blame along to
the police-department, which permits pushes to
exist. The argument that the police cannot
purge Carlton of its ruffianism unless the magis-
trates supply the physic iB a bad argument.
Lawlessness of the wild assault-and-battery
type is bred at street oorners by permission
practically of the men-in-blue. Brutality
nourishes around districts where ill-conditioned
J IM 3 and Bills and Pats are allowed to ex-
change swoars on a large scale in the public
thoroughfare until they acquire iho confidence
of numbers, and don’t care a blanky for the
guardians of the peace, and form themselves
into a prudential intimidation society. If the
Carlton “ beaks ” don’t deal severely with the
local bashers, it is because they are afraid of
being severely dealt with themselves by the
pals of the earcused. Magistrates are mere
human beings and citizens, not specially chosen
for their moral or physical courage or fighting
ability. Their position in Carlton would ex-
pose them to danger, as things are at present,
if they did their duty by it, so they will con-
tinue to “ play for safety,” The Bulletin sup-
poses, until they feel secure of ample police
protection from larrikin vengeance.—E.
[Tub Bulletin publishes this apology mainly for the
purpose of expressing its utter disgust thereat. In the
first place the law doesn’t compel a J.P. to sit on the
Bench. If he is afraid to do justice there he can stay
away or resign, and let the (fovernment send along a
stipendiary magistrate who doesn't live in the district lo
deal it out to the brutal push. The Carlton J'sP. don't
sit on the Bench and act as the practical accomplices of
all the local ruffians because they are terrorised into it,
but because they want to do it—because they apparently
rather like the local brutal assault man. Secondly, they
don’t pass light sentences because Carlton is the pushiest
suburb around Melbourne. Carlton became the pushiest
suburb because the local Bench advertised it as the place
where harmless people could be crippled with impunity.
The push came there because it was practically invited
there by the Bench. Thirdly, the police can’t break up
pushes by themselves. All they are allowed to do is to
hale the push before the local beaks, and if the beaks
won’t do their duty then the police are helpless. In every
one of the cases above-quoted the police did their duty
manfully—and certain beaks insisted on coming upon
the Bench of their own free will for the purpose of nulli-
fying their efforts. If it is claimed that they came there
to acquit the push because they were afraid to stay away,
then the same logic would excuse them if they helped the
push to stoush the police with broken bottles—they were
afraid of what might happen to them if they didn’t help
to stoush the police.)
The Anti-Billious Bogies.
It is curious to look back at this late hour on
the war-cries of the N.S. VV. anti-Billious party
of eight months ago and consider how these
serviceable whoops have mostly dropped out of
sight and remembrance. In those days Pump-
kin Smith fell on a terrifying hollow turnip
with a lighted candle therein, to the effect that
under the Convention Bill N.S. W. would have
to help pay Victoria’s bounties. The Bill said
nothing about that, and if it had been true it
followed naturally that Victoria must help to
pay N.S. W.’s bounties —the ones that are
granted in aid of the mining industry; still it
was a very alarming turnip at the moment and
Sydney Telegraph played it for all it was
worth ; and it doubtless helped a little to raise
the 66,000 anti-Billious minority of 3rd June.
But since then neither Parliament nor the Reid
Ministry, nor the Telegraph, nor Pumpkin
.Smith has asked that this dismal ghost shall be
taken out the Bill—for the sufficient reason
that they know it was never there. The Tkle-
GRAPHaIso complained (leadingarticle, 26th May,
’9B) that the Convention Bill would be practically
ruinous to the Newcastle coal industry (pre-
sumably because it would compel Tasmania
and Queensland to admit N.S.\V. coal free,
where it is now shut out by heavy duties), and
it pointed out that Federation would mean the
destruction of N.S.W. Freetrade. There is
nothing in the proposed amendments to make
Freetrade any safer than it was before, or to
help the Newcastle coal industry; and both
these bogies appear to have perished miserably
by the wayside. The mongrel little Star com-
plained among other things in its issue of 2nd
June that—
All the burden of taxation will ultimately be thrown on
the people of this colony (N.S.W.).
The electors of N.S. W. part with the power, as a colony,
of raising any revenue by either customs or excise.
All bounties imposed before June 30 have to be paid by
the Commonwealth.
All these alleged dangers are left by the
N.S.W. Parliament’s proposed amendments
exactly where they were, and the Star is a
dumb dog, price Id.. and has never once appealed
to the Legislature to demand that the awful
things it said were in the Bill should be sum-
marily thrown out. Then Attorney-General
Jack Want called on the people to reject the
Bill because an alleged Referendum had been
“ kicked out ”of it; yet when the amendments
came before the N.S.W. Council he assured
that august but stupid body that he didn’t want
any Referendum, and the Council fired it out
with enthusiasm. Heydon, M. L.C., shouting
promiscuously at Glen Innes, told the public
that what was wanted was that each province
should collect its own customs revenue, and
that the handing over of the customs to the
Federal authority meant unspeakable disaster,
yet Heydon didn’t believe sufficiently in his
own spectre to even propose his own great
remedy when the subject came up in the House
where he and the other dull-witted Tories do
congregate. The Federal amendments sub-
mitted by N.S.W. Parliament hardly touch
any of the grievances alleged by the old anti-Billi-
ous gang, and not one of the anti-Billites has
raised a protest against the idea of leaving all
these alleged grievances unredressed. Onetheory
is that Heydon and Want and Pumpkin Smith
and their press organs knew all along that these
dreadful things were never in the Draft Con-
stitution at all, which would quite explain why
they don’t demand their removal. The other
is that they really think they are in the Con-
stitution, and want them to remain there —so
that they can use them as an excuse for
denouncing the next Federal scheme as they
did the last one.

Trial by Justices.
“ Ginghie Jack ” to The Bulletin
Some little time ago I wrote you re the practice pre-
vailing of police magistrates sitting to try cases with a
charge-sheet in front of them, to which is pinned a list of
previous convictions against accused. As constant
dropping may wear away a stone, so constant reference
to this, and many other police-court injustices, may do
some good.
Only a few days ago I saw a man brought up for larceny,
and the evidence was slight—so slight that no one but a
“ police ’’-magistrate would have listened to it for a
moment; but the P.M. read out the previous convictions,
and said : “ I am satisfied you committed the theft, and
you are the kind of man the publio is to be protected
against” —but it was the previous record that convioted
him, and not the police evidence.
What Judge would allow a list of all previous convic-
tions to bo placed before the jury, trying a man for his
liberty ? Not one ; then surely a police magistrate de-
means himself and lowers the supposed useful and high
ofHoe he holds by descending to what would never be
allowed in any Court of Jastir,e— for a country police-court
is not a Court of Justice for accused persons, but a court
for the upholding and protection of policemen.
And, by the way, it would be better if P.M.’s were filled
with the same spirit which prompted Judge Gibson a few
months ago, when summing up on a charge of inflicting
actual bodily harm, to say words to this effect : “ You
have always to be very careful where the only evidence
against an accused person consists of police evidence.
The police are no doubt a good body of men, but it is to
their interest to get a conviction, and where their evi-
dence is uncorroborated you must always look at it with
a certain amount Of doubt."
I wish, too, to comment strongly on the practice pre-
vailing pretty generally in the country by which very
many police-cases are practically prejudged.
The sergeant or other officer of police almost always
explains the whole case to the P.M. beforehand—no doubt
on the principle that the man who, before the fight be-
gins, gets a good, unexpected hit home on the back of
the other man’s head usually wins the battle. I know
this is done because I have been prosecuting at times,
and I have been behind the scenes a little bit.
One could write all day about how police cases are
heard beforehand, but one instance will suffice to show
the sort of decisions that are given.
Way back at Boggibilla—a town on the Q. border—the
local cricket-club indulges in a game of cricket on Sun-
days, and, a few weeks ago, all the players were
summoned for obstructing the thoroughfare —the obstruc-
tion consisting of now and then hitting the cricket-ball
out into the road. The traffic along that road is so con-
gested that if more than one bullook-waggon in a week
goes along it the populace turns out en masse and go on
the scoot because Boggabilla is becoming such a busy city,
and “ things is looking up.”
When the case came on it was brought out in evidence
that the P.M. had sent for the officer in charge of police,
and. had instructed him to have the summonses issued,
and had informed him that he would sit on the case,
which he promptly proceeded to do.
And, mark the absurdity of this “country justice’s
justice. ” The ease was for obstructing the thoroughfare,
yet the P.M. told the defendants he had no objection to
them playing cricket in this place, provided they did not
play on Sunday, the Lord's day—here he got excited and
nearly gave out the wrong hymn—and considering that
the sportive once-a-week waggon does not travel on Sun-
days, one would think that is the very day they would
not obstruct much if they played.

A Landlord on Poreig-n Capital.
Premier Turner’s proposal that as much as
possible of the new Victorian borrowing should
be done locally caused the ancient Grimwade,
M.L.C., to boil over as one personally wronged,
and make a pathetic exhibition of himself
among the 48 Rich Landlords. The principal
boiling-over of old Grimwade was as follows :
The Treasurer talked about borrowing money here, but
to his (Mr. Grimwadk’s) mind, that was a most suicidal
policy. In fact, it was only like taking our own money
out of one pocket and putting it in another.
Fancy committing suicide by taking our own
money out of one pocket and putting it in an-
other ! The other alternative, of course—the
one Grimwade believes in—is to take someone
else’s money out of one pocket and put it in
another, and that isn’t suicidal, but healthy.
Grimwade, looked at in the light of hard finan-
cial experience, is truly a great ass. He holds
that it is suicidal not to borrow from some
other country—that it is fatal not to be in
debt. Great Britain, as a case in point, does
just what Turner advocates, and what Grim-
wade is all broken up about—it effects its
borrowing locally ; and this suicidal policy
seems to agree with it. France and the United
States are two other suicides of the same order.
And their pitiable condition, as compared with
the gloriously-prosperous state of, say, Turkey,
Greece, Italy, Spain, Honduras, Guatemala,
Peru, Costa Rica, Brazil, Argentina, Ecuador,
Nicaragua, and other countries that have done
their borrowings abroad as Grimwade advo-
cates, is calculated to make one get up and ask
that amazing old man if he is aware that
matches don’t grow on icebergs. The Bulle-
tin respectfully puts this conundrum to Grim-
wade : He says that it is suicidal for the Vic-
torian State to borrow what money it wants
from its own people. Then if some day the
world becomes all one State it will presumably
be suicidal for that State to borrow from its
own people, and if it can’t float a loan in
Jupiter the Lord only knows what is to
become of it. And Grimwade’s theory that
foreign borrowing is healthy enough, though
the local article is disastrous, leads to this
beautiful cul de sac : Supposing New South
Wales and Victoria each borrowed £1,000,000
from its own people that would be h—l. But
if N.S.W. borrowed its million in Melbourne,
and Victoria floated its loan in Sydney, that
would be foreign borrowing, and would be just
what Grimwade strongly advocates. And
then, if these two provinces decided that they
were tired of the foolishness of running two
separate Governments for a mere handful of
people, and agreed to become one State, then
these foreign borrowings would thereby be-
come local borrowings, and the situation would
be too awful for anything. Grimwade reckons
that if a Victorian is found owning the State
securities of his own country it is a national
disaster. Yet, if he is found owning bank-
shares, or land, or houses, or mortgages, or
clothes, or a good hat in his own country it
isn’t. And yet Australia has got the foreign-
capital disease so very badly that it is full of
people just like Grimwade.

A Howl Upon Art. /
The howler signs himself “Jack Shay,” and
the howl is as follows :
The present N.S.W. Govt, is no doubt trying to do jus-
tice to Art by dividing the subsidy between the two rival
societies. But is all art contained between the four
oorners of a canvas and frame? After looking at Sid.
Long's latest (bought for N.S.W. National Gallery), I
think not. An Australian artist’s attempt to embody a
Greek myth : Foreground, a bit of one of the ponds in
the Botanical Gardens ; characters, impossible satyrs and
four life-studies of a painfully tall and thin young lady in
different poses ; background, a rear view of some panto-
mime scenery ; distance, a sky intlumed with bush lire ;
Art, bosh 1 Ashton, Junior’s, imitation of Strbbton is as
bad, and Gaklick’b picture is only commenced. Yet each
of the rival clans is producing little better year after year,
and this year they quarrel over the loot. To them each and
severally, I would mention Walter Crane, my ideal of an
artist. His is the art that ought to be subsidised.
Stained glass, pottery, metalwork, stucco, interior deco-
rations, fabrics—every one of these common-place things
have glowed under his touch. He is a designer
and a oolorist—a draughtsman of the highest class—a
man who has an art-career extending from the Royal
Academy to the Manchester ootton-looms. And 1-hold
that this is the art of the moderns the beautification and
idealisation of everything. Now, how many of our local
artists are good draughtsmen or colorists? Their work is
full of bad drawing and crude coloring, and the most of it
is by whiskered men, who ought to be able to keep their
tin-pot trade-unions together without Government aid.
Yet one of these “Art” Unions had for a climax
a smoke-conoert, at which a comedian, after ask-
ing to make sure no ladies were about, mounted
the platform and told smoking-room stories. And
a Government subsidy helps them to do it. I pro-
pose that the Government altogether withdraw its sup-
port from both art organisations. Also, the proportion
of the School of Arts subsidy which is devoted to art
teaching. Likewise that it abolishes the make-believe
art classes at the Technical College, and with the money
thus saved let it found an Australian School of Design.
A school where young people will be taught all the rudi-
ments of pictorial and plastic art. Where the grammar
of form and line and color will be the first lessons. Where
the artist will be taught first principles and not to copy
particular instances. With such a school as a beginning
we may hope to produce more than a few artists in the
broad sense of the word. Men who with open eyes will
see the beauties of Australia, and put them not only into
pictures, butintoarchitecture in all its numerous branches,
and make Australian Art understood by the Australian
multitude.

“ Old Possum ” sends along this Xmas lament:
Oh, what have you and George been doing? And all
in two years. The pity of it I I have come back to see
my old love, so fresh and bright and expectant, but she
is faded, and her hat is not plumb, and her eye is bleary,
and there is grass in her hair. Oh, what have you and
Gp.orqb been doing? Oh, my Sydn<se, what have they
been and gone and done to you ? The man at Redfern
showed me his large green card. His boots were polished
where there was no sock, and his sock was polished where
there was no boot, and he said he could show me the best
lodgings in town—near the man with his hand up
(Capt. Cook). He put his head sideways, and looked
as if he didn’t expect me to believe him. I didn’t
believe him, but I went. And they gave me
tea in a broken cup, butter in a broken dish,
and unnecessary information in broken English
(there is something more than coincidence in this). Then
I went out and the people in George-street wanted to give
me boots and photographs because they couldn’t sell
them. They told me so. What a kind, pathetic, gander-
ful way they come up to you 1 I went to visit my
old friend, Captain Cook. He is the only excep-
tion he is just the same. The people after
bread still pass him by—the fat people and the thin
people. And my shabby, clean, careworn, jaunty old
friends, I see them all again ; but their faces are longer
and chins stronger from the harder battle. God bless
them 1 I glory in them. The young husband is still
lugging the first-born in the wake of the unheeding ’bus
and a length in the lead of the wife. But he doesn’t get
up now’—and the fare is only twopence. All men are
fools enough to lug the first-born. Then there is
the small, thin man with the claw-hammer coat and
hard hat who carries the large thin parcel. He is there
yet, but the dent in his hat has now grown natural and
refuses to stop out, and he is as green as a grasshopper.
Poor wretch ! I have often wondered what he had in the
parcel. And the giddy girls 1 There are twice as many
of them now. They have fought and gone under fighting,
and they are not ashamed. All their hoys have gone
away to look for work, and they are pining and lonely.
They all wanted me to go home with them and spend the
Xmas and tell them about the paddymelon. They make
me laugh and curse in turns. God bless them, too ! And
the waxworks and circular ’ammer and the rest of you,
I can’t see you now, I can't. I am in no mood for you.
Oh, my love, my life, my pretty, petulant, jolly-and-fat
Sydn6e, what have they gone and done to you? Oh,
Bulletin, what have you and Gborgk been doing ? Ex-
plain or come outside 1

The agreement, or one very like it, of which
The Bulletin spoke the other day, when it
commented on the relations between the Afghan
carrying co’s and their employ6s in Far-Western
N.S.W., has been produced in a Bourke camp.
And it is rather worse than this paper thought
it was. The men bind themselves to work for
the co. for six years for £24 a-year (the worst
of it is that they bind themselves to work in
Australia) ; from that, £ll 17s. lid. is to be
deducted for passage-money; and, until the
expiration of their term, they are entitled to
draw £6 a-year ! During their six years, they
are thus actually entitled to clothe themselves
in fine raiment and make hogs of themselves on
2s. 3d. a-week. The Bouike Bench came to
the conclusion that the agreement was one of
the most disgraceful documents ever brought
into a court of justice, and said so ; where-
upon the co’s solicitor (who didn’t seem to
be working on quite the same terms) looked
painfully injured, and begged the Court to tell
him what it meant by saying such a thing.
The only thing of much consequence, however,
that happened afterwards was that this bleed-
ing country had the honor of feeding and hous-
ing for seven days the particular Said Mahomet,
or something, who broke the agreement that
time ; and it may look forward to some more of
the same pleasure, inasmuch as, when Said was
told that he would be brought up again if he
defaulted on his release, he cheerfully answered
“All right.” Most of the Mahomets, in fact,
seem perfectly willing to spend the whole six
years that way.

The kind of justice administered by the ama-
teur J.P. smells lamentably all over Australia.
It sfnelt so badly in Sydney that the J.P. was
long since removed from the city bench, and
now he merely dispenses his moral fragrance in
the country, where it attracts less notice
because it is far from large centres of popula-
tion. In Melbourne the J.P. is still allowed to
deliver his awful decisions in the city courts,
and lately the Aok was moved to publish a
batch of these from Carlton Court, and to show
how the man who stole a few' shillings got three
months, and the man who called at the house
of a perfect stranger and crippled him for life
under circumstances of awful brutality was
fined a few shillings. Apropos of which a local
solicitor upended himself in the Carlton Court
last week :
Mr. W. H. Leonard, solicitor, on behalf of the legal pro-
fession, wished the presiding magistrates the compliments
of the season. Mr. Leonard, in the course of his remarks,
referred to “ the excellent manner in which the Oarlton
magistrates discharged their official duties during the
past year, their decisions having been such that no fault
could possibly be found with them.” Ilis attention had
been drawn to comments in the Age of that day, which
reflected on certain decisions of the magistrates, but he
hoped those gentlemen would treat the reflections with
the contempt they deserved. He could not see what good
the comments in question would do. In any case, if the
magistrates went wrong it was not the duty of the Age to
set them right. They were responsible to the Minister of
Justice, and not to the Aon or the public. IVhat had the
public to do with them? The object of the Age in pub-
lishing these articles was, apparently, to abolish honorary
justices altogether, and have the work done by stipendiary
magistrates. If that arrangement ever came to pass, then
God help them all. For his own part, he would far rather
have a case heard by honorary justices, who did a great
deal of work for nothing.
The solicitor who wants to know what the
public has to do with the way justice is ad-
ministered looks a pretty blatant ass anyhow'.
But probably he isn’t. For the solicitor who
geqtly bites the ear of the J.P. at suitable
times and places has great luck when he turns
up to defend the brutal hoodlum ; and he
needn’t be very particular how the ear-biting
is done, for the average J.P. is a clumsy person
who doesn’t suspect that any one is taking an
outrageous rise out of him. The thick com-
placency of a bench that sat and joyfully
flapped its ears while it was being assured that
the miserable public should take any kind of
justice it can get and be thankful, is in jf«elf a
strong argument against J.P.-ism.
The present N.S.W. Ministry is a Government
of fits and starts. For instance, it has just
issued orders—which are, apparently, quite
illegal—that the leaky ship Republic is not to
go out of Sydney Harbor without adequate re-
pair. The customs authorities have been
ordered not to give the Republic her papers ;
tug-owners and pilots have been commanded
not to take her out; and at time of writing
she is also in the direct custody of the officers of
the law. In making all this howl about one leaky
ship, the Reid Govt, plainly admits that there
is need of some power which can seize
and detain any unseaworthy man-trap that
may come along. Yet in the Naviga-
tion Bill which it presented to Parliament
a week or two ago, no provision at all
was made for the detention of unseaworthy
vessels. And the Republic was lying in Sydney
harbor then, just as it is now. The
Reid Government is always the same—a luke-
warm thing of shirk and half-measures and
half-remedies and unfinished purposes and odds
and ends and apologies, and all manner of
muddlement.

“ Old Jack ” to The Bulletin :
Instructive to note bow the Broad Arrow Government
of the Land of Sand and Sorrow is doing its level best
to baulk the Penal Commission recently appointed. The
composition of the Commission was effected by the Govt,
in such a manner that in six cases out of ten the meet-
ings lapse for want of a quorum. If it were not for the
persistence of one or two earnest men the whole thing
would end in a fizzle. As it is, such is the obstinate ill-
will of authority to any inquisitorial proceedings, and
such the unscrupulous methods adopted to thwart
inquiry, that it is little likely any great reform will be
immediately accomplished. Means have been taken to
get rid of compromising evidence. Many notorious grum-
blers in Fremantle gaol have been released and sent from
the province. Others have been bribed by easement of
their sentences. Cameron and Beck,’ under sen-
tence of twelve months in irons, have been relieved
of their fetters, and their special - rascality livery of
black and yellow for no apparent better reason
than the fact of the sitting of the Penal Commission.
Further, all the prisoners who have registered their
names as desirous of giving evidence before the Commis-
sion have been required to make a statement of the
nature of that evidence to the gaol officials. This has
been resented. The matter came, by some fortuitous
chance, to the ears of the Commission, and the order was
revoked. But when it is remembered that these things
have been done by order of the Executive, it is easy to
understand the difficulties that lie in the way of any re-
form, so long as the Forrest brood rules the roost. And
this dull, Chinkie-like opposition to sweeping away old-
time abuses characterises the whole of the policy of
Westralia’s leg-iron Government. The Forrest crowd
cannot realise that the brute force authority of the penal
period can no longer be applied to the altered conditions
of Westralia. The clank of the fetters, the swish of the
cat, still linger in their dreams.
The erstwhile fairy godfather of N.S.W.
Free trade party, the gilded Sydney Burdekin,ex-
M.L. A., ex-alderman, and out of luck generally
for .some time back, bobs up as another possible
for N.S.W. Agent-Generalship.

“Prof. Hobday, of the Royal Veterinary
College,” writes The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent, “is quite a crank on animal cripples.
Fitting false legs and eyes to cats and dogs and
legs to crippled cows is with him an everyday per-
formance. His greatest recent feat was providing a
toy terrier with false teeth. Disease had wrecked
the snapper’s stumps. Chloroform reposed him
while the moulds were taken, and a dentist did
the rest, and now the yelper pursues the public
with shop teeth more viciously than ever. The
false legs run from 2s. 6d. to 25 guineas—a leather
socket filled with horsehair or a silver and vul-
canite, steel-springed leg or paw with joints.”

The above remarks have no direct bearing
on the “ Forty Thieves ” as now played at Her
Majesty’s, Sydney, but this paper thought it ad-
visable to get them off its chest lest it might begin
to suffer from suppressed poetry. The “ Forty ”
continue to be as gorgeous a spectacle as they were
last week, and Gulliver, and Edward the donkey,
grow even more wildly humorous as time goes on.
The writer isn’t prepared to swear to any notable
change in any of the other performers, but the
continued improvement of Gulliver and the don-
key it does swear to. Also, the beautiful ballet of
the poppies and the gray field-mice looks prettier
every time one meets it ; and the book isn’t grow-
ing any more flat than it was, for there is very
little room for increased flatness ; and the spec-
tacle in general is gorgeous beyond description.
** # *
London still Sleeps publicly at Sydney
Royal, and the takings from the people who come
to see him in bed are very remunerative. The
Sleeping of London only professes to be a respec-
table, glittersome melodrama, put on regardless of
cost; but while it is all there as a spectacle, it is
also quite as much there in a literary sense as can
be expected. Miss Eugenie Duggan, the beautiful
slack-wire orphan in the circus, comes in for an
enormous heap of wealth and goes to live in an
anoestral ’all, and her wioked cousin, the “cap-
ting,” who expected to inherit the wealth himself,
makes tremendous efforts to marry her. And,
like most captings in melodrama, he tries to do it
by using language and dragging the lady around,
lie bursts into her bedroom one night—the enor-
mously rich heiress has a plain iron bedstead and
one hard chair in a top room—and announces his
intention of staying in the chair all night, and
blasting the lady’s reputation by being seen
r g out in the morning ; but changing his mind
sets fire to the house and leaves. Also,
he imprisons the heiress in a mansion where the
gilded Flossie has her lair, and otherwise strives
to win her affections or to bump her head against
the wall, or something. As London turns over
laboriously in its sleep Miss Amy Gourlay, who
wasn’t much noticed at first, begins to stand out
conspicuously as a very bright little lady indeed.
The Bulletin didn’t observe her to any great ex-
tent on the opening night when London had just
blown out the candle and dropped off, but now she
strikes it as a very clever and winsome little
actress who does some of the best work in the
show. She is a misguided girl seeking with a red
hat for a johnny to devour, and it is pleasing to
note that she finds her johnny. William Gourlay,
disguised in woman’s clothes, supplies the wildest
joke of the show—when he weeps on the shoulder
of the sympathetic policeman and says he was a
good girl once. The idea of William Gourlay
being a good girl, even so seldom as once, has a
lot of points about it. And the only serious ob-
jection to pretty Eugenie Duggan is where that
immensely rich heiress, in her frilled nightdress,
is just about to turn into her plain little iron
bed in the small top room with the one hard
wooden chair, and Amy Gourlay, having left her
half-eaten johnny to his fate, calls round on an
errand of mercy—and Miss Duggan calls her
“ see-ee-eester. ” It is a very pathetic scene, all the
same, and Miss Gourlay makes an effect in it that
causes London to partly wake up and fall ouc of
bed.
* * * *
“ Robbery Under Arms ” still robs as
usual at Sydney Criterion, where it is now in its
eighth week, or somewhere thereabouts.
* * * *
“Carmen,” at Sydney Lyceum, concern-
ing the stragglesomeness of which this organ spoke
last week with well-deserved severity, pounded
itself into some kind of shape after a day or two,
and gave a very much improved version of Bizet’s
opera. The chorus began to explode all together
instead of firing separately ; and Farley recaptured
the note that had gone astray; and Leumane
acquired more confidence ; and Miss Lilian Tree
didn’t look quite so large when you got used
to her. Also, some vestiges of a Spanish
atmosphere gathered round the play; it began to
feel as if it was really located in that sunny land of
oheap guitars, where, according to operatic tradi-
tion, all the people live by singing to each other
for a few coppers. But the uniforms of the alleged
Spanish infantry destroyed the illusion again ; no
Spanish soldier ever went forth to battle in togs
even distantly resembling these. By the time
this issue sees the light, however, “Maritana”
will probably be on, so it isn’t worth while to
argue the point further.
* * m *
The Jubilee Singers are settling down for
a long stay at Sydney Palace, and this paper still
drops in there occasionally when it wants some-
thing cool dropped on its fevered frow. There is
a soothing cadence about the melodies of this dark
company that suggests the rippling of many waters,
or a gentle breeze in the forest on a hot day, or
a policeman walking stealthily over dead, brown
leaves, or anything else that has a restful, whisper-
ing note in it. The co.’s conjuror and sleight-of-
hand person is very clever in his art, but after all
ho isn’t the star. It is the quaint darkey
music of old Kentucky and other alleged places
that makes the attraction —the kind of music
which hasn’t too much bang and clatter in it, and
which doesn’t disturb a person too suddenly in his
sleep.
* # * *
Peggy Pryde, Ajax, the pretty and grace-
ful Cora Caselli, and a few other people keep up a
cheerful bustle at the Tivoli, where the Xmas god
has howled even more joyfully than usual. Also,
a number of brief shows have cropped up like
Jonah’s gourd during the holidays, and have
mostly passed away again like the same fleeting
herb which was ringbarlced by the selector out-
side Nineveh.
* * * *
The “ Strategist,” a rather over-rated
comedy of the cheerful rough - and - tumble
variety, is on this week at Sydney Alhambra.
Frank Hawthorne and Carden Wilson are the
best spots in a reasonably strong cast, and the
show is mounted in a fashion that the mouldered
Alhambra hasn’t been used to for many years.
* * * *
Maggie Stirling is to take a trip ’Ome
shortly, and her testimonial concert, at Melb.
Town Hall, will be the biggest thing in musical-
public demonstrations that has happened these
many years. Maggie Stirling’s platform appear-
ance makes her popular before she opens her mouth;
she sings easy, catchy ballads with one of the best
—if not actually the finest—mezzo-soprano voices
in the world, and she is domestically interesting
as the wife of a consumptive husband,
and the supporter of a young family. Also,
the vice-regal Brasseys have shown their
good taste by “ taking her up ” consider-
ably, and the heart of the great aggregate local
Mac warms to the tartan pattern of her name,
whilst Australian natives in general claim her as a
Bister. The idea that Maggie Stirling is going to
Europe with a particular view to taking lessons in
singing doesn’t seem very reasonable, but she will
benefit by hearing the more educated warblers
render the newest songs, and come back to Aus-
tralia with a certain iclat. Anyhow, the amiable
lady will deserve her enthusiastic send-off.
** * *
A plague of hot weather ravaged Mel-
bourne during Xmas week and, with the exception
of one packed house at the Royal (on Boxing
Night), business everywhere fell more or less below
holiday averages all the time. It was open to any
manager to advertise free distributions of cooling
drinks between the acts, and make a handsome
profit on the transaction, but the theatrical mind
seldom rises to meet a great emergency. The
Princess’s certainly put in a special plea
for patronage on accou.t of a sliding
roof, whereas the Circus didn’t even allude
to the tent’s open, airy advantages, and the
Alexandra made no claim to be the least oppres-
sively crowded place of entertainment in Mel-
bourne, although the fact was undeniable. Judging
from the Haleck’s consistent emptiness last week,
The Bulletin fears that the doom of “ Jack and
the Beanstalk ”is pretty nearly sealed. The poor
little attempt at a pantomime doesn’t seem to
improve with practice at any point, and the Giant
has developed into the saddest fiction that ever
stamped on stilts.
** * *
“ Madame Sans-Gene,” at the Bijou, is a
good show which gets very little encouragement
thusfar. The title of the play has no meaning for
95 per cent, of the local population, it appears.
They would be interested in the story of the
dauntless washerwoman if they would come along
to sample it, but they mostly avoid the Bijou and
ask no questions. All the same, Brough cannot
do better than persevere with “ Sans-Gene ” for
another fortnight, on the chance that the warm
approval of small audiences will lead to large
business results. Mrs. Brough in the name-part
is letting herself go more boldly and naturally than
on the first night, and her tongue onslaught upon
Napoleon’s two sisters is a capital burst of indig-
nation. Brough, too, has got a firmer grip of the
imperial Nap. character, which is here displayed in
unneroic light, and needs very careful treatment.
Joseph Carne comes out strong as Lefebvre,
especially in the prologue, but W. T. Lovell’s per-
formance of the Comte de Neipperg is no brighter
than his previous work in other young men parts.
"#* * *
The advt. announces that “The Geisha ”
has come to stay at the Princess’s. The Melb.
press is giving unlimited marmalade to the
Firm’s latest production, and it looks likely to
remain for another month at least.
** * «
“ New Babylon ” bore up manfully against
the weather last week, and will, no doubt, wave its
tangled tale at the Royal as long as Bland Holt
can make a profit on the job. The shipwreck
which occurs at 8.20 p.m., or thereabouts, is calcu-
lated to run for a month, even if there were nothing
to speak of in the subsequent proceedings. “New
Babylon,” however, offers a large assortment
of things worth honorable mention, and The Bul-
letin finds nothing to grumble at except the cattle-
show scene. A pair of piebald ponies are offered
for sale at the Islington cattle-show (instead of at
Tattersall’s, as in days of yore) to the sore distress
of every Londoner in the audience. Worse than
that, somebody’s prize bull, value 700 guineas, is
paraded behind the footlights, and, although no
right-thinking person can possibly take exception
to a beast of the field in its proper place, a good
many persons object to this particular beast in its
dramatic capacity.
** * *
At the Opera House old friend Horace
Wheatley is singing and dancing on two legs with
reasonable success, and Jimmy Shields is doing
likewise on one leg, to the intense satisfaction of
large audiences. Shields on his one leg is really a
stronger favorite than the vaunted Sampson, who
will lift the long, long dumb-bell for the last time
this week.
