Touching the rather gigantic success of
Fitz Gerald Bros.’ Circus in general, and the
growing popularity of elephant Lizzie in particu-
lar, the writer would like to make a few remarks,
but a sudden attack of Xmas spasms compels him
to hold the matter over for another week.
There were Forty Thieves in Bagdad in a bygone
centuree,
Born of poor but honest parents, as might chance
to you or me.
They never paid their ’bus-fares, though the notice
on the box
Said the tuppence should be dropped there. They
were prone to aiming rocks
At the old Arabian bobby, when his vast Arabian
feet
Woke the echoes in the evening down the camel-
haunted street.
And they fought with vim at Chowder when they
got upon the spree—
Chowder by the Shatt-el-Arab, in a bygone centu-
ree.
They got run-in at Bagdad, as any cove be;
They got out on bail in Bagdad, mouldering by
its rivers three;
And when they couldn’t pay the fine, they took it
out instead—
The usual term was seven days, and they “ did it
on their ’ed.”
At least, they meant to do it, but in these strange
times the push
Wore flowing skirts, and when the cold north wind
began to “ whoosh ”
And their brown legs stood up in the air, a naked
sight to see,
They’d have all caught cold in Bagdad in that
far-off centuree.
They played cricket in old Bagdad, where the
Calipns used to be,
With eleven from Damascus ; and they stoushed
the referee ;
And though the wicket’s vanished, and the ball is
long since bust,
And the pitch has long since crumbled, and the
players all are dust,
A ghostly echo lingers where the Forty used to
roar,
And on moonlit nights their ghostly forms make
runs upon the shore.
And scare the jackal in the dust where the grand-
stand used to be,
By the glassy Shatt-el-Arab in the seventh cen-
turee.
They were mostly hanged V'i Bagdad ; they were
hanged upon a tree
By the river Shatt-el-Arab, as might chance to
you or ine.
They were tried by Abdul Windeyer, and promptly
sentenced,
And this time, sad to say, they couldn’t do it “on
their ’ed.”
And there was peace in Bagdad when the Forty
were no more
And the publican no longer heard them pounding
on the door.
When they had a thirst at midnight and were
howling wild and free,
And wanted beer in Bacdad—Bagdad in El
JezirA
And on the 'beaoh at Chowder bottles moulder
silently,
Chowder by the Shatt-el-Arab flowing swiftly to
the sea.
For the push that used to heave them, they are
numbered with the dead,
And the ancient town of Bagdad is no longer
painted red,
And no man chews another’s ear within its silent
walls,
And sandalled feet go silently by the traders’
lonely stalls,
Since the push that woke up Bagdad was scragged
upon a tree
In the city of the Caliphs in the seventh centuree.
The Gatton Murder.
A Bulletin correspondent vents some theories
whioh, as they are interestingly written, we print
even at the risk of their being discredited by
revelationsthat may be made before publication :
The murder was probably committed by two
men. The alternatives are one, or three, or four. But
one could not have overpowered the three victims. Even
if they fully confided in him, he could not have bound
their hands by himself. And the hands were bound dur-
ing life. There were not four, because the motive of the
outrage was lust (probably two motives existed : lust and
revenge), and four people would not go to such lengths
for the same two girls. Further, they would be rivals,
and hate each other as rivals. These reasons exclude
even a third man. In the circumstances, that one brute
should be found is intelligible ; that two should be found
is possible; but that three could trust each other
sufficiently to work together is beyond all probability.
Again, there are physical limits. To overcome one
strong girl would exhaust man as well as girl. To double
the outrage is to double the outrager. If there were
three, the third took no part in the outrage. But, with-
out the others’ motive, it is in the highest degree unlikely
that a third would consent to share their guilt ; it is in
the highest degree unlikely that the others would accept
an accomplice whose hands were not dyed as deeply as
their own—to be horrified, and betray them. Two girls
were outraged ; the tracks of two horses were found; two
men asked a farmer lor horse-feed at midnight.
• • • •
The two men were brothers (or cousins, or inti-
mate friends). They had lived together, worked together,
played together. Only so could they have trusted each
other sufficiently to outrage and murder together. If
they were two brothers, two sisters would have attracted
them : brothers often marry sisters : the family qualities
which attract one brother attract another—for they are
of the same blood.
* • • •
The plan was conceived in one brain and ao-
oepted by another brain which could be maddened by
similar motives, could dare similar risks for similar ends.
A single outrage would have been appalling ; would have
argued the agent an exceptional brute in a civilised
society. The double outrage argues two exceptional
brutes, in the same place at the same time—and probably-
sprung from the same brutish stock. Brother could trust
brother. The younger brother would look up co, and be
influenced by, and rely upon the elder. The second man
must have been powerfully swayed by the first. Equal in
execution, one must have been superior in conception.
Who but a brother, in a society where such crimes are
held in horror, could have made another carry out his
conception ?
* * • •
The men were of strong physique, therefore
used to toil. Education—the cultivation of the brain-
lowers brutish strength, blunts brutish impulses, deters
from brutish outrage ; therefore they had little educa-
tion. They were between 20 and 30 years of age—if older
or younger, energy or desire would have failed them for
auch a crime : they were mature enough to plan, yet not
too mature to execute. They were unmarried, and lived
in a society where there was little vent for sex-passions.
(It is possible, however, that if the younger girl was the
chief objective the elder man was married. In that case,
the younger man may have been no blood-relation, but a
friend dominated by the other. Cut.this is less likely.)
They knew the country. They knew their victims—
no haphazard meeting prompted the double outrage.
Their victims knew them—for outrage had to be followed
by murder. They knew their victims’ movements, and
desired two women only—two others were let pass. They
were used to the saddle, but knew little of firearms—the
murders were accomplished in the method of aboriginal
races, with a stick ; the horse’s throat was cut because
they were uncertain as to the efficacy of bullets. They
lived away from cities—where there was small outlet for
passion; they were in the 'same station of life as their
victims; they were members of the same community;
they had frequently met them ; they might have gone to
school with them.
• • • •
The elder of the two men must be locally known
for his force of character and bodily strength—the
qualities which carried him through the day and night
of December 26 have indubitably made their mark
before. He is subject to violent fits of ill-temper, and is
locally feared and disliked. If both his parents are alive
he is one of a large family. He broods over slights and
fancied slights. He has already committed small acts of
violence. His anger is deadly—and silent. He is
sociable by fits and starts. He drinks rarely
and deeply. He is cruel to animals. His age is
from 27 to 29 (if married, he may be older). He is rather
over the middle-height, stoops slightly, is very strongly
built about the shoulders, has a prominent jaw and teeth,
coarse hair, and somewhat sullen expression. The
younger, aged from 20 to 23, is in character a weaker re-
flection of the elder, but differs in appearance, and is less
unpopular. The elder loved and was repulsed by the
elder girl—was repulsed several times, and eventually
with scorn. He has been attracted by her for years, and
has brooded over his fancied wrongs till his brain turned.
The younger man was attracted by the younger girl, but
it is doubtful whether he told her. But one or both of
them has fallen foul of the brother, and nursed a grudge
against him.
• • m •
The elder man’s grievance was of long-standing :
he had often hugged the idea of humbling scorn-
ful beauty. The revolver may have been bought—
months, years before. The tragedy dates Deo. 26—“ a
shocking treble murder.” On Dec. 14, what the news-
papers called “a shocking double murder” in Sydney
was reported all over Australia. Stuart Briggs shot a
girl whom he loved, and who scorned him. lie shot the
girl’s grandmother also. This is the kind of news that
strong-passioned, imperfectly-educated people brood over
for days. The elder murderer could hardly have missed
seeing it—could hardly have missed comparing Briggs’s
case with his own, and drawing the moral. On Dec. 21,
the Sydney inquest was held, and Briggs’ remarkable letter
read: —“ This is to state that the girl that has come to grief
by me is one who •professed, to love me, but it is sincerely
false. . . . But finding that she is acting falsely, I did the
deed. . ... And no doubt this will come uithin hearing of
the public, and be of a very substantial lesson to those who
are supposed to be had or taken in.” If that letter, or the
gist of it, was reprinted in a paper circulating in Gatton—
such as the Queensland Times— no more was wanted to
concentrate the murderer’s resolution. He would give
the girl one more chance, and then
• * • •
On Boxing Day he went to Mount Sylvia
races, saw hi 9 victims, and was repulsed. He drank
heavily ; he secured his accomplice ; they both drank
heavily. They heard that the violims were coming to the
Gatton ball ; they returned to their home or to some-
where nearer Gatton than the victims lived; had tea;
and learnt that the ball was postponed. Then the Mur-
phys would be disappointed. The Murphys I—and
floating ideas took definite shape. The men
rode forth ; struck the main road between Gatton
and Blackfellows’ Creek ; and waited at the slip-
rails. When the dog-cart came along, its occupants were
bailed up with the revolver ; probably told if they made
a noise they would suffer, if they were quiet no
harm would come to them. The elder man remained
mounted, pointing the revolver; the younger got
into the cart, and hound the hands of brother
and sisters with their own handkerchiefs. Then
he took his seat in the cart and drove through the
slip-rails; the elder man keeping at the side. A mile
further on the elder nmn dismounted; hung his horse
up; and told the Murphys to get out. They stumbled
out with their hands tied, and Michael was struck down
at once with the club. The blow crushed his skull in ;
the handkerchief slipped off his wrists in the fall. The
girls screamed ; the younger man held Ellen ; Norah ran.
tripped, and fell. Ellen fainted; Norah screamed and
fought persistently. The elder man got the hames
strap and choked her into silence. Later, the billet
of wood smashed their skulls also. The murderers
remounted ; washed themselves; and rode off. At first
maybe, they intended to go right away. But the horses
were tired ; as their insane fit passed the men grew tired
too. They turned and came home. If callous, they are
going quietly about their work; if remorseful, they are
the most active in the search for the murderers.
• • * • -i>
The absence of struggle noticed on the ground
points to overwhelming force—or inferiority of force. If
the idea of many men is excluded, the victims’ hands
were tied when they reached the place of doom. The
hands could only have been tied in the first shock of
surprise, before the Murphys realised what was happen-
ing to them. Of the climax to the proceedings they could
never have dreamt: possibly they thought they were
subjects of a rough holiday joke. They were confronted
by determined men—and a revolver. They yielded and
submitted to be tied before they understood'the situation.
When they understood—dimly—it was too late to resist.
The theory that Michael was decoyed off the road by a tale
of accident would be feasible but for the hand-tying.
The hands could not have been tied on the spot, or on the
track, or at any place but the place where the victims
were taken by surprise. Michael did not resist because
he never imagined the need for resistance ; or because the
revolver temporarily took him aback ; or because he felt
the responsibility of his sisters if he resisted unsuccess-
fully. The men may have been masked and discovered ;
or more probably they were not masked. In the latter
event there was so much less reason for distrusting them.

The Ballarat Woollen Mills lately called for
competitive designs and tenders from builders of steam
engines, that of the Austral Otis Engineering- Company,
South Melbourne, being accepted. These engines are of
the compound surface condensing type, with cylinders
17" and 38" by 60" stroke, and will indicate 700 h.p. on a
consumption of 161 b. steam per hour. The design is a
handsome and massive one, and they are probably the
finest example of engine-building yet produced in the
colony.*
Mephan Ferguson, engineer, &c., of Melbourne,
Adelaide, and Perth, the sole agent in Australia for J. and
E. Hall’s patent carbonic anhydride refrigerating and
ice-making machinery, is at present showing a large stock
of these machines, which have been got ready for the
summer season. The cost of refrigerators to produce
ftcwt. of ice per day has been reduced to £lOO each.
Special prices are quoted for machines to make up to 60
tons of ice. The patent carbonic anhydride system has
been adopted by the Victorian and other Australian
Governments ; also by all the leading steamship com*
panies, meat companies, dairies, and butchers of the
world.*

The N.S.W. loan estimates for the year ending
30th June, 1899, provide for the spending of 62,035,837
of borrowed money on public works. Of this sum
£1,281,114 is to go in works that are nominally repro-
ductive, and the other £754,000 is pure, unadulterated
swindle, to make the accounts look better, and to save
this generation the worry of paying its own liabilities as
it goes along, and to help Reid to keep his billet by adver-
tising himself as the Premier who didn’t increase taxa-
tion. Witness the following taken from Coghlan’s
“ Statistics of the Seven Colonies ” :
Spent on Spent on Total Loan
Productive Unproductive Expenditure
Works. Works on Works.
1895- .. £711,346 £559.552 £1,270,898
1896- .. 985,447 562,658 1,548,105
1897- .. 992,353 650,514 1,642,867
Proposed
1898- .. 1,281,114 754,723 2,035,837
Even the red bull which Potato Young sent up to Kemp-
sey was charged, according to Premier Reid’s own state-
ment in Parliament, to permanent and reproductive
works—the permanent and reproductive bull which our
descendants will still be paying interest on 500 years
hence !!
• • * •
The English, Scottish, and Australian Hank
makes very moderate progress, so far, at getting on its
feet again. For the 12 months ended 80th June, 1898,
the net profit was £6034, which compares with some pre-
vious results as follows
1894 Profit .. £10,154 I 1897 Profit .. £10,870
1895 Loss .. 6,279 1898 „ .. 6,034
1806 „ .. 27,950 I
In 1894-5-6 the E.S. and A. Bank was working under the
old arrangement, and things grew steadily worse. Then
it made new terms with its creditors and reduced interest
considerably—this year its reconstructed deposits cost it
less than 3J per cent, ull round. It has worked under the
new arrangement for two years, and again it shows a
downward tendency. The gross profits for the five years
(i.e., the profits before the interest on reconstructed
deposits are provided for) have been
1894 .. £164,330 11897 .. £130,535
1895 .. 162,408 1898 .. 119,560
1896 .. 139,812 I
This is a very steady decline, especially when it is con-
sidered that during last year most Australian hanks have
begun to exhibit a distinct improvement. Within the
last two years capital has been written down by £431,550,
reducing it to £530,438. And this year’s earnings are
only equal to£l 2s. 4d. per cent, per annum even on that
reduced amount.
The E. S. and A. shareholders have, so far as
concerns the immediate future, about the poorest set of
prospects going—those of the Queensland National crowd
always excepted. According to the latest re-arrangement
it was wisely provided that the first charge on net profits
should be £15,000 n year towards reserve fund, but so far
this amount hasn’t been available. Next the 3 per cent,
deferred inscribed stock (at present standing at £900,010)
is to get an increased rate of interest not exceeding 4j
percent., which will absorb unether 613,500. Next, out
of any balance available for dividend one-fourth is to be
set aside for the redemption of the deferred stock, so
before a 5 per cent, dividend can be paid the E. S. and A.
needs to show a net profit of about £64,000, instead of
the present 66,000—which is a very long, hard road to
travel.
In other respects, things look well enough. Dur-
ing the year ended 30th June, 1897, the bank paid off
about £460,656 of its reconstructed deposits. In the year
ended 30th June, 1898, another £117,545 was wiped' off.
The indebtedness to the reconstructed depositors is now
£3,267,149. New deposits (£1,420,278) are about £30,000
more than they were a year ago, so the business appears
to keep up reasonably well. Cash (£812,000) equals 47
per cent, of all immediate liabilities to the public—the
reconstructed deposits, being fixed for an indefinite
period, require no reserve against them. The re-
serve, including this year’s profit, amounts to £16,904.
In addition the bank has made (apparently all
this year) 629,175 by the purchase of its own deposits at a
discount. This is put to a separate account to cover
depreciation, and after writing of? £BBl6 for loss there re-
mains a second reserve of £20,359. The E.S. and A. is
the only bank which has the honesty to show profits made
by the repurchase of its own depreciated securities
separately from those made in the ordinary course of
business ; the average institution would have lumped the
two, and proudly shown a profit of £35,000 odd, and said
nothing about how the bulk of it was earned. If the
E.S. and A. can go on buying up its securities on this pro-
fitable basis it should be able to build up a reserve which
will eventually do a good deal to help it out of its diffi-
culties—and there is no visible reason why it shouldn’t go
on. All things considered, it looks sound enough from a
depositor’s point of view. And as it is steadily buying up
and extinguishing the deferred 3 per cent, deposits (the
ones which are to get 4£ per cent, when it is available) it
is reducing a big contingent liability for interest, as well
as making a handsome profit out of the deal in the mean-
time. In view of these things the shareholders’ pro-
spects are not getting worse on the whole, though they
are not improving as yet.

That tremendous absentee corporation, the
Australian Agricultural Co., held a meeting in London
recently, and supplied some interesting information about
itself. From 1824 to 1857 the dividends totalled £237,250,
an average of e 7189 per annum. From 1857 to 1838 the
dividends were £2,025,00’>, an average of 649,390 per
annum. The paid-up capital is 430,000, so for 41 years
Australia has provided this great Bull corporation with a
very steady dividend of 11J per cent, per annum—and it
is just about time the long-postponed absentee-tax hap-
pened in N.S.W.
* » • •
Apparently the time is not far off when all
the great importing houses will be merely branches
of absentee companies bossed in England. Within
the last year or two, Rich and Co., Henry Bull and
Co., M'Arthur and Co., and Farmer’s, of Sydney, and
Paterson, Lang and Bruce, of Melbourne, have all been
floated on the English market. When is that absentee-
tax coming ?
* * • *
N.S.W. Govt, has not taken over the Tarra-
wingee line, which Parliament decided it should buy, and
has apparently not moved in the matter. But, on a
report made by the local officer, the Tarrawingee co. has
forbidden the Silverton Tramway Co. to run any more
trains over it, several of the bridges and culverts having
fallen into utter disrepair. So Broken Hill is shut off
from two of its three pleasure and picnic places.
The Victorian public find that the notices on
Sillar-boxes do not give the correct times of clearance.
ertain changes were made, and they were notified once
in the obscurity of the daily press, but on the hoxes
themselves the* old misleading information remains un-
changed. The P.M.G. says that, now his attention is
calleS to it, he will have the proper alteration made. The
international packet-post has also been superseded by the
parcel-post, and this too was notified once in the daily
press, but the official Postal Guide still contains a clause
referring to the now non-existent packet-post for the
United Kingdom. So the public still sends packets, that
help to fill up the dead-letter office. Melbourne’s muddled
Postmaster-General mvv yet wreck the Turner Ministry.
* « • •
Melb. land prices are now as far under value as
they were above them at boom time. A big block in
Flinders-street, opposite the rail way-station and almost
adjoining the Gas co.’s offices, changed hands recently
at £2OO a foot, the foot being a 313 ft. strip with frontages
to both Flinders-street and Flinders-lane! The boom
price of this land would have been something like 61500
a foot, but then in those da3 r s one-frontage paddocks in
the suburbs with a depth of 150 ft. were selling for £lOO a
foot.
* * • *
“ Barrier ” writes : You ask, “ Why don’t the
B.H. mining co’s agitate for a reduction in freights from
the Silverton Tram Co., instead of heckling the S.A.
Govt, only?” Well, take a list of the B.H. directors, and
another list of the Silverton Tram directors, and the
answer is easy. It is precisely the same with regard to
water-supply. The charge for water (of wli : 'h large
quantities are used in the concentrating mills) is prepos-
terous ; yet, though Block 14 and, to some extent, the
South and British people have attempred to organise
some opposition, they have not been able to get a move
on the vastly more powerful B.H. Prop, and Block 10 co’s.
For the same reason, the co’s don’t move a muscle to
assist the project for running a rail-line between B.H.
and the Darling at Menindie, though that work would
make firewood vastly cheaper and would open an alterna-
tive’ route to the seaboard, 60 miles by rail and the rest
down the Darling and Murray.
Some little time back B.H. Water Supply share-
holders felt a little uneasy by reason of a move to run
the Govt, into providing a public supply. Tiie B.H. feel-
ing was exceedingly hostile to the co. But the directors
made a concession of 50 per cent.—from ss. to 2s. od. per
thousand—on all water used for gardening ; and Cerberus
has now pitched his growl in a lower key—so low that it
can’t he heard above the howling dust-storms which had
been raging out there for a couple of months past.
When a private co. seeks a concession in Aus-
tralia the tired democrat who can’t successfully put up
his back against the granting of it altogether reckons ho
has done pretty well if he has limited the operation of the
shindykit to a specific term. Sometimes this pans out all
right—sometimes it doesn’t. For instance, if the co. gets
a monopoly, and if it bus to quit in, say, 21 years, it just
hustles round and squeezes out of this generation what it
would be a fair thing to squeeze out of this one and the
next two or three together. Example: The Broken Hill
Water Co. It had to fight hard for its Bill; and when it
got it there was in it a provision that in 26 years from
date the works would go to the State. The result is that
the co., which spent nearly seven years making a reservoir
and waiting for it to be filled (that., of course, was before
the days of the God-fearing, rain-producing Ministry)
is trying to make the unfortunate owners of that sun-
baked dust-bin pay in 21 years the extravagant cost of
the Bill and the works, along with speculators’ profits.
And when the State gets the works they will probably be
so worn out and silted up (because Lite co. can’t he
expected to renew things the last few years), that the
whole show will not he worth more than a very casual
curse. The Water Supply Co. has only been moderately
successful in the squeezing process, but the Silverton
Tramway Co., with its 60 per shout, dividends, has been
a monumental triumph at the work.
• * •
A man from Maoriland has been in N.S.W.
some months spying out the fatness of the land and the
fatheadedness of its legislators. Finding that gold-
dredging was practically unknown here, he applied for a
patent for nearly everything used in gold-dredging, ex-
cept the hare hull of the dredge. The parts re which patents
are applied for have been in use in Maoriland for years,
and are well-known in N.S.W. and some parts have been
long in use here for harbor-dredging, and all of them have
been described and illustrated in public papers. Also,
the N.S.W. Govt, sent Surveyor Jaquet over the pond
to study the dredge in its lair and report thereon, pre-
sumably for the benefit of the mining community ; and
Jaquet’s book has been issued from the Govt. Printing
Office. The patent was ready for issue when some meddle-
some mining men got wind of it, and have at least tem-
porarily blocked it. If granted it will either cause the
infant gold-dredging industry in N.S.W. to be still-born,
or to start seriously hampered by royalties.
* * * »
The B.H. Consols—the huge English co. with
the little B.H. mine—held its annual meeting in London
recently, and congratulated itself that : t hadn’t made a
loss. In fact, six months’ receipts had paid 12 months’
expenses, all but £2OO ; so that £lO,OOO worth of assets
were in hand. This was a more successful result than had
been achieved for six years. It doesn’t always take a
Broken Hill to satisfy J. Bull. The Consols’ new imported
manager, Sweet, by the way, is described as having had
22 years’ practical experience, much of it among German
silver-lead.
• * * *
Gallyraont, N.S.W., is a Bull-owned mine, con-
trolled by Eissler, who cabled to London :—“ Made a
thorough examination and accurate tests of values of ore
prove samples systematically salted since Nov. (’97), de-
ceiving Hocking and myself.” A straightforward but
humiliating admission for an able mining man like Eissler.
Of this mine big things were predicted up to three months
ago. Nothing seemed the matter with it except, perhaps,
that amongst its claqueurs was the Sabbath tipster. It has
the crushing capital of £300,000, but plant and develop-
ment were on a big scale. The first crushing was a dis-
appointment and something was said about using too
much or too little water. Six weeks back writer was
shown an affidavit made by a workman that he and cer-
tain others had worked the mine with the view of getting
it on tribute, i.e., as much mullock as quartz had been
crushed. Then followed many threats of prosecution.
Now comes the admission of sailing. This is altogether
too puzzling. The mine was not for sale or working under
an option of purchase. Men who sold nearly three .years
ago were bought right out, and had no interest—salting
or otherwise. What, then, was the object, and who
the culprit? The energetic and experienced Eissler
practically lived on the mine, and as his manager was al-
ways there, the saltern must surely have been geniuses.
Eissler is the author of standard mining works. Eissler
on Cyanide,Eissler on the “ Metallurgy of Gold”;perhaps
we shall now have Eissler on the “Metallurgy of Salt.”
Shares, which long before the first crushing nearly reached
par, have been falling in London for some time, and, five
weeks ago, were down to 4s. 9d. When worked by small
parties, the claims now forming this mine were only noted
for their short chutes and small veins, and they were for
sale, and refused at much less than £lO,OOO for the lot
long before Eissler came along. What his company gave
the writer knows not, but there was surely nothing in the
property to warrant a co. with £300,000 capital. Nor
was there anything to warrant having fat, ornamental
directors with fat fees in Sydney to do little or nothing.
• * • •
Gibraltar—another of the Anglo-Australian
Co.’s mines—seems to have got on the down grade. No
crushings announced in London for three months running,
and the last is small compared with others before it.
Shares, too, have fallen over 50 per cent. This co. has
two other mines, one somewhere on the south coast, the
other some 30 miles from Cobar. Big things said of each,
but that seems too usual a circumstance for the moment.
This co., which did the biggest work in N.S.W., seems to
have had the hardest luck.

Shamrock (Bendigo) got on to good gold again,
working it from the Shenandoah level. Stone running
over2oz., and a 2s. div. for Xmas; working only 60ft.
from the northern boundary, and a lot of money wanted
for shaft and winding-plant if the find proves permanent.
Sensible thing would be to amalgamate with the North
Shamrock.

• * * •
Gold-stealing has never gone right out of fashion
on Bendigo, and is very common just now. Quartz not
as rich as in the old days, but opportunities for dis|>osal
much greater. Some buyers call themselves pyrites-
merchants. Some Chinese do good business in illicit gold
buying. Many miners, shift-bosses and managers sacked
lately, two or three convictions, but the business
goes on in spite of it all. Writer lately discussed
the subject with a veteran in Bendigo. Subject—Lansell.
“ I say it (gold) don’t belong to he. Ho did not put it
there, and I’ll have my share if 1 get a chance.” “ Well,
who put it there, my friend?" “It came with the
(blanky) flood.”

Justice Holroyd, Vie., has held that the pro-
posed amendment of the M‘Arthur-Forrest cyanide patent
is bad, and has dismissed the appeal from the decision of
the Attorney-General on the ground that if the amend-
ment were allowed the invention would vary substantially
from that described by the existing specification. What
will be the next move of the poor patentees, who are
having a lively legal time in Australasia? Meanwhile tens
of thousands of tons of gold-tailings are being treated
with the process without royalty ana with profit. Should
they eventually win all along the line what a glorious time
they will have collecting royalties. Verily they will be
“ The Australian Gold Recovery Co. 1"
• • * •
For low-grade gold-mining a comparatively new
field—Ellesmere, Vic.—wears the belt. Like most other
camps, it started with crushings much beyond the present
average, which is so low that sdwts. per ton is now re-
garded as rich ore. Central Ellesmere crushed for the
half-year 6900 tons for 91 loz., average 2'l2grs., giving a
profit, over actual working expenses, of £lO3O. Cost per
load, 6s. 9d., against 7s. Old. last half-year. Thomas
United :—The quartz from this mine averaged under
2dwts. per load. From crushing over 4000 loads of its
own, and doing some public crushing, a half-year’s profit
of £5lO was made. This co., without making a call, has
sunk a main shaft 130 ft., and put up a good winding and
crushing plant. Stewart’s United has paid nine divs.
from stone averaging less than 3dwts. During the half-
year it crushed 4881 loads for 476Joz.—under 2dwts. It
has just modestly declared a div. of £3OO. Stewart's Ex-
tended crushed 2778 loads for 2190 z., a little over 2dwt*.,
and, though it has no battery of its own, the half-year
shows a small profit. Let it not be imagined that
“ loads ” are not full tons. As a rule they are consider-
ably more. Also, be it remembered, in N.S.W. especially,
that there is no water-power at Ellesmere. All the re-
sult of cheap mining and management. Lodea big and
ground soft.
*£* -.SW * * *. •
Between 300 and 400 sluicing leases recently
taken up in Vic. Within the next 12 months the dredge-
system, inaugurated by J. A. Wallace, at Yackandandah,
will be in use all over the province. The old man gave
some sound advice on the business in Melb., lately.
He spent many thousands before he made the process a
success. By its use he was satisfied that many thousands
of ounces would be got which would never otherwise be
recovered. Some persons, in their enthusiasm, thought
tailings of any kind would do for dredging, but in his
opinion it was all the better if there was a little gold left
in them. Where there was no water to cause trouble the
Chinese had been at work and had left very little behind
—often too little to pay, but where John could not get in
for water the ground was all right.
• * • •
“ Tassic Re par. 3/12/’9B on Tas. Smelting Co.
“ Badger,” no doubt, thinks he has “got the tale by
rote ” (to quote Paterson), hut he hasn’t by a long way.
The Tas. Smelting Co. are more or less the same people
that are engineering also the Broken Hill experimental
plants under “ director ” Schmidt’s supervision. They
have unlimited capital at their back, and, despite
“ Badger’s ” superior knowledge, the co. will have its
fingers in most of the going mining concerns on the West
Coast. There is no violent hurry, as it would only mean
waste of cash to buy up any shows before their works are
completed. The other day the co. sent A. T. Brown, the
Vic. expert, alias “ Condemnatory Brown,” to Mt. Read,
where he was for a number of days sampling Hercules and
Mt. Read ores, and having a good look over the pro-
perties. These samples will decide more than sale of ore.

Rumor current Oonah S.M. Co.’s rlirectors intend
letting mine on tribute. If correct, shareholders ought
to shoot directors into kingdom-to-come. Even among
generally ineffieienct Tasmanian West Coast directors
the Oonah directors are noteworthy. The mine is a little
patchy in upper levels, but there is absolutely no reason
why matters shouldn’t improve further down. What the
mine wants is sinking. It ought to have been done long
ago. At present, they are picking out all the ore between
the levels, and soon none will be left. Then it is a cage of
making calls to sink or letting on tribute. Another pro-
mising property gone to the devil.
“ Shaker ” : Writer has seen published by quite
a number of papers (B. 22/10/’9B included) the statement
by the chairman of the Coolgardie Mining and Prospect-
ing Co. that “the co.’s working the ground pegged by
the original syndicate had produced 17 t-ons of gold, equal
to £24,000,000.” Yet even The Bulletin has not spotted
the fact that, to obtain the above result, the gold must
be worth at least £4O per ounce.
Cabled that the resolutions of the Great Boulder
committee with respect to the famous, or infamous, mill-
ing contract have been rejected by a majority of the
shareholders in London. Some 4000 members of the co.,
representing over 400,000 shares, petitioned for this meet-
ing in the hope that the contract would be cancelled and
the directors fired out. They must now grin and bear it.
What the contract means they will soon have an oppor-
tunity of knowing, as a start has been made with the
process known as Konemann’s. From the field : “It is
claimed that the Boulder Milling Co. has made a success
of its new roasting system—a shaft furnace—by crushing
the ore through a three-quarter mesh, roasting the coarse
ores and screening out the fines. The latter they hope to
be able to roast when they have made a few alterations to
the furnace. The cyanide part of the plant, which is to
treat the tailings, is not in operation yet, and, if a success,
it will be more expensive than the present methods, as
the milling co. is to receive the first 3dwt. and 40 per cent,
of the balance, and gives no guarantee of extraction.
The tailings for the last year have been running about
Bdwt.” No doubt further particulars by mail will show
that the process was pronounced a great success at the
meeting. But machines and processes cannot lie like
directors. This contract is a millstone around the direc-
tors’ necks, and in due time will carry them under.
Generally claimed by boomsters that but for the
great “ cat ” boom of Westralia the ten or a dozen big
mines of that country, and all at Hannan’s with two or
three exceptions, would never have been Incubated, and
there is more joy over these few than grief over the 199
dead and dying felines which Forrest and others have long
had reason to wish had never been born. When the boom
raged nearly all the Westralian companies were above par
in London. Time has changed all that. Many companies
have been wiped right off the slate, but as it is the rule
with Bull companies never to stop putting gold into mines
while there is any left for that noble purpose, a good
many “ stiffs” still pretend to be alive. The ruling prices
of these mines are regarded as a sort of Joke —except, per-
haps, by the holders who bought above or about par. A
few samples :—Arrow Brownhill, Is 3d ; Boss and Flinders,
3d ; Big Blow (19s 6d paid), Is 6d ; Blacket’s Gold Mines,
la ; Brownhill Proprietary, 6d ; Consolidated Murchison,
Is ; Consuelo, 6d ; Gladiator, 9d ; Golconda, 3d ; Cement
Claims, 2d ; Golden Ridge, 3d ; Hannan’s 100 Acres, Od ;
Hannan’s Main Reef, 8d ; Hit or Miss, 3d ; Kalgurli Great
Western, Is 6d ; King of the West, Is Od ; Lady Loch, Is
3d ; Mawson’s Reward, 4d ; Menzies Golden Age, 6d ;
Miner’s Dream, 6d; Mount Margaret, Od ; Murchison
New Chum, Is 6d ; Sam’s Wealth of Nations, Is; Stray
Shot and Excelsior, 6d ; Sugarloaf (25-Mile) Cement, 6d;
and many others in the same “ cat ”-egory.
London distribution of divs. from Kalgoorlie
mines bake View Consols, 6s. a share, £62,500; Ivan-
hoe Gold Corporation, 65., £62,500 ; Golden Horseshoe,
155., £75,000 ; Associated Gold Mines, 25., £46,000. Total,
£245,000. It. is big money and it has taken most of these
mines long to earn it, in face of enormous monthly out-
puts. That’s the trouble—divs. are not in the same street
with gold values. The ss. div. of Consols and Ivanhoe
are both disappointments. It is known now that the
Whittaker Wright crowd which controls both mines bor-
rowed money to pay the last two Consols divs., but that
was long ago and the mine has produced much gold since.
It was thought Ivanhoe would pay 10s. for a certainty.
What has become of the money Whittaker Wright and,
perhaps, Providence only know. Anyhow, it is about
time shareholders began to regard it as their business to
find out. In each mine the average per ton has
been coming down, and it is likely to continue to
fall. They seem destined to become ounce proposi-
tions, if not less. The only thing that keeps up
is the wilful extravagance of London management. The
powers of London directors are almost illimitable. A
man who lately returned from London, when asked where
all the money had gone, simply shrugged his shoulders
and said : “ 1 suppose a great deal of it has been used by
the London and Globe crowd in other schemes.” Some-
thing must change, or a big slump in shares is a certainty.
The 15s. div. from Horseshoe is a surprise. With con-
siderably less gold it pays £12,500 more than Consols,
and £30,000 more than Associated. Shares have risen
over 40 per cent. Whispered that it is proposed to in-
crease the shares, giving four for each now held. They
have reached £l7, and may go to £2O. The lode is very
rich, but whether it will always be in the ground is by no
means certain, therefore any'further increase in values is
unwarranted by the mine. Nearly all the shares allotted
to Australian vendors have gone to London. In fact,
with the exception of Associated, comparatively little of
these divs. comes to Australia.
When Tozer-Rougemonb leads his party in
search of those gold-deposits out of which the natives of
those parts make their omelette pans, will someone follow
on with an eye for cinnabar ? North-west aborigines’ pig-
ments are largely this sulphide of mercury, for which
there is a constant demand with no big surplus stocks to
wipe off.
The N. Y. Mining and Engineering Journal
opens an article on the much-discussed Konemann tel-
luride process in a style which should freeze the very
hearts of Boulder shareholders.

Scene—Fitzroy, Melb. Scribe
waking, after several heavy nights.
Mouth as though he’d swallowed a dog
head first; breath bulging out the
window —,£lOOO for a reviver . . .
Knock at door; fraud summons with Is.
conduct-money. Tears of emotion, and
henceforth a fixed belief in Providence.
Felton station, where millionaire Tyson
died, was once the property of the dashing Alfred
Sandeman, who for years posed as the genuine
gentleman squatter of Queensland. Sandeman
mortgaged the station to Tyson and went to Eng-
land, hoping to realise on property there and clear
his creditor out. But in the long run Tyson shut
down and Felton became his. The millionaire
looked on the transaction as among the luckiest in
his life.

RECRIMINATIONS.

HOB: “ How can I, when it’s always me that gets

The alleged N.S.W. Shops and Factories
Act has been shelved, apparently for evermore.
If the law were properly administered, half the
Sydney drapers and other big shopkeepers would
have been made to answer in court for their sins
during the holiday season. There is one eloquent
instance, where the employes at a large drapery
store were kept at work for 12£ hours a day during
Xmas week, and for hours on Xmas eve. At
the close of the busy season a number of
very tired girls are temporarily sacked, with
the prospect of re-engagement when business
brightens up again—at Easter or thereabouts. By
the way, it would be interesting to know how
many Sydney shops or factories now comply with
the requirement that copies of the most important
clauses of the Act shall be conspicuously exhibited
for the information of employes.
* * * *
In Nolland v. Nolland, heard lately in
Sydney Divorce Court, Justice G. B. Simpson in-
formed Nolland (respondent) that he could not
proceed with a cross petition as he hadn’t paid
costs in a previous case. A few weeks before, in
Caulfield v. Norton, Chief-Justice Darley held
that Caulfield’s not having paid costs of a previous
libel-action was no bar to the libel-action he was
then about to begin. If both Judges be right in
their different jurisdictions, equity and common-
sense are sacrificed to law—to a law which, it may
be safely said, was not understood by the Parlia-
ment that made it.

“ S.C.C.” lays at our feet a hunk of great
wisdom:—
The rather foolish remarks of “ School Ma’am ” (B.
24/12/’9B) seem to show that the writer doesn’t understand
the matter of “Dominie’s” avoidance of the female of
tender years. Nor is the danger entirely in very “big”
girls. “Dominie” evidently isn’t such a fool that he
can’t profit by the experience of those poor devils whose
want of caution has led to their downfall at the hands of
the prurient girl-child. Quite likely the “usual bush-
girl ” may be “ painfully constrained ” in the presence of
the school-master, and the latter may be safe alone with
999 out of 1000 such. But that is no proof that the lovely
“usual bush-girl” won't take a fit of some sort, imme-
diately after being so “ painfully constrained,” including
the imagination of assault and outrage. Page 7of same
issue of Bulletin gives an account of just such a case,
where the imaginative child was no more than 12. If
the constable involved had had a little sense, he would
have cleared in the opposite direction harder than the
average constable clears at the approach of danger.
“ School Ma’am ” never heard of a “ female teacher taking
precautions for her own moral safety against, the big boys
of the school.” And I don’t suppose she ever heard of a
woman losing her liberty and character through a false
charge of outrage brought against her by a boy, either.

“ Hermes” : I slung the port-
manteau onto the footboard and
scrambled up beside the driver. A
Sydney four-horse bus. Driver a
short, brown, ugly man with a weird
eye and an insubordinate whisker. A
tall, pale gent with a despairing look
in his eye scrambled up on to the
second box seat, and in anxious tones
inquired: “Will you catch the 5.17,
driver?” “Oh, yes, sir, an’plenty
to spare." Then the driver pulled
up at. Bridge-street corner and boy
on step yelled horribly, “Rhlway-e-e,
Relwhae-e-e-e, Rilewyyh ” driver
suddenly topping-off with a prodi-
gious roar of “ Rhoilwhooy.” Then
again the plaintive tones of the young
man in gray : “ Driver, I’m afraid you
will never catch the 5.17.” “Oh,
that’s all right, sir, we’ll catch the
blanky thing, safe enough ! You see
how my beauties ’ll tuck their hind
legs under ’em when we pass Market-
street.” We reached Post-Office
corner, and once more the boy de-
livered himself of various horrible
sounds supposed to represent “ Rail-
way !” Then the driver shouted to
his horses, made his whip crack like
a repeating rifle, and off we jolted.
We raced along to King-street, stop-
ped a moment to let two waddling
dowagers flop inside, and next minute
were flying past the new markets, the
man in gray holding desperately to
the seat and struggling to keep back a
series of shrieks. Down Brick Held
Hill, with the whip rattling like a
machine gun, in and out among the
crowd, gathering speed as we went,
to take the Christchurch hill with
a rush. The big ’bus rocked and
swayed, and, stuck up there with
our feet dangling over the horses,
we felt like corks on a raging sea.
Two trams were approaching in
opposite directions. The man
with the flag hesitated. Our
driver didn’t! With a shout to the
horses, and a scream from the man in
f gray, we dashed between the trams and
went whirling up to the platform like a
southerly buster in full blast. As we stopped
at the steps the driver pointed triumphantly to
the hands of the clock: “Five-ten, sir! Seven
blanky minutes to spare.” But the lips of the
man in gray were moving in silent prayer, as he
gingerly let himself down and fled.

PERSISTENT.
A year, another year, and all goes well.
The world is much the same as heretofore.
The heavy load of dull advice it bore
Has aged it not. The follies which they tell
It daily wrought have worked no Upas spell
Upon its buoyant forces. As of yore,
When poets say ’twas young, it heeds not lore
Of wit or sage, with candle, book, or bell.
It shows its old percentages of joy,
Percentages of sorrow—shade and shine—
Percentages of soul and body cares ;
To so much sterling good so much alloy
Or thereabouts—working its own design
Amid the babble of the lives it bears.
E. LOWE.
AFTER SCHOPENHAUER.
There is no rose without a thorn —
Sad is the song that ever flows ;
But sadder still the mightier truth,
There’s many a thorn without a rose.
W.B.
A BUSH LITANY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the last day shall break in crashing thunder
And pure souls rush to God,
We dare not rise to realms of gold and azure
On stairs by saints’ feet trod ;
But if far out upon the south sky’s blackness
Alone, nor curst nor blest,
Our two souls drown in the deep dark—together—
Let God take all the rest 1
W. MAYNARD.

The alleged boats of many N.S. W. coastal earn e reft re
never in the water; in ease of accident they’d be as
watertight as fowl-crates. Good old Marine Board .
Saw boat constantly being used by a well-known
coastal mail steamer for sending lines ashore, it
leaked like a sieve anti needed the active applica-
tion of the bailer whenever the two hands work-
ing it had a chance. What odds the other boats
(not in constant use) being watertight ? AJso a
recent episode in Sydney harbor. Small steamer
was about to start on long voyage, but was
stopped on account of a considerable list. At the
captain’s orders the hose was promptly turned on
to fill the boats on the opposite side, so as to tip
her uii in the right direction. Of course the water
would have been discharged again as soon as she
got away, and she would have listed as much as
ever but it was necessary to straighten her in the
meantime. Poured about half the harbor into
the cracked and sun-dried boats and every drop
promptly leaked out again. List no better, so
crew got down below with much strong curse and
proceeded to hump cargo uphill.—F.
“ Perce ” lets off steam ;
A Westralian bride recently died during the honey-
moon, and there were columns of lament written about
the “ dreadful tragedy ’’; said tragedy consisting of the
fael that the bride was snatched away when the couple
were presumably in the full-tide of hymeneal enjoyment.
Sentiment has hitherto declared that old things are
dearest: old friends, old homes, old arm-chairs—even
the old oaken bucket is extolled in long metre ; but old
wives are regretted only in music-hall songs like “My
Old Dutch." Outside the music-hall, amongst “ respect-
able ” people, the death of a wife after the first year of
marriage ceases to be a tragedy. True, the Emperor of
Austria tore his hair and begged heaven to tell him why
he should suffer thus, but his infidelities and her unhap-
piness were notorious. George 11. wept over his dying
old wife and comforted her by saying : “ I vill neffer get
married no more; I vill keep a harem." In the same
paper which devoted half-a-column to the “ honeymoon
tragedy ’’ this paragraph appeared : “ Our old and es-
teemed townsman, Mr. Bottlenosu, of the Swag and
Damper Hotel, was married last week. He and his young
bride received the congratulations of many friends.” The
first wife bail worked in the pub. for 20 years and Just as
a competency was attained she died. lie married his
housemaid a month later, hut this was no tragedy ; only
an old wife gone and a young one put In her place amidst
congratulations. The poet of Reading Gaol, though not
himself respectable, voices Respectability’s real oreed :
“ Tin hard to part from those wo have known but a short
time.”

Some wolf-in-sheep’s-clothing—full up to
the neck with evil—has got him into the true fold.
Bishop Goe, in tones of bitter disappointment, in-
formed Vic. Chinese Missionary Society, the other
day, that the religious books which they placed iq
the hands of the yellow heathen, and which the
Society, in its simple faith, imagined were pure
and correct translations of the Scriptures, were,
sad to say, a jumble of Christianity and Con-
fucianism and various other strange and sinful
opinions. Wherefore the Society now proposes
sending two missionaries to China for Instruction
so that it will be able to know what is in its own
books. Meanwhile the local Chow will continue
to profess Christianity so long as its profession
improves the “spling callot ” business and while
the shepherdesses continue to be nice and young
and plump.
A few Melb. Germans have attempted to
acclimatise the German Sunday afternoon and
evening. They have established a club—the Club
Tivoli —in Collingwood, near the odoriferous
Yarra, and it has caught on hugely. It is nicely
situated, with a handy bier-garten attached, and
Studley Park conveniently adjacent for moonlight
strolls after the dance. The Teutons have a
bowling-alley, card-tables, and a fine dancing-
hall, and, of course, a club license. The lager and
sausage and dance are not, perhaps, an exception-
ally edifying way of spending the Sawbath, but
quite as intellectual as the methods of the dour
Scotch by terian who has so long made of the Melb.
Sunday a hell-upon-earth. Meanwhile, a half-
mile away, the young men at Kew R.C. College
may be seen playing football on Sunday mornings
and lawn-tennis in the afternoons.

Writes “ Face ”:
On Boxing Day when a ferry steamer was putting off
from Manly w harf a woman fell overboard, and, although
both boat and wharf were crowded, mostly with alleged
men, no one offered to jump in and save her; all hands
rushed for a good front place to watch her struggling and
speculated as to whether she could last until a boat which
had put off from the beach should reach her. Several
daring characters rushed violently about and threatened
to dive in after her at the least provocation, but no one
did dive, and she was eventually picked up more dead
than alive by aforesaid boat. Which suggests the ques-
tion whether, after all, our males will not follow the
recent French performance when the inevitable
and long-expected colossal barbor-ferry accident does
occur. The average city Australian is not troubled with
any nasty pride, and instances are not wanting to show
that he doesn't mind bustling out of danger and leaving
the women and children to look after themselves if there
is any danger of his getting wet or soiling his clothes.
Can this, from “ Spectator,” be another version
of the above alleged occurrence :
Boxing Day witnessed a scene of quiet unrewarded
heroism which escaped the notice of the daijies. When
the s.s. Brighton, heavily loaded with excursionists, was
about forging out from the wharf a lust dash for her was
made by a belated party, and a girl of about 14 was
knocked into the w ater. Her head apparently struck one
of the piles, for she sank like a stone. Two passengers,
one elderly and the other a younger man, unhesitatingly
jumped off the sponson after her, not taking off so much
as a hat between them. They ran no little risk, for the
huge paddle-wheels were expected to si art at any
moment, and the child had sunk to the bottom. The
elder man brought the girl to the surface, when she was
pounced on bv her shrieking relatives. The rescuers
simply jumped on to the steamer, shook themselves, and
went off to Sydney w ithout a word of thanks or solitary
cheer.

Extraordinary story sent to
The Bulletin :
A medico in a little N.S. W. way-back
town lately objected to certain items in
the local grocer’s bill, which had reached
abnormal proportions in the month, and,
refusing to pay, was summoned. He
resolved to dispute the items, but on
attending court was amazed to find that
the presiding J.P. was the tradesman
to whom he was indebted. By the time
the flabbergasted sawbones had re-
covered from the shock plaintiff had
relieved himself of many nasty remarks
concerning persons who refuse to pay
their just debts, and had entered a ver-
dict for himself for fall amount with
costs ; and when at last defendant did
find voice to object he was threatened
with “ the rising” for “ contempt,” and
was hustled out into the street by the
local “ cop.”

Some unpleasantness has
been created in a Queensland town
over the conduct of an alleged
white teetotaller who —so it is
solemnly averred burnt - corked
himself into some resemblance of
a kanaka, and went round trying
to buy a drink in broken English,
with a view to getting convictions
against publicans for supplying
intoxicated niggers after hours,
contrary to the “statoot.” He
was such an ineffective copy of a
nigger that no liquor was forth-
coming. Which was a pity, for a
charge of selling grog to a white
man painted black would have
been interesting reading.

The “Queensland Horror” looks like one
of the most remarkable mysteries of crime that
Australia has yet produced. Three people —an
athletio young man named Murphy and his two
sisters, one 27, the other 18, both strong, well-
grown young women-—were driving home in a
dog cart along a tolerably frequented road, in a
well-settled district, near Ipswich. They never
got home; next morning they were all found dead,
their skulls fractured by heavy blows (and the
girls horribly outraged as well, and with their
hands tied behind them) in a paddock a mile or so
off the main road. Even the horse was included
in the wholesale slaughter. Yet there were no
signs of any struggle on the road, though they
were presumably captured there, and then driven
to the place where they met their death. Nor
were there signs of any very desperate conflict
where the murders seemingly took place. Appa-
rently, the Murphys were waited for on the road
—other people, who passed earlier, spoke of seeing
“a man” loitering about. But, in the ordinary
course of things, it must have required a number
of men to capture three adults in such a
fashion. How many were they ? Who were
they? Was the capture effected on the road, and
were the prisoners then driven across the paddock,
or did they leave the road to escape from some-
thing they saw ahead, and drive on till they were
overtaken ? If so, what did they see on the road
to make them turn off? The tracks showed that
the vehicle hadn’t pulled up (as if the occupants
were considering the matter or arguing with any-
body) before quitting the road. The turn-off had
been taken without halt or hesitation. Appar-
ently there was no earthly possibility that
it was merely a tragedy among themselves,
winding up by suicide, for the man had
been killed by having his brains beaten out,
and no man suicides in that way. The mur-
derers seem to have left practically no track
behind. There are stories about footprints being
found to show where one man was concealed be-
hind a tree, and that is about aIL
And, in spite of the fact that the country is
black with police and detectives and trackers
for leagues around, there is a great
scarcity of the usual news of the usual “sus-
picious characters ” being seen getting
away. It looks as if the crime had been
done by perfectly respectable local resi-
dents, who had simply gone home afterwards
and gone to bed, and then turned out in the
morning to help to hunt for themselves. By boiling
down the dead horse an ordinary revolver bullet
was found which would fit any number of
weapons, and up to time of writing one revolver
has been found to fit the bullet. It was found
in the possession of a harmless resident
who can prove that he, at least, wasn’t there.
Presumably no strangers would have waited so
elaborately for this particular party—one that
must have looked rather difficult to tackle, though
apparently it was captured easily enough. And
no resident of the locality is reported to have gone
amissing, so there is some reason in the theory
that the murderer or murderers are going round
as usual in the immediate vicinity.
The Gfatton tragedy recalls another Queens-
land horror, the murder and worse of the
barmaid Fanny Briggs, near Wiseman’s. During
the absence of her employer this poor girl
was most foully outraged and murdered. Her
employer on his return found tracks of naked feet.
The aboriginal troopers, when brought to the
scene, made trouble about the tracks; but ulti-
mately the body was found in a brigalow tree in
the scrub, showing terrible evidences of brutal

Apparently, the victim whose body was
found in that boot-trunk floating down the Yarra
must have been the original {Superfluous Woman.
The story of the boot-trunk and the deceased
inhabitant who was crowded therein has been
circulated all over Australia. Any number of
families whose ’laser or Sarah - llann hadn’t
written lately have risen up and raised Cain,
to find out if ’Liser or Sarah-Hanu was all
right, but she always proved to bo alive and
well. Many young women, having their
movements thus unexpectedly looked into, have
been found under circumstances which led
to their removal home, to be deservedly Bpanked
and set to hard labor on the family wash, but so
far every supposed denizen of that drifting trunk
has been accounted for. There seems to be abso-
lutely no woman missing—apparently, the mystery
in the box had no local home, billet, lover, board-
ing-housekeeper, unpaid bill, relative, pal, or un-
claimed trunk to draw attention to her absence.
Nobody even remembers about a woman who
ordered a new frock and didn’t come for it, or
whose boots had been half-soled and left on hand.
The real superfluous woman appears to have been
found at last—in a boot-trunk.

The Melbourne morgue and the detective
office have been visited by nearly 50 people de-
sirous of applying the supernatural to the solution
of the Yarra body-in-a-boot-box mystery—ranging
from the “ balmy ” parson, who prayed on the
steps of the morgue for divine guidance until he
was moved on, to the lady spiritist who stood .
beside the much-decomposed corpse in a “trance ”
for a solid hour on one of the hottest days.
• • » *
Palmists, clairvoyants, and other male
and female Cagliostros of Sydney have done a
lively trade in revelations about the existence of
squatter Tyson’s will. One well-advertised dame,
said to be under most distinguished patronage,
located the missing document in a hole in a wall
from which a brick had been abstracted, but was
painfully hazy as to where the house containing
the wall is situated.
The N.S. W. Lager Bier Brewing Co., Ltd.,
of Moore Park, Sydney, has placed upon the mar-
ket a splendid Lager Bier called Sydney Lager
Bier. This bier is the choicest thing ever offered
to the people of N.S.W. Analysts praise it most
highly ; doctors recommend it to convalescents,
and connoisseurs at the last Sydney Agricultural
Show gave it first prize against all competitors.
What has long troubled the rational people of
N.S.W. is “what to drink.” There is no fun
in summer weather in drinking fiery alcoholic
liquors thaf break up the nerves, affect the diges-
tion, heart, liver and kidneys ; and on the other
hand there is only an intense vagueness and total
dissatisfaction in drinking wish-wash. Sydney
Lager Bier is the happy mean between these ex-
tremes, and people in the country who desire to
have the newest and best drink going should com-
municate with the N.S.W. Lager Bier Brewing
Co., Moore Park, Sydney, for particulars. Al-
though the new lager is so much superior to all
others, it is the most reasonable in cost upon this
market.
