A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, January 3, 1899.
My Dear Myee—
Randwick on Monday didn’t seem to suffer
from the empty vice-regal box, as everyone in
town turned out in smart raiment. Certainly to-
wards the end of the afternoon the meeting was
rewarded by the sight of His Ex. in a patient smile
and a long frock-coat, accompanied by a much
fatter Captain Fielden than before Xmas, but
Lady Hampden didn’t show up. She merely
came to town with Miss Dolly Brand for the
Highland Gathering, and went back to Sutton
Forest next day.
I hardly know where to begin among so many
attractive frocks. Mrs. Alison, just back from
’Ome, looked like her pretty mother in bridal
grey, the skirt in three flounces, a style which has,
so far, resisted the public raid on styles. Two
brown-eyed sisters from Randwick wore a blue-
and-yellow muslin each with happier results than
you usually get from frocks that imitate each other
to a button. Mrs. H. E. Cohen was in barrel-
striped pink, without a collar, and walked about
with a pretty red-haired Melbourne woman in
black and white. A white beige frock, covered
all over with a coarse black spotted net
ended in a deep black lace flounce, and is
sure to be imitated by anyone who wants
to revive a tired white dress. Mrs. Percy
Rundle wore a purple hat with a light-green silk
gown, and repeated the hat in hyacinth at her
waist. Everybody had flowers in the belt, and re-
gretted it ten minutes after the racing started. I
also noticed a few timid attempts at the polonaise
that rules London and Paris rigorously this season,
but the article is not yet understood by local
frock-builders, and won’t be at its worst till the
winter sets in.
What is society'doing at Bowral while Hollo-
way’s latest melodrama runs so giddily at Sydney
Theatre Royal? The moral lessons that any
Nice person could carry away from that astonish-
ingly shady play, “When London Sleeps,” are
being lamentably wasted on good country-people,
who glean their ideas of wickedness from Rolf
Boldrewood and the Down and Gumtiiee Jernel.
As all “ Nice People ” know a little more than
this, they could have wildly-exciting evenings at
the theatre just now, to sav nothing of the im-
niorals they could pick up without stooping, as it
were. I can only say that I sat in front of a row
of sad-eyed bushies, who constantly disturbed the
atmosphere with that woeful click of the tongue
that means distress, and can only be rendered in
print by the letters “d’thih.” Also, that the
bushies didn’t go home after the first act —a broad
tribute to the play’s naughtiness.
That pretty, volatile little lady, Amy Gourlay,
plays the peculiar part of Maudie with a delicate
diablerie that just saves a fearfully tense situa-
tion. She looks altogether charming in a cream
suit that shows scarlet hose and linings, and a
natty hat to match. Her fluty speaking-voice
rings sweetly on the ear after the strange sore-
throatedness of the three other young ladies in
the play. Miss Eugenie Duggan in particular
engineers some croupy inflections into tier tones,
and seems to sit on her drawl to broaden it. “He
her hell loves her me ” may strengthen the case for
the hell-loved one, but really cannot be reckoned
a verbal improvement on the archaic “He loves
me.”
It isn’t every melodrama that can sport three
such well-dressed and pretty women as Miss
Duggan, Miss Gourlay and Miss Ida Gresham.
We are so used, at 3s. a head in the d. c., to find
melodramatic virtue clothe itself in eleven-
pence -’apenny-pink-satin with pearl-trimmings,
that the smartness of these ladies’ frocking smites
hard. Miss Duggan whisks her long blue satin
train about excellently, and pleases the fastidious
taste by throwing on' a long white cloak-to-the-
heels, instead of the short and lumpy waist-
wideners usually donned by the heroine when she
rushes into the midnight in her ball-dress. In the
white flowing gown of the circus-scene with a bind
in her hair, Miss Duggan struck a strong resem-
blance to Essie Jenyns as Parthenia. Another
time when the likeness cropped up—accidentally
perhaps, or possibly because Charles Holloway
happens to be Essie’s step-uncle—was when she
visited the Indian Exhibition in more flowing and
waistless white, with a little white Juliet cap on
her aesthetic head. This gown, by the way, was
quite a success in the horribly realistic suttee
scene that follows, but even for purposes of stage
suttee what heiress cf many thousands goes to
Exhibitions in a sort of white fancy night-dress,
capped with a white satin fancy cap ? This might
pass itself off as a nice tea-gown, or even an
aesthetic evening dress, but it is not quite the thing
for sightseeing, even in London, where the sun only
shines sometimes.
“ Hilda Carrode with a Past ” accentuated her-
self, but becomingly, with a black gown of the
future, cut right off the shoulders, as all ball-
gowns are to be cut next season. She also kept in
touch with smart people by wearing her hair on
top, where all fashionable women insist, in spite
of other people, on wearing theirs for some time to
come.
And here let me state a little fact for The Nice.
A big drapery establishment, heavily patronised
by the masses and visited slyly early in the morn-
ing by kerridge folk out on “the save,” has given
the greatest fillip to the neck-coil now so common
in this city. Yes, several hundreds of shop-girls
are employed there, and there isn’t one who doesn’t
do her hair down her back-bone.
Speaking of distinguished people reminds me
that the furious bubble of curiosity has died away
to a languid simmer since Govt. House betook
itself to Sutton Forest. This change of tempera-
ture may be due to the fact that there doesn’t
seem to be any chance of learning anything—you
know the sort of anything they want to learn.
All the same, it seems that other provinces are
just as interested as ever in the cause of the Hamp-
dens’ approaching exit. A pathetic letter from a
Westralian lady to a Sydney friend illustrates me
here:—“. . . I’ve already written to Maggie,
Ella, and Mary without receiving the slightest
satisfaction, and now I appeal to you. Do tell me
what your wicked old Governor has been doing.”
And now it turns out that he hasn’t been doing
anything (no one ever believed that he had) —he is
going ’Ome simply because his eldest son is about
to marry—which, as the son aforesaid is heir to
the title, is a serious matter, and calls for the pre-
sence of his pa.
At the Electric-light Carnival it created quite a
fizz in one’s brain-pan to come across fourteen
thousand people, well-dressed and well-fed, none
of whom are to be met at Exclusive affairs. Is
society, then, only a very small mite in this city’s
cheese? Deducting the unclad, and the strata
above the unclad, and the blind and the cripples,
the inner circle becomes too small to think in—
which probably explains several things. Every
variety of man, woman, and the sort of infant
that goes to chapel, pork-suppers, ’arbor excur-
sions, and comes in at the death of its own great-
grand-children, crushed through the gates the first
night of the carnival. The seating accommoda-
tion didn’t expand to the extent of the visitors,
and hundreds had to stand all the evening. As
for the electric-light, it was a brilliant success,
not leaving a dark corner anywhere for those who
sought one. And if little Continental tables and
chairs, with iced drinks and coffee, could have
been scattered about the lawns, anyone might
have spent a pleasant evening there.
A weird rumor seems to have come across
the seas telling that red is the only color tolerated
by decent society. Otherwise, how is one to
account for the sudden universal emulation of
poppies and peonies that marked half the women
at the Carnival—and red isn’t a shade that can be
chosen in the dark, and worn with any other color.
A prawn-tinted hat, shooting up from a flushed
forehead as straight as Browning’s red-brick wall,
with lappets of tangle between, doesn’t unite
peaceably with the madder-lake frock, esi>ecially
when the case is aggravated with a neck-bow like
the cover of this blushing paper. A Macliiavelli
couldn’t put three reds together without murder,
and the woman who buys without matching will
perpetrate some horrible crime if she isn’t watched
whenever she goes out in a red frock.
I looked in at the Pantomime again last week,
and found that, fortunately, things were being
lopped off wholesale. Viewed from half-a-step,
with a whole pillar in the eye, I saw even a little
less of the good things than at my first visit, but
managed to notice that Pattie Brown hasn’t tied
her sash differently yet. Will some case-hardened
person who is no relation please mention the
matter to her ? Otherwise she looks more charming
than I have seen her yet, as she has an excellent
opportunity to show what she’s made of. But she
still remains out of work.
Johnny Coleman undergoes a change for the
worse in his raiment to my mind ; giving up the
suitably-silly green frock for a grotesque old coat
that has too much the air of trying to be funny to
be successful. Also, it strikes one dolefully that
there is a harrowing absence of spontaneity in
everyone except the delightful Donkey, Little
Gulliver’s legs, and the small girl who takes off
Peggy Pryde so brilliantly. This child, little
Stella Tracey, ought to have a bright time coming
if she does not overstrain her poor small voice in
her early youth, It already shows signs of ill-
usage. , Her first song might reasonably be cut, as
nobody really wants to hear a little, shrill-voiced
girl imitate a hiccuping man.
What unaccountable things fashions are ! If
you don’t say “very’’three times over whenever
you say “ very -thusly, “ I’m so very, very, very
sorry I was out when you called ” —you are abso-
lutely and entirely out of it (or, perhaps, I ought
to write it, It). But don’t let us scoff. This may
be going to end well. If Sydney Society comes to
saying all its words three times over, think of all
the g’s and h’s that may be reinstated ! One
would have to be woefully hardened before one
could say, in the favorite words of a representa-
tive Sydney lady, “I’m so ’orribly, ’orribly,
’orribly sorry you’ve got to be goin.’ ”
The monotonous medal promises to become a
Eublic grievance. All the Sydney schools seem to
ave broken out into a perfect epidemic of medals,
gold and silver (especially sdver), which are
awarded for anything or nothing mostly any-
thing. Private teachers, too, bestow medals in-
stead of books upon their pet scholars. And for
such reasons ! Punctuality, Good Manners,
Needlework. Gold medals—Passing a Junior
Theory exam, (a child of eight in Sydney has
passed it), Geography, Politeness. By next year
the child that hasn’t a medal will probably be an
outcast or a curiosity.
Sydney church-goers never forget Dr. Julius,
long ago of Ballarat, who was made Bishop of
Christchurch, M.L. Wilson Barrett or Wallace
Brownlow never drew more crowded audiences
than does this interesting pastor, whenever it is
whispered that he is going to preach. Now that
his sprightly daughter, Ethel, is going to marry
Rev. Cecil Wilson, Bishop of Melanesia, in Feb-
ruary, Bishop Julius’ hearers will expect to hear
a few racy remarks in his next sermon regarding
the thieving propensities of missionaries.
The backblocks chess-players, who begin to make
it an annual custom to come to Sydney at Xmas
time, held their own against nearly all the metro-
politan chess-clubs this year with the exception of
the strongest coterie, the Sydney Chess Club. On
the occasion of the match played with this club,
they were beaten by 18£ to 4£, and even then
about six good players were absent from the
winning side. Col. Lassetter, President of the
S.C. Club, entertained all the players at a tre-
mendous “ shivoo ” last Wednesday, which some-
what consoled the waybackers.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Captain Frewen, Gov. Brassey’s departing
aide, left for ’Ome per same boat as young Lady
Clarke and her buxom, blooming sister, Miss Hilda
Cumming. The gentle ex-attach 4 will probably
find time pass more quickly this trip than on the
outward-bound voyage. His present shipmates
are famous for their high spirits. Toorak resi-
dents remember to this day the sounds of revelry
which invariably heralded the approach of the
four-wheeler, chartered by careful parents to con-
vey the then Miss Amy Cumming to and from
school.
“Last time a Via society leader sampled the
old country she told her chums, so the latter say,
that her main reasons for going ’Ome were:
(1) Purchase of diamonds suitable to her newly-
acquired rank ; (2) Opera-box near that occupied
by Prince of Wales; (3) To stimulate potentate
aforesaid by joint blaze of brilliants and beaux-
yeux to cunosity-pitch, and consequent enquiries
re the who-ness of his dashing neighbor. First
part of programme, the enterprising Australienne
fulfilled to the letter. Final clause presumably
resting in abeyance.
“ At the Bijou, Mrs. Brough, as Sans-Gene, in
her soap-sud days, wears short-skirted lilac frock,
apron, cap, etc., and apparently appeals to Mere
Man as powerfully as did Trilby. French washer-
women seemingly break hearts as mercilessly as
hospital-nurses hereabouts, unlike our own poor
laundry-splashers—elderly, Gamplike toilers, most
of them.
“Later on, as the blundering, ungrammatical
Duchesse, Mrs. Brough interviews tradesmen and
sich, in pink dressing-jacket over Empire petticoat
to match. Whisking off her coatee, prior to try-
ing on a riding-habit, she treats male spectators to
a full view of bare arms, shoulders, and high-
waist ed cerise under-skirt. Instead of grumbling
at this sartorial candor, the ducal husband
should congratulate himself that, though a vul-
garian, his Catherine’s toilette will bear inspection
below top stratum. There are Melbourne society-
leaders, pretty, dressy, brainy matrons, who, in
sleeping-cars, for instance, shed dainty finery
husks, prior to offending the gaze of sister-travel-
lers with frowsy unaer-skirts, adapted from
shabby street-suits.
“Sans-Gene’s hunting-habit is fawn cloth, waist
arm-high, funny little velvet jacket, and light-
grey soft-felt hat. Her reception toilet of gold-
flowered cream brocade with ermined crimson
mantle represents money thrown away, owing to
the fact that the parvenu-wearer trips up when
she curtseys and in the innocence of her heart tells
broad stories to supercilious sneerers. In real life
the ex-laundress would have aped the fine lady
convincingly enough, especially as, later on, in a
stormy interview with Napoleon, she shows her-
self the possessor of a head more tightly screwed
on than most. I’rior to failing the Eagle of France
in his eyrie Mdme. La Mareohale flings a green
empire cloak over her bo-diamonded decolletage
and soon has her ears pulled caressingly and her
bare shoulders slapped resoundingly by the cajoled
Corsican.
“Mr. Brough, as Napoleon, is convincingly cor-
pulent but too short-nosed for the Man of Destiny.
Boney is dished-up without his melodramatic
cloak, untroubled by hankerings after his Jose-
phine. Somehow, 1 don’t believe he hankered
anything worth mentioning. Anyway, Brough
makes the upstart Corsican whimsical, eccentric,
short-tempered, while affable, humorous and
susceptible.
“Miss Sisley, in blue, with pink rose in her hair,
personates a fresh young Duchess. A welcome
innovation this blooming damsel ! The Brough
co. needs some fresh young faces badly. One
handsome, graceful matron goes a long way, but
pretty girls and an effective lady-killer have been
cried out for, nevertheless.
“Mrs. Brough’s plump shoulders are useful for
the part of Madame Sans-Gene. Napoleon had a
familiar way with people he liked, and when he
expresses his approbation of the ex-washerwoman
he slaps her on the bare back with a smack that
resounds through the theatre. There is a good
deal of slapping by Brough as Napoleon. He is
fond of slapping his thighs at decisive moments,
but these slaps are not as real-sounding as those
administered to Mrs. Brough. Mr. Brough’s form
is not Napoleonic, so he ‘makes-up,’ with peg-
toppish effect.
“At the Bijou, Vera Renny, as the upstart
Queen of Naples, in brocaded Empire gown, chig-
non and side-curls, makes a realistic parvenu
princess. That lolloping stage-gait of hers does
well for a jumped-up daughter of the bourgeoisie.
Italia Conti, Bony’s other stage-sister, with her
tip-tilted plebeian profile, looks a prettyish young
snob.
“On New Year’s first night, a South Yarra con-
tingent were jointly interested in Miss Sisley’s
first appearance with the Broughs and the stage-
debut of young Purbrick. Pa Sisley, elocution-
teacher at the Conservatorium and Working-men’s
College, lias handed down his histrionic leanings
to his two bright daughters. A while back, these
courageous chicks engaged the Bijou, and supplied
an entertainment off their own juvenile bats, plus
a song or two by the late Gladstone Wright and a
little amateur orchestration. Mr. Purbrick is a
versatile legal sprig, who, though he distinguished
himself in law-court exams., has not as yet applied
for admission to the bar; on Xmas. Eve, he shouted
* Down with the Tuileries ! ’ as complacently as if
content to live and die in his present rdle of
‘Second Citizen.’ Off the boards, the lawyer
is a little dark and vivacious youth, a promising
orator, and favorite smoke-night reciter. The
Brough co., by the way, boasts another recruit
from local legaldom in solicitor Pringle, servant
to Sir Kit, in ‘ The Liars.’
“Local small fry run ‘stiff, stark, staring
wild,’ thanks to Fitzgerald Bros.’ big show.
’Twas the voice of the weekly washerwoman ; I
heard her, and many like her, complain of baffling
knots in their clothes-lines. As for cows, cats,
and similar dumb martyrs, they would be far
better off lying down with the lion, outside or in,
as might be decreed by Mein Herr, the animal-
king, than enduring present annoyance at the
hands of boyish beast-breakers.
“The heart of John Bull, junior, ’tis said, still
beats true to his Xmas pantomime. Young Aus-
tralia, when given the choice, gladly substitutes
circus for transformation-scene in his holiday pro-
gramme. And there are more reasons for this
preference than our national horse-cult. The
juvenile pantomime-patron sees the stage spec-
tacle and nothing more. A glimpse behind the
scenes is further out of his ken than a peep into
heaven.
_ “ But the ring holds less than half what the
circus-goer gets for his money. Caravans, brass-
band, wild-beasts, stables, horse-flesh, canvas-
town, tent-folk—white, black, brown, and yellow—
food for glamour, enchantment, romance, if you
like.
“For that matter, most of us own to a weakness
for the scent of the tan and its associations.
Especially those whose childhood languished in
arid, wayback villages, neither town nor country,
uncheered by flowers, fruit, shop-windows, amuse-
ment of any kind. For such ‘ spirits in prison ’
the unlooked-for arrival of a circus with its music,
fizz and fuss was wont to disperse the brooding
dullness effectually as ‘the waft of an angel’s wing.’
Oh, those equestrienne fairies ; * beautiful as but-
terflies and proud as queens! ’ Local mothers
preached themselves hoarse on the text of the
sirens’ paint and powder. Their daughters
listened half-heartedly, just as their younger
brothers did to the oft-told maternal recital of the
Sydney boy who peered through a hole in the
circus tent till the clown jabbed out his prying eye
with a penknife. The victim’s parents were said to
have rejoiced in their son’s salutary suffering.
This sounds harsh, but an old-time Anglican
bishop, whose only son was drowned bathing on
Sunday in his father’s first Australian diocese, told
Sunday-school scholars that he ‘ could not regret
his boy’s self-hastened end if careless companions
took warning thereby.’
“ Circus toilettes of to-day put those of long ago
completely out of court. In the Fitzgerald snow,
‘ Mdlle. Millie Dagmar ’ (Mrs. Tom Fitzgerald)
wears white habit nicely laced with gold, a splen-
did color-match for her white and fawn mount
‘ Napoleon ’ —the handsomest of * Bony’s ’ local
namesakes, human or otherwise, up to date.
Twice as tender-hearted a brute, indeed, as the
crusty Corsican himself, for, if beaten by his fair
rider, ’tis said he goes off his feed at once. When
the haute icolihrc quits the saddle for the ‘ rolling
globe, ’ she affects deep-rose satin, the short skirt
painted with a bold design of poppies, while a
touch of faint green lisse tones down the corsage
tints.
“ This lady is as busy a bee as any in the circus-
hive. Besides designing her own costumes, and
those of junior members of the troupe, she keeps
a watchful eye on the latter and their way of put-
ting on their finery. See her tie this one’s shoul-
der-straps and drape the other’s sash, and you will
understand how her careless charges contrive to
look as dainty as a group of ‘all-nation’ dolls,
newly-dressed for a bazaar.
“ Hearing of roamings by land and sea, ponder,
ing the discomforts of frail canvas dressing-rooms
throughout Bananaland and Westralian summers,
one would expect to encounter in ‘ La Belle Rose,’
the equestrienne and ‘globe-trotter’ aforesaid,
a weather-beaten campaigner with a wrinkle and
a freckle for every minute of her busy life, rather
than the girlish, pretty, young matron of the
reality.
“‘Such a lot of funny things’ one hoars in
Fitzgeraldville of ‘ lovely young Lizzie,’the nine-
year-old performing elephant. This engaging
artiste, though full-grown, may fairly be praised
as an ‘ infant prodigy,’ considering that elephant-
folk habitually defer departure to the happy
hunting grounds till the ripe age of one hundred.
Anyway, ‘Eliza, Elizabeth, Betsy, or Bess,’
knows her way about, riding her trike and using
her serviette like other up-to-date females. Up
Bowen way (Q.) she once got hysterical at
midnight, and soon had the hotel back-
yard hare as a billiard-table of barrels, hen-
coops and outhouses. While messengers hunted
the township for her trainer, the landlord
stood at an open window in his sleeping kilts,
crying like a child, and handing out loaves of
bread, bananas, all the eatables in the house, to
the serenader as fast as his wife could bring them.
Poor, dear Liz, she’s too loving and confiding, like
the rest of her sex. Way back she set her affec-
tions on some male knockabout, not her keeper.
This wretch got too thirsty one night, and re-
ceived black eyes and bluggy nose in consequence.
When Lizzie saw him she tenderly stroked his
poor face with her trunk, moaning and whimper-
ing to herself at the thought of his pain. For
kinder critter ne’er drew breath than this elephant
gal Elizabeth.
“The rage for a European verdict is going to
deprive Melbourne of its most popular ballad-
singer, and Maggie Stirling is to have a big benefit
in February to set her on the way to Paris. As a
wife bravely struggling to support a sick husband
and a family of children, Maggie Stirling has long
had the public sympathy. It is to be hoped that,
whatever she gains, she will not lose that human-
hearted singing that always fetches her audience.
By the way, they say that the coming Melbourne
accompanist is a Miss Rosie Siogenburg, pupil of
Herr Edouard Schaff; she played at some late
concerts in a way that showed the right accom-
panist is born, not paid.
“IJc occupations for women. A Melbourne
spinster has worked up a splendid connection for
herself in the house-agency line. If a house-owner
can’t get his place tenanted he consults Miss X.,
and she does the trick for him, if anyone can.
Toorak’s finest mansions are entrusted to the same
lady’s care ‘to be let furnished,’ and she justifies
the faith of her clients every time.
“Now she offers to advise customers as to what
business or occupation is best suited to their
powers —terms, two guineas per prescription.
This field is panning out splendidly, and besides
leads indirectly to good business in sales of schools,
purchases of tea-rooms, placing of governesses,
&c., &c.
“ There used to be sold at Bayreuth, during the
musical festivals, cuff - buttons and scarf - pins
adorned with portraits of the ‘great master,’
Wagner, and people used to sport these emblems
at the performances. Melbourne Cornervatoriurn
of Music girls are now following the practice by
wearing, as brooches, buttons adorned (if that is
the right word) with portraits of Professor Mar-
shall Hall. The beloved prof, affected to be much
annoyed at the issue of the souvenirs, which, how-
ever, are still worn as emblems of worship.

No. I—THE BAKER.
Mr. T. Streater writes: “I have received so
much benefit from Dr. Tibbies’ Vic-Cocoa I feel
that I must thank you for it. I am a baker by
trade, which, as you know, is very hard work, but
I am pleased to say that when I am done of a night
I go home and have a cup of your Cocoa, and feel
quite fresh again, and then I go and enjoy myself.
But before I took to Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa, I used
to mess about indoors as tired as could be.”
Vi-Cocoa will become a household word amongst
us. Prepared from substances of admitted
dietetic value, Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa is at once
digestive and refreshing, nutritious and invigorat-
ing. This is the secret of its phenomenal success.
It seems, so far as we can gather, to agree with
everybody, and everybody is agreed in giving it a
good name. Apparently it is the accepted bever-
age of a people who have recognised and appreci-
ated its remarkable health-giving properties. And
certainly in this age of rush and worry, v ear and
tear, when the energies of mind and body are so
severely taxed, it is an ideal food.
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa can be obtained from all
chemists, grocers and stores, or from Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, Limited, 2(19 George-st., Sydney.
As an unparalleled test of merit, a dainty sample
tin of Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa will be sent free on
application to any address, if when writing (a
postcard will do) the reader will name The
Bulletin.*

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Maoriland Seddon grows more and more
like a re-incarnation of Vic. ex-Premier James
M'Culloch.
Weather-prophet Ellery (Vic.) used to tell
of a post-card forwarded by an old lady asking
him to be sure and let her know when there would
be a “consternation.” She wished to see one.
Stipendiary Addison, retiring from the
Sydney Bench, where he sat for 17 years, has been
42 years in N.S.W. civil service. He is a lineal
descendant of Addison, the classical English
essayist.
The only boastful anecdote Jimmy Tyson
indulged in referred to the bunyip. He believed
in the bunyip, and asserted that he riddled one
with rifle bullets and hoped to capture the body,
but, bunyip-like, it eluded him.
The case of Ellen
M‘Nabb recalls a matter
tried by Judge Moles-
worth, sen., many years
ago. A Fitzroy volun-
teer, after a review,
deliberately shot his wife
dead —no word of pro-
vocation, no nothing—
simply shot her. In-
dicted for murder, found
guilty of manslaughter.
The astounded Moles-
worth said it must be
either murder or acci-
dent, and passed sentence
of one week’s imprison-
ment !
S.A. has had 11
Judges: Jeffcott, ap-
pointed in 183(5; Cooper,
1838; Booth by, 1853;
Gwynne, 1859; Hanson,
1861; Wearing, 18(57;
Stow, 1875; Way, 1876;
Bouoaut, 1878; An-
drews, 1881; Bundey,
1884. Jeffcott had the
shortest record—one year.
His successor, Cooper,
had the longest—23
years, though if Way
lasts another year he
will leave Cooper behind.
Boothbv, who had to
be forcibly removed from
the Bench to prevent
him turning the whole
provinoe upside - down,
was the most remarkable
of the crowd. Booth by
was the last Judge ap-
pointed direct by the
British Crown; when
Responsible Government
came in Gwynne and
Hanson received their
appointments locally.
Boothby, in the glory
of his British appoint-
ment, refused to recog-
nise the existence of
these locally-made offi-
cials. Even when he sat
on the Bencli with them
he used to mention in
his judgments that hie
learned and bogus
brothers had no real
standing there, and that
he, the only genuine

always on the point of
committing the other

turned loose a whole
assize-load of criminals,
on the ground that they
were not legally before
the court, some docu-
ments connected with
the assize having been
signed by one of his
learned but sham brethen
instead of by himself.
Then Boothby was shift-
ed off the Bench and
S.A. had a much-needed
rest.
S.A. Chief Justice Way’s devotion to the
little Bethel of his father is paralleled (for some
distance, anyhow) by Shenton’s connection with
the Perth Wesleyans. The knighted Shenton on
week-days bosses the Westralian Leg. Council, of
which he is President; and on Sundays and spare
evenings he conducts the Wesleyan choir and
urges the weary, sore-footed hymn over the track-
less wastes of doctrine.
The father of the present Acting-Justice
“ Dick ” O’Connor was clerk of N.S.W. Parlia-
ment for many years. Printed somewhere that
he was a clever flautist, and once took a favorite
instrument for repair to Henry Parkes, when the
latter presided over the toy shop in Hunter-street
and labored at the turning-lathe. When Parkes
first got into Parliament he ambled up to the
clerk and blandly said, “ Hi suppose vou don’t
remember me? ” “ Oh, yes,” said Mr. O’Connor,
“ I do ; you are the man that ruined my flute.”
Robert Stout was for years the champion
lolly-chewer of Maoriland Parliament, just as G.
Reid is in the N.S.W. Legislature. The other
Parliaments have never had any champion lolly-
chewer, though the beer-chewer is mostly there.
Three provinces in succession have held the record
in the last 30 years, as owning the champion
political drunk of Australasia ; one of them, in
tact, held it twice. And a fourth province would
have had a chip in, only its very promising drunk
died young and was cut off before he attained the
fulness of his beer.
On N.S.W. roll are seven solicitors ad-
mitted in the 40’s, seven in the 50’s, and 28 in the
60’s. On the Bar roll—not reckoning Judges or
ex-Judges—are two members called in the 50’s,
eight in the GO’s, and 19 in the 70’s.
At the “break-up” of Bankstown(N.S.W.)
public school every recitation on the programme
was by “ The Banjo,” and had been originally
published in The Bulletin. Let us print the
songs of a nation, and we care not who publishes
its leading articles.
The cachinnatious A. J. Peacock—
Australian Jackass Peacock—is undoubtedly
the youngest Minister of Education that Victoria
has had thus far. Peacock is almost too young.
The other week he turned up at a country State-
school attended by a pet monkey, to amuse the
children. They laughed, and he laughed—O !he
did laugh when one of the boys began to scratch
the top of his head and the monkey hurried over
to discover the cause of the irritation.
“FORTY THIEVES,” AT HER MAJESTY’S, SYDNEY.
A Sydney paper solemnly ascribes to
millionaire Tyson the remark that “he hoped his
heirs would derive as much pleasure from
squandering his wealth as he did in making it.”
This historic phrase was formerly attributed to
“ Big ” Clarke; it was also used by Captain
Cook and Christopher Columbus, and Li Hung
Chang. What Tyson really did say was:
“ Had I but served my God as I have served my
king, he would not have deserted mo in my old age,”
or words to that effect.
John Soult Fortescue Le Clere, who died
at Rockdale, Sydney, last week, aged 83, seems to
have been a nephew of Napoleon’s great general,
Marshal Soult. He had seen some ups and downs
—was originally intended for the diplomatic ser-
vice, but entered the church. For some time he
got his livelihood as a Murrumbidgee fisherman at
Hay.
Listen, ye rhapsodists, and learn. Says a
voice from Kosciusko :
Night comes on at last. The full moon “ lifts her lamp
on high,” and we are positively awestruck. Go ye hence,
ye Atheists, and you, ye votaries of those hideous doc-
trines of eternal damnation, double-distilled hell and
annihilation, or reverently bow before the Power
Supreme, for you are face to face with him, the Great
Meteorologist-in-Chief. Behold his work, supernal laws,
and deny and damn men who dare.
Dear Clement—how he wallows in elephantine
incoherency ! How he pats the Almighty on the
back, and gives Him the same billet that Wragge
himself possesses !
“ Southerner ” : Everyone in Australia
does not know that Madden, Macedon Syme’s
friend, is brother of the Chief Justice. A third
brother, the attorney, began the family streaks of
luck by marrying the daughter of Premier Francis.
Madden, the father, is yet extant in pink, on a
white horse, like Napoleon Knight’s, to look at
the hunt, which he chronicled in the Austral-
asian.
Archbishop Dunne was once building a
church at Toowoomba (Q.), and was asked by
Tyson what sort of a subscription he would like.
“ Oh, anything, down to sixpence,” was the
answer. The big cattle-man responded with a
cheque for .£5O. “And that says the
Archbishop now, “ is worth more to James Tyson
than all his millions.” How humorous is the
cheerful cocksureness of the cleric !
J. F. Perrin, editor of Perth Monitor,
previously editor of the M.L. Tablet, on arriving
in Victoria 30 years ago, wrote to Charles Gavan
Duffy enclosing a letter of introduction and soli-
citing use of his influence. Duffy replied: “The
best recommendation you can have is to let the
Victorians know that your uncle did all in his
power to hang me." It was before Chief-Justice
Perrin that Duffy was tried for treason-felony.
Premier Kingston’s political opponents
find him too strong to dislodge, but they are
always taking mean advantages of him. Because
the Mayor of Glenelg has a personal grudge
against him, Kingston was not asked to the Foun-
dation Day banquet on December 28. This, too,
although his father was second in command of the
survey-staff which originally laid out Adelaide.
Kingston now is more than ever determined to
lay out the Conservatives when the proper time
comes.
“S. le W.” : The society scribes have
never mentioned Vic. Chief-Justice Madden’s
bow. I don’t know his Court of Fashion one, but
his Supreme Court entree is superb. He catches up
the red-and-fur night-gown opposite the breast,
raises it so as not to interfere with, but expose, his
shapely legs, draws one foot back 24 inches, and
slowly bends a la bowing figure in the lancers.
All this is to another belaced figure, only the
second figure is draped in black. The same is
done in a general wav—l suppose, this time, to the
bar or the public. He ought to have been an actor
—in fact, he is one
The discovery that Eugene Cerf, the Bal-
larat hawker, is a relative of Captain Dreyfus, of
Devil's Island, simply goes to show that, small as
is the population of Australia, it is able at short
notice to produce a relative of any man on whom
the eyes of the world momentarily rest.
N.S.W. Parliamentary humorists have
found another butt in the bull-voiced Monaro
Miller. He was looked upon as a rather ordinary
bore until once, when he was roaring his loudest,
“ Jack ” Haynes politely asked him to speak up,
and Miller, not noticing the “borak,” spoke up.
“ I beg your pardon,” said .John, leaning forward,
with his hand to his ear, after the manner of the
deaf. Miller nearly lifted the roof as he bawled
the remark again. “Excuse me, I couldn’t quite
catch it,’’ said Haynes, solemnly, his hand still
to his ear, and it was not until he had awakened
the whole neighborhood, and members were rush-
ing in to see what the explosion was, that the
man from Snowy River “ dropped.”

Some years ago, when a
batch of M.L.C.’s were
to be appointed prior to
the then Premier leaving
for England, it fell to my
lot (as a junior clerk) to
deliver half-a-dozen of
these formal guberna-
torial communications.
In the last instance I
saw my man’s face flush-
up as lie read the letter ;
then turning to his clerk
he said: “Leave the
room, I wish to speak
to this gentleman on
important private busi-
ness.” When the sur-
prised clerk had clearod-
out I was requested to
seat myself, asked for my

nominee, who was actu-

citement. Then he rang
violently, and when the
clerk caino in, “Smith,”
said his master, “ there
is no paper in this office
fit to answer this import
ant communication on.
Go immediately and buy
ten shillings-worth of
fancy note-paper.” The
astonished clerk disap-
peared, and the new
M.L.C., pointing to a
photograph on the wall,
informed me that that
was his country seat, and
would I do him the honor
to spend two or three

then the clerk returned
with about a cwt. of
notepaper of all the

(sort Mary Ann com-
municates with her

future legislator sat down
to write, but he was

he had to get me to do

happy man then grasped

added that he would see

said I wasn’t going, but

off his clerk on the
spot with a guinea and
my name to the committee-room. When the
clerk returned with the necessary invitation I was
at last able to get away ; but I have often since
thought that I fooled away a si wend id chance of
borrowing .£5O or marrying an M.L.C.’s daughter.
If that man is ever offered a C.M.Gr.-ship, the
lunatic-asylum will get him, sure !
How history is fabricated is illustrated by
an incident re the cottage at White Hills, near
Bendigo, where Premier Salisbury is alleged to
have lodged during his visit to the goldfields about
45 years ago. When the Tory hack, H. C.
Richards, was here last year he visited the place
as reverently as though visiting a sacred shrine,
and took home a photo, to Salisbury. Now
Richards sends out a portrait of the Prime Minister
to the occupant of the house, with the remark
(vide Argus report) that Salisbury “ could not
identify the particular places at which he resided.”
But the Age reports that Salisbury “ recognised
the place, and was so pleased that he sent an en-
larged photo, of himself in an oak frame.” There
is nothing in the Argus report about Salisbury
sending the portrait himself. How is it likely
that he should recognise the place, even if it still
stands, which is doubtful? Salisbury left England
in 1850 for a tour. He was back in 1853. He
spent most of his time on board ship and in
Maoriland. In some accounts he is said to have
lived in Bendigo in a tent; but gradually the
White Hills house myth has grown up. In a few
years people will point to his footmarks on the
garden path, and a few of his Lair combings will
be preserved in a glass ease, whilst the walls may
be expected to become covered with his writing—
in ink or his own blood.

• • * *
“ Jep” : The usual summer query—Why
won’t the bushy wear straw hats ? —answered.
One droughty summer, after a brief sojourn in
town, I revisited the selection arrayed in city
1 clobber ” and an up-to-date straw hat. The
paddocks were as bare as a bear’s rear. I greeted
* Old Poley,” the family milker, lying half-
starved in the middle of a paddock, with winsome
and well-remembered “kerp, Poley 1” Noticing
the startling activity with which the cow got up,
and the hungry look in her eyes, I sprinted fence-
wards, Poley close up. Ten yards from shelter
she got alongside, and licked the hat from my head
with her tongue, and, from a safe perch, I saw it
journeying to Poley’s innards —the first real food
she’d eaten for three months. I always wear a
common felt hat now when I visit “our farm.”
• * * •
“Jeff” on the old bone controversy :
I have seen horses and cattle chew bones in New Eng-
land country, but they have been old and white without
a vestige ot meat on them. I’ve a moke, however, that
will put the horse liars up some. The first station I
shore at this year she lived on waste bread, curry and
rice, plum-pudding, &c., and would occasionally try some
Irish stew. Next shed (Bunna Bunna, near Narrabri) she
completely gave up her vegetarian principles, and the
dogs had no hope of getting any raw legs of mutton or
shins of beef that “ went bad.” Then, by way of variety,
she ate all the feathers from off two emu skins. One even-
ing she tackled a sheep that had died of starvation and
devoured a goodly portion of it. But the chief of her
delights is a real good long beer, together with a loaf of
bread and a tin of “ joy ” (golden syrup), and that Is
what she gets when I want her to do a 70 or 80 mile stags.
After eating all this stuff she was rolling fat.
• • • •
“Yang Anyut ”•. Re brush-turkey. In
Borneo and neighboring islands there is an allied
megapode which makes mounds 40ft. in diameter
and sft. high. Numerous hens to each heap, but
only one cock. Nests generally close to sea. Eggs
laid in pairs or threes, those which are to produce
male birds being much longer than others. Incu-
bation, 40 days. Young birds burrow out them-
selves, are fully fledged and able to fly an hour
after. Males congregate at breeding time, and
fight till only one is left in. He is the parent of
every brood in that heap, and the defeated ones
mope about by themselves.

“H. 8.”: During’BB drought was fixing
flood-gates in a back creek anticipating a fresh
from the Condamine, and noticed, although no
local indications of a rise, that the ants, spiders
and other insects were shifting camp, carrying '
larvae, etc., up the banks. The water did not pass
that spot until 12 hours later. How did they get
their “ wire ” ?

“E.S.S.” : In the dark scrubs on the
Richmond River one occasionally comes upon a
nest resembling that of the magpie. But it is not
occupied by any bird, though it may have been
built by one. It is the nest of the pretty little red
possum. Doubled up into a ball, he sleeps there
the day out, and goes off with the first shadows
for a taste of whatever sweets the night may bring
him. Does that red ’possum build its own nest,
or does it annex that of a bird ?
LAST STAGE OF DARLING PEA.—(By a Darling Amateur.)
“ Kati-Kati ” : Re beauty or otherwise of
“faded” Australian landscape and vivid green
English ditto. The principal factor in the forma-
tion of a taste for particular style of scenery is the
sort of country in which one’s courting is done,
The English James takes his Sarah through lanes
of bright green grass, overshadowed by trees so
full of color that it seems to drip from the tips of
the leaves, and flanked by high hedges gay with
flowers, and ever after that style is beautiful to
him. The Scotch Alexawnder strolls with his
Jean surrounded by hills purple with heather,
and henceforth no other aspect is so pleasing. The
Australian Billjim swings himself on to his 30s.
hors© and is carried through dull greys of blue-
gums to his best girl who has developed no
national name yet, and together they dream love’s
young dream amid the faded hues of an Aus-
tralian landscape, and in time beauty of landscape
will mean to him a harmony of color in the lowest
key. The present Australian predilection for the
“crude” coloring of an English landscape is an
inherited liking developed by the courting of
hundreds of generations of ancestors under the
greenwood tree.

0. N. Bayertz writes from Dunedin about
an aboriginal grievance:—
“ Atcheringa ” writes in Thr Bulletin, 19/11/98, that
in Cook’s voyages somewhere there occurs a story of
some Maoris who, when asked for the whereabouts of
any strange Itnds, pointed apparently in the direction of
Australia, anti called out “Ulimaroa.” Cook may have
written this story, though I can’t find the original any-
where, hut it is quite certain that no Maori ever pro-
nounced the word. There is no “1” in Maori. They
might have said “ Urimaroa.” “Uri ” means(l) offspring,
descendants; (2) race. There is no Maori word “ maroa,”
but there are two separate words—(l) “ ma,” meaning
white; (2) “ ma,” the prep., for, by, or by way of; and the
conj. and, and “ roa,” meaning long, tall, or slow—taking
a long time. I can't, however, get any reasonable mean-
ing from the word. Cook had no ear for language.
Phonetic inaccuracies abound in his attempts to tran-
scribe Maori words. In Kingston’s “ Captain Cook’s
Voyages,” p. 79, a chief called “Toiava” is mentioned.
There is no “v” in Maori. On page 91, “ Eaheinou-
mauwe ” is given as the name of the North Island,
instead of “ Aotearoa ” pronounced ah-o-tay-ah-r6a. On
the same page “ Tovy Poenammoo ” is given instead of
“ Waipounamu " for the South Island—“ Wai,” water;
“ pounamu,” greenstone. (Perhaps the vin “ Tovy ”is a
misprint for 6.) The Federal Capitalists will probably
get a better name for their city than the meaningless and
unintelligible “ Ulimaroa.” Other great explorers, by
the way, as well as Cook have raised “1” in Maori. Paul
Blouet thus concludes a chapter on Maoriland in his
“ Johnßull and Co." : “I fancy I hear again their (the
Maoris’) melodious Mokololulu (linger longer lulu) Kiri-
latata, Waramanatihipu —“ Good-bye 1 Ta-ta." Oaoo-
phonous drivel 1 [But it isn’t caoophonous.—K.B.J

Stonehenge (0.Q.), a little nearer to Sheol
than most other places, is situated on a gravelly
ridge adjacent to a puddle-hole known to map-
makers as the Thompson River. Old - time
travellers often left the town bootless and couldn’t
account for it. Once in the long ago writer struck
the gravel-heap shod with a pair of Callaghan’s
best and a oouple of extra pairs in the bag. Three
days later departed in a canvas shoe belonging to
mine host and a dancing-pump left behind by a
one-legged showman. Sharp gravel ridges with
the thermometer something over 150° in the sun
(there’s no shade) are not conducive to long-lived
foot-wear. I hereby enter Stonehenge, near
Sheol, in Queensland, in the new competition for
the boot-destroyingest (new adjective) place in
Australia.

“ H.M.T. ” : One of the most peculiar pro-
ducts of W.A. is the paper-bark tree. As far as
the writer knows it is commercially valueless, bub
the bark indicates the nucleus of a future industry.
Tt is about an inch thick and will split into 10,000
sheets easily, each of which can be made to receive
a legible impression. The tree
grows in swampy land or on
the edge of billabongs, and
its waving bark streamers are
weird companions in the
moonlight.

wall to doze. Then we found the pepper-dredge

P.A. : No wonder Rougemonfc lied well.
The appreciative credulity of his dupes would
have demoralised George Washington. No
one but cockneys of the Newnes stamp would
have treated him seriously after his statement
that while swimming he saw natives ten miles off
running about the beach—this, too, after receiving
a smack from the tail of a 150 ft. whale. More-
over the man who jumped on the back of an alli-
gator would never see London again, for there is
nothing but electricity to compare with the
appalling suddenness of the ’gator’s grab. I once
prodded a baby croc, to see if he was asleep, and
I shall never forget the lightning flash of his jaw
as he tore away the stick from my hand, giving
me a shock like the kick of a mule.

The chairman of a Central Queensland
Divisional Board recently told the tale of how the
local niggers outwitted him for a while. Chair-
man was in the habit of receiving and paying for
kangaroo-scalps, which he threw (by way of keep-
ing them till properly tallied) on the top of a high
galvanised-iron roof—the strangest place to keep
scalps on record. The aboriginals of the district
showed most commendable perseverance in hunt-
ing down the local marsupials. Day after day
they kept bringing in fresh trophies and receiving
pay for the same. One night the boardsman
aforesaid happened to pay a visit to the shed
where his hard-bought scalps were reposing. To
his surprise he noticed a ladder fixed against the
roof—and a nigger on the ladder. That chair-
man calculates he bought every one of those
scalps 20 times over. A dose of small shot on the
aboriginal shanks settled the new native industry,
and a good Christian darkey from the mission-
station retired into private life for & while.

Dear Bulletin.— Strolling through Syd-
ney Botanic Gardens saw on seat man evidently
from out-back, so we talked learnedly about the
drought, the relative merits of imported bulls and
native-born cows, the crops, &c. Talked cattle
and drought to him till we became great friends,
and strolled up town. Country friend invited mo
to “have one.” He was so guileless and simple
and brown that by this time I had ceased to
wonder at the ease with which his tribe are cleaned
out by the Sydney spieler. Had one accordingly,
and when I awoke yokel had gone; also every-
thing I had about me.— Smarty.

V“6 x 8 ’’ discourses on the never-ending
Out-back question of which we are all now pretty
tired :
He “J. Jinsrle’s ” attack on Lawson. I know the N.S.W.
hack-country as well as any man living-. I’ve tramped it
for years, and know every hut, stump, short-cut, rabbit-
trap, and shanty along the tracks Henry Lawson has
truveiled, and, bar “the 20,000 cattle,” he exaggerates
little. During a specially hot and dry season the “coun-
try out beyond the Darling, the land of the living death,”
is strewn with corpses. In ‘96 heat-wave there was
hardly a station between Bourke and Wilcannia, or a
track between the Darling and the N.YV. border, that
d n t rccoid the find of a dead swagman or two ! On big
•Salisbury Downs run (dry corner) were found no less than
seven swags, the owners of which innet have perished. I
myself helped to bury three victims of that heat-wave.
As to shrivelled leather and bridle-reins, the man Lawson
refers to is the chap who j s riding through the country
seeking s!.earing or other work, and not able to get it
because of wage-cutting blackleg cockeys, retrenching
syndicates, arid fat absentees ! lie has no means of look-
ing after his gear : hardly able to look after bis stomach !
In'96, after the big strike, met scores of such unfortu-
nates on skeletons of horses with hay-hand bridles and
bag saddles ! Lawson is no! hing if not sympathetic. Ho
sp'-uks and feels for the wretch who is out battling, and
is kept out by servile, “smoodging" station “lifers!”
Permanent station work, even at poor pay, is bard to get,
uml to keep it demands a fearful amount of cringing ami
crawling nowadays ! Lawson's htishmen aren't built that
way. and prefer even the hellish dry country to that!
As to Lawson's accuracy, there isit t a sentiment, a type,or
character you meet that you can’t find in “ While the Bily
Boils," or ‘ln the Days When the World Was Wide.” As
for “change of hell,” no words could express it better.
The dry country is a hell, and cooky-shearers, station-
crawlers. and snake-juice vendors are its devils !

“Werrimoe” : One day, wliilo tailing
hoifors on Deep Creek station (Casino, N.S.W.),
I esatne upon a very large carpet-snake, coiled up
in the long grass. I was struck with the peculiar
shape of the spotted mass, which resembled a bee-
hive. On being disturbed, it slowly uncoiled itself
and crawled away, revealing a neatly-made grass
nest, full of eggs. Toese eggs, which had a soft,
tough skin, and were about the size of a walnut,
were all glued together in one solid mass. I could
not ascertain the exact number, but I should think
there were considerably over a hundred. I pulled
one off and broke it. It contained a young snake
in process of formation. This was proof enough
that the carpet-snake hatches its eggs in the same
manner as a hen.

The tale oi a Big Set ub dog. Tony Bul-
lock, of Twowharves, had the best triok-dog on
the three rivers. A farmer from the Clarence was
greatly taken with the dog’s performance in the
bar of the Canecutter’s Curse Hotel. One old
trick delighted the farmer wildly. Tony placed a
bit of meat on Sir Joseph’s nose. “ Sirjoe ” (as
we called him for short) sat motionless. Said
Tony: “Ready!” Not a canine eyelash quivered.
“ Present! ! ” Not even a gleam of carnal joy in
the dog’s eyes. “Fire!!!” Sirjoe tossed the
hunk of old-team btillook into the air and caught
it in his mouth as it fell. The Clarence farmer
bought Sirjoe, and took him to Grafton, where he
put the dog through his tricks. “Now I’ll show
ya his great trick. Gi's a bit a meat.” (A bit of
the sirioin was produced and placed on Sir joe’s
snout.) “ Now, chaps, lam proud o’this trick.
It’s as good as ever ya seo inna circus. ‘ Ready ! ’
See, not a move. ‘ Present! 1 ’ Twig ? Sirjoe’s
as still as death. ‘ Shoot! !! ’ ” But the dog
stirred ‘not. “ Shoot! !! ” the farmer roared.
“ See, chaps, notta move,” said a malioious wag.
“ Oh ! sha-sha-shoot, you wretch ! ” screamed the
showman. But he shot not worth a cent. And
he might have squatted there rigidly till the meat-
bit went gamey ; but for the quickness of a Lis-
more Rifle Reserve man, who shouted “Ready!
Present! Fi-yah 111” And the dog fired.

“ Bargo ” : The man from out-back is the
one most impressed by the vitality-restoring advt.
The town-man, having his tuppenny’bus to run'
after, seldom reads them, but the “ bushy,”
receiving One paper a week, pores diligently
through every line. Recently up South, a cocky,
reading from a weekly paper a nervous-cure advt.,
told trie that the advertiser had altered two lines
of it. I could hardly believe his memory till he
brought me the previous week’s papers, and sure
enough the alteration in the advt. had taken
place. From constant weekly wade, he had the
advt., “ Specks before the eyes, lassitude, torpor,”
etc., completely off by heart.

Kur or Kaadaitcha-luma: or “The
Walking About of a Bad Man.”
The Kandaitoh is an evil omen, a bad spirit, a
noarro (no good) fellow ; a murderer, and hence a
wearer of the strange shoes bearing above name.
These are made of emu-feathers and human hair,
glued together with human blood. The soles are
thick, soft, and yielding, and are oval in form, toe
and heel being shaped alike. They leave hardly
any tvaek on any kind of ground, but on a firm
surface it is Quite impossible to follow their trail.
They have nothing in common with the Indian
mocassin, which is one piece of hide made into a
shoe for the protection of the foot. The wearer
of the Kaadaitcha foot-gear has around his waist
a hundred folds of human hair twined between
which and his skin is hidden a stone of magical
properties ; sometimes it is hidden in the hair of
his head and occasionally carried in the arm-
f»it. Of the use of these strange shoes very
ittle is known ; but it is a fact that the
murderers generally hunt in couples ; the evil
man calling to his assistance the tribal medi-
cine-man or sorcerer. On no account will
they proceed without wearing the band round
their body (made from the hair of some famous
warrior), capable of producing a dpuble effect of
valour to themselves and fear amongst their
enemies. The shoes, as shown in illustration, are
slightly convex at the sole and flat on top, not
unlike Chinese bathing-shoes. They are still to
be found, and the practice still prevails in Central
Northern Australia. It is the man that is the
Kaadaitcha, though the shoes are now generally
known by tnat name. The illustration snows the
sole of the shoe.
The medicine man and the other Kaadaitchi
hide about the neighborhood of the camp in which
resides the intended victim until a favorable op-
portunity occurs to steal upon him and kill him.
Then a hasty retreat is made, as they know the
avenging party will soon be out after blood for
blood, as is the custom of all the aborigines.—
Haeby Stookdale.

Malle ’: Re “ Bush Notion’s ” recent letter
on N.S.W. teachers’ grievances. Was lately In a Northern
town, and had occasion to visit the post-office. It is an
alleged cottage, bigger than a dog-kennel hut not quite
so large as a decent horse-box, and was probably painted
in the early days of Australian exploration. The depart-
ment occupies the best part of the premises, the balance
boing the postmaster’s residence. Knowing of the rent
charge, 1 hunted up the latest departmental blue-hook to
ascertain the amount deducted from this officer, and was
flabbergasted to find that Govt, pays £26 per annum
for the shanty, and charges the P.M. £3O for
his portion ! Further search reveals two other
cases where officers are charged a higher rent
than, and several where they are charged an
equal rent to, that paid by department. In all these cases
it must be understood that Govt, occupies the best por-
tion of building, and the back premises are common to
everyone employed in the office, therefore the postmaster
has very little domestio privacy. Moreover, those who
continued in the superannuation fund have to part up
their 4 per cent, on their rent allowance, whioh is thus
treated as solary ! When the Govt, is reduced to this sort
of sweating, no wonder lteid wants more revenue.

Vic. Customs department announces a stern
determination to prosecute licensed victuallers who may
be found carrying on the business of a wine-and-spiri't
merchant. It is a contravention of the Act for any publi-
can to sell bulk quantities of liquors through travellers.
If Govt, gave a publican liberty to do a wholesale trade it
would put him in a position to buy his liquors cheaper,
keep large quantities maturing in bond, and supply cus-
tomers over the bar with better poison than they get at
present. And this at the expense of the blessed middle-
man wine-merchant! The Act loves the merchant middle-
man. Hasn’t James Service been Premier, and wasn’t
G. D. Carter the author of a famous Budget, and are not
they and other pillars of the Vic. State interested in keep-
ing the Act up to the mark ? Already the big grocer who
Imports liquors from ’Ome is beginning to outrage the
feelings of the “recognised" wine-and-spirit merchant,
and an Act to prevent the grocer from pushing his trade
(with a olause prohibiting whisky importation by private
persons) ought to be passed immediately.
• • • •
A business-like idea, in which the N.S.W.
Government ought to copy Victorian and W.A. legisla-
tion, is in the compulsory registration of firms. The idea
is simply to compel firms to register the names of the
people who run tne show, so that there is some tangible
person to serve the summons on. As matters stand, it
often happens that Smith, calling himself Smith and Co.,
works up a business and a reputation which is good for
much credit. Then he sells out to Mosenberg and Israel-
stein, who keep the honored name of Smith still above
the door, and sign it on cheques, and say nothing about
the change. Also, it often happens that Jones and Co.
really consists of six men, none of whom, except Jones
himself, who has nothing, can be proved to be in the show
when the trouble comes; while Cow Fat in business is
almost always Ho Oat in private life, and Israeloff, money-
lender, is as often as not really Aaronstein, or Cohenloup,
or MacDonald, or Duncan Abrahams.

A Sydneyite wanted to keep a shop he was just
.eaving vacant for a week so that he could take all his
custom to new premises opposite ere anyone could open
opposition. Didn’t want to pay week’s rent to keep old
shop though, so got friend to offer landlord 2s. 6d. a week
more them he himself was paying. Offer immediately
accepted, and keys handed over on departure of previous
tenant. Landlord has not since seen new tenant, and
had to burst in the doors at the expiration of the
week.

as can be ascertained, but the carelessness and utter in-
competence of the managers of many cycling depbts
must stand as records. In one agency, it has been
found that no less than 100 machines—each capable
of being retailed at from £2O to £3o—have absolutely dis-
appeared from stock, and in scarcely any of the shops
which have “gone out" in the smash is the complete
stock to be found. The reason of this is the lax manner
In which bikes were tossed about in the good times of two
years back. Any casual friend of the management could
enter a cycle agency then, and borrow a machine for a
few days ; and whether he ever returned the jigger it was
evidently nobody’s business to inquire. Hundreds of
machines have vanished into the evsiglceit from different
Australian cycle-shops since 1896.
» » * *
Minister Outtrim, after a recent month’s ex-
perience of Sydney telephone management, lately re-
turned to Melbourne with the gallful assertion that the
Southern system is quite satisfactory by comparison.
There is no arguing with an official who refuses to be con-
vinced. Visitors from Sydney are unanimous - in their
bad language re the crossing of wires and general ineffi-
ciency of the Melb. ’phone service. As for the local resi-
dents, who pay £9 a year for being “ laid on ’’ to this
scandalous fraud, they know that the telephone couldn’t
be so much more popular in Sydney if its subscribers
there had not their lines cast in pleasanter places. If
they believed Outtrim’s statement, and had no hope
of things improving, they would give the tele-
phone up as a costly delusion and annoyance. Why
not a mass meeting of' subscribers to protest against any
further continuance of Duffy, P.M.G., in the billet where
he has proved himself a public nuisance?
• * * *
The delivery agent of a certain time-payment
Australasian pictorial publication one day informed Mrs.
Dooley that on the following morn full numbers thereof
would be delivered to her as per agreement and that she
would have to pay £l2 10s. Dooley being informed of the
trouble when he returned from toil, thought hard until
he evolved a “plan of campaign.” Next morning,
“ Dooley’s ’’ front parlor foretold an auction sale. Articles
grouped in numbered lots, and so forth, with Dooley in
the midst minus hat and coat. Presently the agent ar-
rived with—“ I’ve brought the book.” “ What book? Is
It wan ye’re goin’ to put into the sale?” “ Sale ! ” said
the agent, hastily picking his parcel off the table again.
“Where is Mrs. Dooley?" “Divil a one of me knows.
She left here yesterday. I’m the bailiff in possession and
I wouldn't advise ye to lave anny property here. Shure
thim divils of landlords ’ud sell the coat off yer back if
they got the chance ! ” Then the agent went sadly away.
* • • •
The humors of the hash-house. Restaurant-
keeping is a profitable business : people must eat. Yet
they do not always “pay at the desk,” as directed by
notice—they prefer to eat to-day and pay to-morrow—or
never. The restaurateur has no remedy either, except to
summon them. It’s a dobt, the law says—and the law
knows. The man or woman of nerve will seize the
“ balancer's ’’ hat. One woman in Melbourne is popularly
supposed to have a roomful of hats taken from default-
ing diners as collateral security. During the last few
hard years “ scaling ’’ or “balancing” has become a fine
art among the “crook" pugs and the vast multitude of
semi-well-dressed men who live in or on the streets, race-
courses and hotels. Only a week or two ago, in Pitt-
street, Sydney, two “ stiffs ” who wished to (line worked
a “ balancing-joint," and “ scaled ” a restaurant-keeper.
They accosted an elderly man in a casual way, and “ sup-
posed he could do with a feed." He could. “ Well, come
on then," and in the trio went and had their soup, meat,
pudding, salad, tea and toast, at 6d: a time. They hur-
ried, and finished beforo Hie old man, and the spokesman
told him they were going out, but that ho need not
hurry—they would paj- for him, and he could stand up so
that the cash-taker would know him. At the door, they
explained that the old man would pay, and he, in response
to their pointing, stood up and nodded. The cash-taker
was satisfied, and the two operators cleared. When the
old fellow turned up at the pay-oountor with his explana-
tion, ho was scoffed at and abused, and Anally, having no
money, he was thrown out on the street, where he curt jd
his philanthropic friends, and explained the story to an
amused crowd.

Melbourne and suburban J.P.’s have not re-
covered from their antipathy for Vic. Factories Act, and
people who come before them on certain charges “ work”
on this weakness. If defendant pleads that he can’t pay,
because he is thrown out of work by the operations of the
abhorred law, which stops him from getting piece-work
at Chinese rates as ho aid formerly, the bench over Hows
with righteous indignation, and the pointer gets off
cheaply or walks out “on the nod.” Up-to-date law and
out-of-date administration—new wine and old skins.

“ Bargo ”: Know a tenant who shifts in a
hurry, and has a bed of his own making, which, by the
pulling of a string, falls instantly to pieces. Then wife
takes chair and box ; hubby gathers up the patent bed-
stead—and off!
* • • •
An enterprising Cornstalk thinks of trying the
Yankee trout-farming system in ponds scooped out along-
side a fast-running, cool stream In the Blue Mountains.
The troutlets are to be carefully reared and fed in artificial
preserves, from which, when a fish of any desired weight
is wanted, it can be simply lifted out with a hand-net.
In America, areas of water are made into fish-farms,
wherein an incredible amount of trout are stocked. They
grow rapidly, and provide big divs. for the “ farmers.”
* • • *
A Vic. country mounted policeman had a debt-
warrant to serve on himself, t’other day. He served it,
endorsed it nulla bona, and returned it. Unfortunately,
it was made out for, say, John Jones, “ mounted con-
stable stationed at,” say Crow Flat; and now there is a
departmental “How's this?”
• > * •
An 18-year-old Sydney clerk, going into a city
bank recently, picked up a roll of notes and cheques
totalling £l2O, of which £75 was notes. The property
was easily identified as belonging to a wool Ann, and
finder hied him to their office, with visions of substantial
reward. He interviewed one of the heads of the firm,
who received him haw-hawly, and discredited his state-
ment of where he found the lucre. “ They did not bank
there, so how could it be lost there?” But inquiry
Bhowed that their clerk had called there on the way to
the firm’s bank. Result, dismissal of delinquent, and
barely civil acknowledgement, to finder, who, on reporting
the matter to his own employers, was advised to apply
for his cab-fare. He did, and received—half-a-erown.

“ Yang Anyut ” : Re Chinese power of
dying at will. When I was living, as a boy, outside
the concession at Shanghai, near by lived a China-
man who used to have frequent “ barneys with
his elderly sister-in-law, and once, after a particu-
larly hair-raising row, the lady swore to be
revenged, and took a seat at John’s front-door.
That evening there was much wailing in the
village over the old girl’s death, and I naturally
expected dire punishment to overtake John, but,
upon seeking information from my Chinese nurse,
1 found that the victim had merely sat on her
relative’s door step with the intention of dying
there to spite him. She actually had died there,
too, and the revenge would come in the under-
taker’s little bill, for, according to Chinese custom,
John would be compelled to give his sister-in-law
a gorgeous 16-s'orey funeral, the expense of which
would cripple him for many moons to come.
Adelaide people are seriously thinking,
now that Buxton has resigned, of petitioning the
'Ome authorities to send out something worldly
and uproarious for a change. It is surely up to
S.A, to have a sporting peer theatrically inclined,
with a big cheok suit and eloquent flow of bad
language. Buxtoa always wore sombre black,
never went to the races or the theatre, and habitu-
ally looked as solemn as a boiled owl belonging to
a serious Noncomformist family. What is now
wanted is a dook with ample funds and a chronio
thirst, and, if possible, it would be advantageous
to have a person with a disgraceful scandal
attached to his name. Things have come to such
a pass in the gloomful city that all its citizens,
from Kingston downwards, are either pious or
suspected to be so, and, unless something is done,
there will be a crisis.
MICK SIMMON

When the Liver Goes Wrong
the Whole System Suffers.
A Bad Case. A Good Cure.
(ftom At "Australian Christian World*
July Ist, 1898.)
(BT OU* OWW REPORT HR.)
•*Out of pure gratitude for my deliveranoe
from a most serious and painful complica-
tion of complaints, I am willing to
give you the whole afternoon if you wish
it 1” answered Mrs. Cole, of 2 Purves-street,
Forest Lodge, Sydney, to our reporter
on his requesting an interview as to
that lady’s case. “ The whole trouble,”
said Mrs. Cole, in answer to further ques-
tions on the subject, “arose, I firmly believe,
from the poison that got into my system
through a deranged liver. Soon after that
organ got out of order and started to work
irregularly my appetite began to get very
fickle and changeable. Without cause or
reason I turned against delicacies which
were formerly favourite dishes of mine, and
sometimes I sat down to meal after meal
and rose again without being able to so
much as swallow a single mouthful. In the
morning I invariably had a most unpleasant
acrid taste in the mouth, together with a
dirty coated tongue, and, as time wore on
(for I must tell you I was seriously ill for
over a year), my liver trouble got worse and
worse. When I woke in the morning I
felt giddy, stupid, and dazed, and often
made vain efforts to get out of bed, being
forced to lie back again on the pillow to
collect enough strength before finally suc-
ceeding. At nights I was unable to sleep,
and I should lie wide awake restlessly
turning about and counting the weary hours
as they wore away, till the dawn began to
break, when I usually fell into a sort of
stupor,—it was not a proper sleep,—and I
woke from that half dazed as I have
just described. I became very, very thin,
not like I am now (Mrs. Cole has now a
particularly healthy appearance, with a
clear eye and that clear freshness of
complexion which is never seen with a
disordered liver), and, as for my complexion
—well, sallow was not the word for it. It
was as yellow as if I was suffering from
fellow jaundice.” •
“All this must have had a very depressing
effect upon your spirits i”
i“ It did indeed, as anyone might expect,”
answered Mrs. Cole, “ though you have not
heard the half of my bad symptoms yet. I
was most low spirited and dull and
crotchety as well. I couldn't bear the
slightest noise, and the smallest trifles
would have a most distressing effect upon
my nerves. Nothing could interest me. I
didn’t care even to hear the hews of the
day, and the trifling cares of domestic life
drove me into a state of nervous irritation.
I was always languid and weary. I went to
bed every night as fatigued as though I had
done some excessive labour, when as a
matter of fact I had done nothing; but I
could not sleep, and I rose in the morning
feeling more tired, weary, and lifeless than
I did the night before. I suffered from
terrible headaches, and I vomited almost
daily. More than once, after I had managed
to walk a short distance to my mother’s
house, I ha ve had to fling myself upon the
grass, one of these sick fits attacking me and
causing me to vomit ten ibly, and being so
giddy that I positively couldn’t stand. Then
palpitation of the heart often came on
suddenly, and I know of nothing more dis-
tressing and terrifying whilst the violence
of the fit was on than that.”
“ You suffered from other complications V*
“ Yes, I regret to say, after a time pain-
ful neuralgia attacked me. The attacks
came on at short and regular intervals. I
should first feel a peculiar tickling sensa-
tion in my face and jaws and then a sudden
and awful whirling sensation over all my
face and head, just like the mad rushing of
a big red hot wheel in my head. Ido not
think there are w'ords in the dictionary
capable of expressing the exquisite agopy I
suffered whilst these periodical attacks
lasted. Many doctors attended me, but
unfortunately for me they were not able to
either give me relief or effect a cure,
although I faithfully followed the treat-
ment they recommended and took the
medicines they prescribed. Then my
rheumatism ”
“ V hat Mrs. Cole, you surprise me, did
„ ou suffer from rheumatism also ?”
41 1 did, very acutely, too. The pain in
my legs was terrible. My feet swelled up
to a prodigious size, and of course it was
impossible to put on a shoe, and worse
than all my knees swelled. I was quite
unable to walk. The pain in my ankles,
my knees, and other joints was unremitting,
and gave me no rest neither by night nor by
day.
“In what way was Clements Tonio
brought to your notice T”
“ Through a little book that was placed
in my hand when things seemed at their
worst. I supposed I had reached the worst.
No medicines that I took did me any good,
nor were the many doctors who attended
me able to alleviate my sufferings. Well,
as I said, someone gave me a book that con-
tained an account of a case that in many
important particulars was precisely similar
to mine. I naturally read it with engross-
ing interest, and, seeing that Clements
Tonic had effected a complete cure for that
patient, and being satisfied as to the
absolute truth of every word of the account
of the patient’s illness and cure by Clements
Tonic, I commenced to use that remedy
myself. Very glad I am, too, that I did.”
“ In what way did it act in your case ?”
“The first thing I noticed, the very
first action of the remedy, was to make
me sleep, and about that there could be no
mistake. Natural sleep came upon me after
taking the remedy as regularly as the night
follows the day, and from the time I started
Clements Tonic my sleep became refreshing
and dreamless. After long weary months
of nearly sleepless nights, only snatches of
sleep being obtained at odd times when the
pain troubled me least, I can hardly express
to you the priceless boon this sleep was to
me. The neuralgic pains in my jaws and
head gradually gave way to Clements Tonic,
and at the same time the swelling in my
knees and ankles became more reduced evei y
day till it went away altogether, and I was
once more enabled to get my shoes on and
to put my feet to the ground without pain.
I was able to eat again without feeling at
all sick afterwards, as I used to, and I have
had no sick fits since. Clements Tonic also
rid me of that low, depressed feeling, and
when I now awoke in the morning I felt
bright and cheerful and once more fib to
undertake my domestic duties, no longer
with a feeling of wearhiess, but with posi-
tive pleasure. One by one all the compli-
cations which beset me were driven out of
my system by Clements Tonic. My head-
aches were a thing of the past and I was
troubled no more with palpitation of the
heart. My friends all noticed the change
for the better in my health and spirits.'
Clements Tonic, in addition to removing the
poison that collects in the blood in conse-
quence of a diseased state of the liver, has
a marvellous effect in toning up the system,
and my natural healthy colour speedily
returned and I commenced to get stout.
It was not very long before I was completely
cured and as well, if not better, than I was
before my illness.”
“You will consent for the proprietor oi
Clements Tonic to publish all this ?”
“ Yes. You can make what use you like
of what I’ve told you, and I shall only be
too happy to answer any enquires, if any
should be made, in regard to my recovery.”

I, Sarah Colr, of 2 Purves-street, Forest hodg-e,
Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales do

read the annexed document, consisting of eleven
folios and consecutively numbered from one to eleven,

illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also
contains my full permission to publish the
same in any way; and I make this solemn
declaration conscientiously believing the same to be
true, and by virtue of the provisions of an
Act made and passed in the ninth year of the

None beats “ White Heather” Blend.
