POLITICAL
POINTS
In. Reid circles there is talk qf the appoint-
or? to N.S.W. Upper House of three ex-
j\l b.L.A. of the Labor-party.
Federation is like an amateur climbing a
greasy pole. Plenty of effort, but no progress,
fteid is the grease on the pole.
“ Melbournian ” : Gillies has lost his im-
pact. . Even the angular M‘lntyre was more
effective than Duncan has been of late. Vic.
Opposition ought to fetch in a leader from the
outside.
Over a score of N.S.W. legislators profess
so deep an interest in Norfolk Island affairs that
they have tapped the Govt, for a free trip there—
free boat, free provisions, free lodgings. Modest
legislators !
Willis, Sleath and Thomas, of N.S.W.
Leg. Assembly, voted against the appointment of
a Royal Commission to enquire into the Suffield
case. Had they voted differently Suffield had had
his enquiry.
Up to date, public money, or rather loan
money, spent in the awful M'Sharry (N.S.W.)
arbitration-case amounts to .£29,433 12s. Id. And
Premier Reid calls this “permanent and repro-
ductive works! ”
John Forrest was eight years Premier and
Treasurer of Westralia on Thursday (29th Dec.).
This is the biggest continuous record in Austra-
lasia. Herbert, who bossed the politics of Queens-
land for 6 years 53 days, comes second in intense
continuity of billet.
N.S. Wales Leg. Council numbers 58
members, of whom 42 have held seats in the
Legislative Assembly. When the people are so
tired of a politician that they won’t put him in
for three years, he is appointed to boss them for
the rest of his natural life.
Samuel Tomkinson, M.L.C., S.A.’s oldest
legislator, finally retires from Parliament next
year. The old man boasts that he has never voted
for a democratic measure, and yet his honesty
and independence have made him one of the most
popular men in S.A. politics.
Sleath, M.L.A., thinks the N.S.W. mili-
tary estimates should be cut down, that there is
altogether too much cash wasted on guns and
swords and tilings. He reckons that a man with
a pen behind his ear and a ruler in hi- fist is armed
enough for all practical purposes.
At the last moment there was a strong
chance of a settlement of the row between S.A.
Leg. Council and the Kingston Government, but
several prominent Tories kept deliberately out of
the way, a course which so angered Kingston that
he declined to listen to compromise. S.A. Tories
now realise that they irreparably blundered in
blocking prorogation.
JACK WORRALL-“The only man in Australia who can score 100 on a bad wicket."
The N.S.W. police estimates, which in
previous years were rushed through the Assembly
m 10 minutes or so, were this time under discus-
sion for hours, the individual grievance of almost
every “cop.” in the force being moaned over at
great length. Reason : The bobby has a vote
now.
An inquisitive member proposes to ask
N.S.W. Govt., when the House meets, how many
paid Govt, positions ex-editor Dr. Garran, now
on the Public Works Committee, has held since
leaving S.M. Herald office on the score of ill-
health? Also, how much per year his income
from the various posts has averaged ?
“It is not what Cicero, or Aristotle, or
Plato, Pythagoras, or Thucydides, or Julius
Caesar said that we want to know,” remarked
King O’Malley during the recent S.A. crisis ;
“All that we are anxious to learn is what Kingston
says. He is the man raised up by God to help
this country to its democratic rights.”
The return of the N.S.W. Aborigines
Protection Board for the year just ended continues
the story of its predecessors. Among full-
bloods and half-castes there were 194 deaths in
the twelve-months, but of these 192 svere
deaths of full-bloods. The genuine black
aborigines decreased by 67 ; half-caste 3
increased by 108. In 1882 the census
gave full-bloods 6540, and half-castes
2379 ; now the former stand at 3230,
and the latter at 3661. It is almost
possible to fix the year of the total
disappearance of the black race in
N.S.W.
In N.S.W. Assembly .re-
cently Geo. Reid was sitting on
back Govt, bench, and Fegan,
who is a most servile barracker
for Yes-no, went over and, kneel-
ing on the front bench, started
a conversation with him. Then
Opposition whip Perry rose and
drew attention to the disorderly
conduct of the hon. member in
“ kneeling before his god in the
chamber while the House was
sitting.” It wasn't much of a
joke, but was better than the
general run of humor which
comes from a Parliament where
Sleath is boss-humorist.
Chief Justice Griffith, a
week after a Q.N. Bank direc-
tors were acquitted, ordered the
jurors in the case to be paid
extra fees, thus raising their
allowances to two guineas a
day. The jury, it is true, ap-
plied for increased fees early in
the trial, and the Judge pro-
mised to consider the applica-
tion, but the increases should
have been granted or refused
before the verdict was returned.
Everybody asks or thinks the
question : Would they have got
the extra pay if the accused direc-
tors had been convicted ? Queensland looks deep
down in the brown abyss of its beer for a reply,
and finds none.
“Mr. S. A. Stephen, M.L.C., of Syd-
ney, who has recently taken up his residence in
London, has joined the London board of the
Australian Mortgage, Land and Finance Co.”
Yet Mr. Stephen retains his membership of
N.S.W. Leg. Council, to which he was appointed
in ’B7, and has perhaps the worst record for atten-
dance of any member of the Upper House. Like
another lawyer-member of the Council, Mr.
Stephen has lived alternately a few months in
London and a few in Sydney.
The Northern Territory electorate, now
more or less represented in the S.A. Parliament
by Oppositionists Solomon and Griffiths, stretches
from Port Darwin to within cooey of Port Augusta;
and the campaigner can mostly rely on strik-
ing one elector every week, if he is a good bush-
man and keeps a-moving. If he isn’t, the chances
are that he will soon become a corpse, with no one
within 50 miles to bury him. The magnificent
Solomon couldn’t, of course, think of overlanding ;
but Griffiths, whose re-election is not so sure, will
probably be forced to prowl over his huge elector-
ate this year with a compass and a waterbag.
There does not seem the remotest possi-
bility of Turner’s Alien Immigration Bill passing
so long as the suave and ancient Cuthbert repre-
sents the Govt, in Vic. Council. Cuthbert is a
perfect lady in his language and manners, but his
post wants something more than that. A man of
fire and eloquence and energy, and with the voice
of a thunderbolt, might scare concessions out of
the 48 Rich Landlords now and then. A person
who pats them with the velvet paw of the ancient
and gentlemanly kitten is of no use whatever.
During the hearing of an action against
Brisbane Tramway Co. for -£lOOO damages, one
witness, a discharged motor-man, said ;
According to the co.’s rules men who were six months
in the service were entitled to 6s. a day. Up to that time
they received ss. a day. His experience was that after
men finished their six months’ service some excuse was
given for their dismissal, and they were sent away, while
fresh men were engaged at 6s. a day.
Five shillings a day ! Wages are as good as that
now in England. Truly this great, bright, sunny
land has slumped suddenly in its early youth.
The indignant scream in N.S.W. Assembly
of Meagher, M-L.A., that he had been accused in
a paper published by an ex-member of the same
House, and allegedly on the word of two present
members, of laying plans for the murder of a
prominent member of the Leg. Council, hardly
created a ripple on the dirty placid waters of
N.S.W. politics. Scarcely anyone even listened
intently to Meagher’s assurances that he never
even dreamt of murdering Salomons, Q.C. Yet
what a storm such an accusation would have
aroused in any Legislature which had a vestige of
self-respect left to it!

As there isn’t enough gum

local person, the Aus-

bright idea—“ ship them
to Queensland.” And to
Queensland, the cesspool

being duly shipped.

the failure of his S.A.

the bitter opposition of
the South Australian
Company, the big land
syndicate, composed en-
tirely of British absen-

valuable freeholds along

structed. This company
was founded by George

“Turncoat Bill”
Copley, the S.A. farmer,
puts both feet into it
more than ever. The
Northern blunderer’s
latest offence was to read
an anonymous letter charging the Labor-members
who recently visited an up-country township with
having their exes, paid by the drought-stricken
ratepayers of the part. And when Copley was
pommelled subsequently from all parts of the
House—the allegation having been instantly re-
futed—not a single member went to his rescue.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
Sydney Municipal Council turns naturally
to the State to make its colossal new markets pay.
Govt, has already taken premises therein for the
Lending Library. A branch P.O. is established
there also, and the Council now desires to lease
the basement for a “national bathroom.”
There are more than twice as many
colored aliens in North Queensland as there are in
the South and centre put together. About nina
out of every twenty men in N.Q. are colored aliens.
Population of the North, 95,837 souls, of whom
(say) 22,000 are adult males. In the same piebald
sub-province there are 16,496 imported browns,
blacks, and yellows. And last year about half
the increase in the North consisted of browns,
blacks, and yellows.
A measure has just passed Q. Assembly
by which all aliens are prohibited from holding
licenses for pearling-boats. There were certain
enthusiasts who wanted a legal enactment to
bring about compulsory sale of boats owned by
aliens, but the Govt, couldn’t see it. Of course
the alien employe goes on just as before— he isn’t
a grievance, inasmuch as he helps to swell the pro-
fits of the white capitalist. Monopoly for the
white capitalist, and Jap competition for the
laborer—that’s the Act.
The Melbourne Tory papers insist on
classing that unionist baker who took a billet in a
non-union shop, and worked the illegal hours
fixed by his boss, and then informed and got the
boss fined JJS, with the detectives who paid sundry
abortionists to do an illegal operation, and then
got them convicted. Yet there really isn’t a
speck of resemblance between the two cases.
The detectives asked their victims to do an
illegal act, and offered them a cash induce-
ment. The unionist baker didn’t ask his boss to
break the law, or pay him to do it, or induce him
in any way. Ho simply went there to find out if
the boss was breaking the law of his own free will
by bidding his employes to work illegal hours, and
discovered him doing it. The difference is just
that between the policeman who finds and arrests
a burglar, and the fizgig who persuades a man to
go burgling with him and then gives him away to
the law.
The Govt, rail-painting Xmas job in
Sydney found temporary work for 2750 unem-
ployed—about three per cent, of the adult male
population of the city ! Considering that this
was a mere casual fragment of the great relief
system it doesn’t look well for the regime of Jolly
and Fat. It is a notable circumstance, by the
way, that N.S.W. pauperism has increased more
rapidly during the glorious Freetrade epoch of
Premier Reid than it ever did before. Between
1889 and 1894—covering the great bank-smash
period—the number of N.S.W. paupers increased
from 44 to 48 per 10,000 inhabitants—an increase
of four per 10,000 in five years. Then in ’95 and
’96 they increased to 54 per 10,000 —an increase of
6 per 10,000 in two years. Coghlan’s figures
(“ Wealth and Progress of N.S.W.” for 1897, page

An ex-Pentridge warder (40 years in the
Penal service) vouches for this :—ln the early
days a certain candidate for warder’s billet, though
satisfactory in every other way, was one-eighth of
an inch under the required height. The day
before his examination he induced a compatriot
(Hibernian, av eoorse) to whack hard with a pot-
stick on the top of his crownpiece. As the examin-
ing official was not a phrenologist, the ingenious
one, being measured to the summit of the bump,
just scraped through.
Fruit in season. In summer she provides
her meat; in winter, finds her store complete.
This is the position the thrifty housewife finds
herself when she takes advantage of the bountiful
supply of fruit we are going to have this season.

“ F.C.” on Melbourne “push” Xmas
festivities: —
Despite last year’s complaints of the giddy goings-on
at the beaches of Port Phillip on holidays, I formed one of
a barrel-full of herrings on Boxing Day-said harrel being
a cattle-truck with canvas awning and forms braced
athwart ship. We meandered gently down to Mor-
dialloe, covering the whole 16 miles in less than an hour
and three-quarters. Bill was there—also ’Lizer; but
somewhat subdued by the presence of a half-dozen extra
constables. Still, Bill had much amusement. He danced,
in an open-sided hall, till he was tired, when a short but
sharp scrap in the scrub adjacent roused him to a sense
of his responsibility and dispelled the temporary effects
of the “sun.” Later on a rash youth threw doubts on
the musical ability of the concertina-wielder and was
promptly thrown out on his ear. Between whiles Bill
cooled his imagination in the shady seclusion of the bar,
whence he would wander back to rest for a while under-
neath the dance-platform—raised some six feet above
ground—where from numerous cracks and knot-holes
he would catch glimpses of ’Lizer’s flying stockings.
'Lizer, on the ’op, mostly closes her eyes or wears a
dreamy expression, and holds herself stiff as a ramrod
from her hips up, but flings her feet round “regardless.”
Bill goes into the business with a tremendous sense of re-
sponsibility, which shows itself in a pained expression of
countenance and a general look as of a lost soul departing
in agony. His lips are mostly sucking a half-smoked
cigar. Occasionally he sings, in ominous key and doleful
tone.
Tired of dancing, casual members of the push with
their donahs strolled along the beach or the pier. A few
went for a sail in one of the many “ yots." “Yotting”
appears to be great sport - when there is only the owner
aboard and the rest of the party don’t know a tiller from
a tea-tray. One such boat was discharging a party at the
pier steps when her bow bumped heavily against the piles.
The pilot or captain yelled to Bill—standing on the fore-
deck—to “ fend off,” which he did to such purpose that
he immediately found himself floundering about in two
feet of water, with a couple of his lady friends. They
took it very well, merely requesting the pilot to consider
himself the offspring of a blind hippopotamus, and the
descendant of a woman of poor character. Then the
ladies strolled along the beach, and sat in the sun, and
took off their stockings, and sun-dried the rest of their
habiliments. Later on they were observed with a num-
ber of their friends indulging in the pleasant game of
“ rollers.” “ Rollers ”is a great game, and a simple. A
lot of boys and girls get together at the top of a sloping
sandbank, lie down simultaneously, and roll to the bot-
tom. The first arrival is the winner, of course. That’s
all there is in it. The spectator takes up a masterly and
strategic position on the right flank and a little to the
rear, and, as in most amusements, the onlooker sees
most of the game—also he appears to have most of the
fun. Mordialloc on Boxing-day is amusing, but Bill as a
tearing terror and a rorty perisher is a fraud and the
worst sort of a “ have.” He’s quite tame, he won't bite,
he won’t even bark unless you interfere with him or his
donah—and who wouldn’t do the same 1

Chief Secretary Peacock (Vic.) being a
bachelor, lavishes his affections upon the brute
creation. His house and grounds at Creswick are
shared by a cold-snouted monkey, five cockatoos,
a peacock, three shell parrots, seven dogs, an emu,
a hutch of guinea-pigs, a laughing jackass, a calf,
a native bear, a black sheep, and various other
fowl and quadrupeds. Most of them are named
after local politicians. The Bulletin has this
information on the worst authority, and.anybody
can rely on it if he pleases.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the Joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CO,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Out the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical bootmaking is unknown,
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price.

Prevention of Seasickness. — Mr. F.
Mitchell, 66 Upper Fort-street, travelling from
Sydney to Brisbane and back, writes on Dec. 5
endorsing the testimony already published as to
the wonderfully efficacy of the new remedy “ Sea-
joi.” He says : “For the first time I was free
from any feeling of sickness. I shall always carry
a bottle with me when travelling by water.”
“ Seajoi ” is sold by Albert Sharp, 257 George-st.,
Sydney ; 2s. 6d. and 4s. 6d.; posted to any colony,
4d. and Bd.*
The lepers at the Queensland lazarette on
Stradbroke Island celebrated Xmas by sailing
across to Cleveland, and having quite a cheerful
drunk at that pleasant watering-place. This is
what they call, a policy of rigid
isolation 1
Sydney Telegraph made a woeful bull
last Tuesday in its efforts to prove that the 20th
century begins at the beginning of 1900 instead of
1901: —
On January 1 Christendom registered its 1899th birth-
day. On the Ist of next January its 1900th birthday will
have been attained. On the New Year’s Day following it
will have reached its 1901st birthday. When a man has
lived to count 100 birthdays, he is usually said to be a
century old. Whether pedantry requires a world to have
101 annual birthdays to its century is a question for
pedants to decide.
But, then, Christendom, or rather the Christian
era, only registered its first birthday on Ist
January, 2, so Ist January, 1899, is its 1898th
birthday. Tho controversy about the beginning
of the 20th century will drive somebody into a
lunatic asylum yet.
The height (or depth) ot irony! The
Bulletin scissors from the Band of Mercy and
Humane Journal of N.S.W. some remarks made
by the late Justice-Minister Gould in moving the
report of the “ Woman’s Society for Prevention
of Cruelty to Animals, N.S.W.” (Gould was the
callous attorney who persistently refused to fjrant
Suffield an enquiry into his case, though his de-
partment had such a doubt about it that Suffield
was several times offered release if he would
petition for mercy):—
Mr. Gould, in rising to move the adoption of the report,
said that it gave him very great pleasure to again be pre-
sent at the annual meeting of the society, and to discern
from the report that the operations of the society during
the past 12 months showed progress, and that its humane
work had not been neglected (!). It was perhaps true, as
had been stated by their chairman, that when he was a
boy, boys too often took an apparently intense delight in
torturing animals, but he was lain to believe that it had
not been due to inherent cruelty, but from a spirit of
thoughtlessness, and the absence of those valuable
lessons that the school-children of the present age had
inculcated in their minds by societies of the character of
the Women's S.P.O. A. and kindred associations (!!!). .
He appealed more especially to the young people present,
to do all they could by example, and otherwise to show
their horror and detestation of everything approaching
cruelty to animals, many of whom ministered to the
happiness of mankind (! 1) No one could have failed to
remark upon the many miserable wandering dogs about
the streets of the city, friendless, homeless and wretched,
but how many people paused to consider what eventually
became of those miserable animals when they dis-
appeared ? From time to time they were gathered by the
police and destroyed under a system which, it bad been
said, inflicted unnecessary cruelty. Whether this was
true or r.ot, he did not know, but it was only fair to
remember that the authorities contended that their
method of destruction was r i painless and humane as
could be adopted. Efforts 1. \ liowever, been made by
the society to secure the establishment of a lethal
chamber, similar to that at Battersea. England, for the
painless destruction of these dogs.
And so on. No accounting fot tastes. Some
men sympathise with their fellow-creatures ; others
with mangy dogs.
During the month of last Sept, the New
South Wales Lager Bier Brewing Co. Ltd.
supplied ttie German warship Falke with 6000
quart bottles of Sydney Lager Bier for use on the
voyage to Samoa and Apia. During the vessel s
stay in our harbor 600 gallons of bulk Sydney
Lager were supplied her. Another tribute to tho
excellence of the local lager comes from the Ger-
man warship Buzzard, which ordered 5500 quart)
bottles of Svdney Lager for the return voyage to
Kiel. During this vessel’s stay with us she
received 50 gallons of Sydney bulk Lager daily.
Both ships* crews cannot speak too highly of the
excellence of Sydney Lager. As the firemen,
officers and crews come from the country that has
always been famous for lager production, it is
clear that the Sydney Lager Bier Brewing Co.
deserves all that has been said by every con-
noisseur in favor of its lager. *

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Horseracing in Australia is, to a great extent*
what the newspapers make it. As long- as “ nags ” can be
inn in a rough-and-tumble style unbacked and “no-
where” one day, and “ crack ” performers a day or two
later—without exciting pertinent comment from the
reporters who are supposed to study “ form ” and criticise
jockeyship, so long will owners stretch the glorious un-
certainty of the turf” to ridiculous limits. Strathroy
ran now'here in the Villiers Stakes (six furlongs) on the
first day of the A.J.C. meeting. Certainly the horse had
a very big weight to carry, but considering that he had
earned a big weight by previous performances over short
courses it was natural for the public to expect him to be
fancied’by the stable. Why run an animal of his preten-
sions if it didn’t seem good enough to back him ? Two
davs later Strathroy won the Summer Cup (14 furlongs),
beatin”' Alerloolas and Amberite at a difference of only
3or 41b and proving himself “on paper ” one of the best
Cup horses in Australia. Melb. Argus remarked casu-
allv—“ Strathroy has hitherto been regarded as a sprinter
only but at Randwick he showed that he possesses
stamina as well as speed.” No reference to the fact that
this new champion had performed badly (with a different
jockey up) in the Villiers Stakes.
Take another paragraph from the same issue of
the Argus concerning the principal event at the V.A.T.C.
meeting, on Boxing Day likewise “The connection of
E Davis’s stable had a good win over Dandy Dick in the
Hopetoun Cup. The gelding ran poorly at Williamstown
a week ago, but on Saturday morning he registered a
splendid trial at Caulfield. He was backed at the opening
of the betting at 15 to 1, but at the finish 5 to 1 was the
best price against him.” A singularly pointless par under
the circs.
Handy Dick, aforesaid, ran in a six furlongs
welter race at Williamstown on December 19. He was
not mentioned in the betting, and, as the gentle Argus
scribe puts it, he “ ran poorly.” Are the public to un-
derstand that the stable didn’t know him to be a good
horse at that time, or that he wasn’t “ fit” ? A week
later Dandy Dick is tried (as though he hadn’t been tried
in public at Williamstown !), and he is “fit” enough,
anyway to put, up a fine performance on the track, which
neiformanee he dulv repeats on December 26 in the
Hopetoun Cup race of nine furlongs. The Buli.etin ad-
vises the punting public to turn the case of Dandy Dick
over in their mind, and then ask themselves whether the
Argus paragraph, quoted above, is calculated to check
these sudden “ reversals of form,” or encourage them
D 6 horse-reporters write as vigilant critics on behalf of
the public, or do they merely scribble to suit the people
who, in many cases, take care to give them the whisper
when a good thing is going?
« • * •
There is no “skinner” about the tote. When
nobody is on the winner, the whole amount invested,
minus the usual commission, is divided between the
second and third horses. The race won by Helmsman at
Randwick was a case in point; no one backing him on
one of Sydney’s machines, so the whole amount went to
the other placed horses. When does Mo pay second horse
when nobody backs the winner?
, • * *
The punter who backs “ form ” dropped cash on
Stand Off for the recent sprint races at Randwick. After
his running in Rosehill Stakes followers of form with one
accord supported him for the Villiers and made him
favorite only to see him scratched at the last minute.
Then the cunning brigade decided that the Carrington
was his particular “ dart,” and he w-as made favorite for
that event—to again be scratched about half-an-hour
before the race. Stand Off will give any race lie is sent
after a great shaking, but in future backers will see him
go to the post before they plunge.
• * * •
Playboy’s win in the Second Hurdle Race at
Randwick, with 12st. 41b. up, stamps him as by far the
best hurdler N.S.W. now has. To give such a slippery
customer on the flat as Lord Gresford. nearly 2st. and
beat him in a go up the straight was a big performance.
* * * *
People are so used to Mr. Justin M'Sweeney s
bad luck that his win, with Akarini, of the Carring-
ton Stakes, at Tattersall’s meeting, at Randwick, came
quite as a surprise. The mare looked in tip-top trim, and,
had she belonged to a stable in the winning vein, would
never have started at the price she did. Djm-Djin, the
favorite, did not get a fair run, and was never pro-
minent, and Akarini, coming with a good run at the
rb'ht end, won comfortably from Latem (idiotically
called after his sire’s name, spelt backwards). The
public were no more successful in picking the
winner of Tattersall’s Cup, on the second day.
Merloolas started a strong favorite, and more judiciously
ridden must have about won, but he was made use of too
soon, and Crosslake, at 20 to 1, beat him in the run home.
Percy Fielder, who rode Crosslake, had good luck in
getting the mount. He rode Prince Carbine on Saturday,
and the Prince’s people, not thinking Fielder had got
quite enough out of the horse, gave the mount to Cis
Parker. Fielder then got the mount on Crosslake and
had the satisfaction of beating his field home.
* * * *
A piece of “ringing-in” occurred at Kurra-
jong, N.S.W., Show trot the other day, which was
won bv Shakespeare, a fine-looking 3-year-old stallion by
Era from a Boccaccio mare. One entry had a big start,
and came in first, but was recognised as an imported
horse which, amongst other performances, had won a
free-for-all trot in Sydney ; he was, of course, disqualified.
* * * *
Australasian’s London correspondent writes
of Tod Sloan “ Possibly one of the secrets of his success
is the undemonstrative way in which he finishes. Nothing
can be more absurd than the invariable, but utterly use-
less, practice of swinging the hands round in a circle,
either from left to right, or vice-versa, when finishing ;
and we should like to hear any jockey explain what
possible advantage can arise from this gymnastic exer-
cise.” If the circular flourish of the hands is a ridiculous
trick, what about the average Australian jockey who
finishes with his head turned sideways whilst he labori-
ously whips the nag? The whipping can’t possibly
induce a fully-extended animal to go any faster, and
putting himself in a position to use the “ flail ’’ makes his
weight considerably heavier than if he were sitting well
forward.
• • * *
Despite the attempts of the cycling authorities
to block betting at the recent Sydney carnival, the odds
to any reasonable amount could be had on any race.
Some fairly big wagering was done on M‘Don aid and
Megson, but it was rather strange that while threes-to-
one could be had about Megson on the first night, nothing
better than even money was going on the final night,
although M’Donald had beaten him meanwhile.

“ C.A.P.” : Recently at Eagle Farm (Brisbane)
races fancied two horses for certain race ; backed one on
the tote, and drew the other in a half-crown sweep. Horses
came in Ist and 2nd. Shook hands with myself and
jumped gaily on hat. Horse 1 backed on the tote came
in Ist, blit disqualified for short weight, and tote paid 2nd
horse. Went to sweep-man to lift money for 2nd horse,
but found sweep-man had paid Ist horse past post. How’s
this for hard luck ?

writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent, “and, not
taking into consideration Merman’s great chance for
Manchester Novemher Handicap on 26th, the “ Walers
have taken over £lO,OOO in ‘place money’ since March
last. Now at 7’s, Jersey’s faithful antipod. bears the
stable oats, and good luck to him, for he is an equine
Umsiopogaas. His credits are the Jockey Club Cup,
£450, and a Caesarewitch ‘third.’ Malua’s mare,
his stable companion, has netted over £2OOO in stakes,
and this is the proud record of the 6-year-old Maluma :
the Lewes Spring and Summer Handicaps, Vyner Handi-
cap, and Windsor July Handicap, with three places.
Georgic, very cleverly handled, only tried, it is said,
three, but 1 believe two times, and landed £3500 with
‘firsts’ in the Cambridgeshire and Manchester Prince
Edward Handicap. Form’s Waterbench Plate was equal
to £'265, ex bets. Newhaven—‘ a pig,’and not worth
transplanting,’ according to Allison’s mud-judgment—
won the March Handicap, and seconded Bay Ronald in
the City and Suburban and Epsom Cup. Aurum, Ac-
mena, Survivor, Old Clo’, Uniform, poor old Paris, Reso-
lute, haven’t yet, though some have threatened to, paid
their passages. Of these, Acmena should be put in a cab
and Paris to bed.”

Dear Bulletin. —I have been a client of
“Tatt.’s” sweeps for 15 years, and have waited two
mails for explanation before writing this to you. On
Oct. 25th I sent P. 0.0. for six tickets in “ Final Special
(55.), in Melb. Cup, or “Final” ordinary (55.), which
letter would be delivered to * ‘ Tatt.” on the morning of
Oct. 29th. Two mails arrived here by which reply might
have been sent; but I did not receive tickets till Nov. 20
—nearlv four weeks from above date. Letter stated I
was too - late for Cup, and my money was put in Sandhurst
Cup. The Hobart post-mark on envelope was Nov. 14—
16 days after my letter arrived in Hobart, though tickets
should have been posted a day or two after Oct. 29. I
asked “ Tatt.’s ” manager why my tickets were not posted
for Melb. Cup, or a few days after Oct. 29th for Sandhurst
Cup, instead of lying in Hobart 16 days. I did not get a
reply. Numbers evidently were issued early, as they
were 8000 odd. A friend of mine sent from here on Nov.
2—a week after me—and his reply-letter bore Hobart
eost-mark Nov. 10th—four days before me ; and his num-
ers were 36,000 odd I—T. J. Lumsden, Invercargill, M.L.
• * * *
The attention of “ Tattersall ” is drawn to the
following item from a recent Melbourne Age : “ A syndi-
cate of 11 residents of Corowa drew a certain horse in
Tattersall’s sweep on the Newcastle Cup, and telegraphed
the owner an offer of £5OO to nothing. They received a
reply to the effect that unless they proffered half their
winnings the horse would not start. He was not among
those who faced the barrier.” “ Tattersall ’ now runs
certain consultations —not many, certainly—which are
not drawn till the day of the race. Therefore, in them
there is no chance for the horse-owner to levy on the
drawer of the animal, and the latter, if his horse wins,
gets his prize less the recognised 10 per cent.— not, as is
too frequently the case in other consultations, also less
another 30 or 40 or 50 per cent, to a greedy owner. But,
in view of the many complaints from people who say they
applied for tickets in the new consultation where the
drawer gets all his prize, and that they were put,
against their positive instructions, into the old
one where he is liable to he systematically squeezed
by the horse-owner, the above is worthy of notice.
If the new system is to have a fair show,
there should be consultations, drawn on the day of
the race, upon every important event, and everybody who
applies for tickets in these special consultations should
get them. So far, though The Bulletin has heard of
many complaints from people who say they were put in
the ordinary consultation against their express wish, it
hasn't heard a single individual say that he was put into
the special consultation when he didn’t so desire. Appar-
ently, in every case where no preference was expressed,
and in a number of other cases as well, the applicant was
put into the old style of consultation—the one where the
horse-owner grabs so much of the prize and the drawer
gets so little. And this apparent tendency to stick in
the old bad rut is eminently unfortunate.
• • * *
Dear Bulletin. —Do you not think that “ Tat-
tersall ” would be doing a good thing for the subscribers
if he were to make first, second, and third prizes lower,
and make the drawer of a starter or non-starter receive
the same money? If starters and non-starters received
the same money, then no one would let the owner of a
mere outsider blackmail him under threat that the horse
wouldn’t start without a cash inducement. And, if the
first three horse prizes were only a shade larger than the
starter’s prize, nobody would let even the owner of the
favorite blackmail him much. Why not make the sensa-
tional prize a cash affair, with no horse in it at all?—C.S.

Star Pointer, record 1.59£, the only harness-
horse in the world to break 2min., recently changed
hands in America for £3OOO. John R. Gentry, the next-
best, has a record of 2min. Ojsec. Alex holds the trotting

* • • *
Many Sydney jockeys have taken to the “ Tod ”
Sloan seat, and, to date, have mostly succeeded in getting
badly licked by commonplace riders who don't lie down ;
the only Sloan to score at Randwick was F. Kuhn, on
Gallina, in the Hampden Stakes. Some jocks, blame
Sloan for several of the recent Australian accidents,
which is doubtless correct, as when boys who can only
just stick on sitting up take to doing circus tricks in a
big field, something has to go.
An old hand like J.W. Trumble boyishly con-
tends that “ the five test-matches will mean everything
on the next tour. These, and these only, will be remem-
bered.” Trumble’s personal experience of county cricket
in England should have taught him that the financial
success of an Australian Eleven depends largely upon
their steady and consistent beatings of the ’Ome teams in
various places. In the northern shires particularly
crowds will be immense, or merely “ big,” at the test-
matches, according to whether the visitors have hand-
somely whopped the local representatives, or otherwise.
Even in Australia the test-match results will not mean
anything. Few people hereabouts remember the scores
made by the last Australian Eleven in the test-match
which they won, but fewer have forgotten their ignomini-
ous first innings in their first contest with an M.C.C.
Eleven. Australia’s parting word to the boys should be—
“ Take care of the counties and scratch teams, and the
test-matches will take care of themselves.”
• * * *
“S. Snell ” : Have seen many slow games of
cricket, but never before anything to equal the fearful
weariness of Monday’s play in the last Vic. v. N.S.W.
match. It was a ghastly performance altogether up to
the appearance of Graham late in the day, and there
were long intervals of depression after he appeared.
There were no acts and three murderous intervals, and
the one incident of the day that served to temporarily
moderate the tired feeling that possessed the great crowd
was a spirited cock-fight between two mi nahs, fought to
a finish on the green. A long-suffering people bore the
monotonous shiftlessness of the play in a spirit of meek-
ness, but the long waits filled the push with a hatred
that’ found expression in hoarse cries. It looked
as if a big effort were being made for the fourth
day's “ gate,” and it would perhaps have been
better for Australian cricket if the crowd had rushed the
ground and asserted itself with due emphasis. This would
have called forth much journalistic abuse for “the larrikin
element,” of course, but it would have brought home to
the powers a hazy idea that perhaps, after all, some con-
sideration might be shown for the feelings of the people
who are expected to foot the score. The dullest item of
that deadly dull day was M'Leod's handling of the bat.
He was in for over an hour and a-half, and made 18 run 9
in a pottering, cheerless way. Charles is a clever but not
a spirited batsman, and with good fielding would probably
never score against such bowling as N.S.W. can now send
against him. The success of Worrall and Graham in
Melb. and Jones in Adelaide shows that it is the pluggcr
who hits up scores against the Howells and Nobles. He
sees his chance and seizes it, whilst the accurate bat gets
bowled while “ waiting for a better one.”

“ Albert Trott has formed the extraordinary
idea,” writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent, “of
a winter indoor cricket class. He proposes to rent a
large iron shed near the M.C.C. ground, and to lay a
matting wicnet on which he will bowl at and coach a
select number of ambitious bats throughout the football
months. He has already several prominent willow-
wipers on his list, and the idea has caught favor surpns-
Last Friday wrestlers Dunn and Lucifer met a
second time at Sydney Opera House. Dunn got the first
fall and Lucifer the second, with a unique hold, which
made the audience yell with excitement while Dunn was
being levered over. Then Lucifer retired exhausted,
under medical orders, and the match fell to the burly
Dunn.
• • • *
The M.B.C. meeting at St. Kilda last Saturday
was spoilt by the heat. But the programme was good,
all the cracks competing. The chief interest centred in
Park, until lately the pet of the lily-white amateurs and
onetime secretary of their body—it was his first Melb. go
as a pro. In point of legs. Park proved himself a tip-
topper, but he showed yrreat lack of the head work
needed to place him among Walne, Beauchamp, M‘Don aid
and co. He was beaten not by a great distance but with
great ease by Walne in the mile scratch, but he beat out
Corbett and other good men in fine style in a heat of the
mile handicap. He would, however, have had little
chance had not the bad riding of others let him get
through. In the final he came third behind Barker and
Gargurevitch.
Barker rode well, getting another second behind
Walne in the ten-mile event, and scoring an honorless
third in the mile scratch. This fell to Walne, who rode
like his old self again, proving himself the master of
M‘Donald in the final sprint, after riding a mile which
occupied the marvellous time of 4.16.
The big handicap of the day, crawlsomely
termed the Brassey Stakes, provided a remarkable exhi-
bition of selfishness, firstly on the part of the back-
markers, who all wanted the other man to pace, and,
secondly, on the part of overlander Greenwood, who de-
clined to take his turn with Gibson and Feenane in front,
and deservedly got no nearer to them when they were
respectively first and second.
Mutton, the fast Sydneyite, won one heat in
good style, but in doing so he crossed another rider, and
got rubbed out for the day. The decision not pleasing
Mutton, he made remarks, which cost him a further dis-
qualification of a month.
The particular star of the lily-white-amateur
Vic. Cyclists’ Union, W. L. Park, has enlisted as a pro.
The amateurs, who were as dead as Julius Cffisar, there-
upon notified their willingness to co-operate with the
League, and that body forgivingly accepted the shama-
teur brigade, which hail scorned them when amalgamation
was suggested by the League itself a year ago. The com-
bination of amateur and professional racing will probably
mean the making of the former class, which will now have
first-class trophies put up for races on the same pro-
gramme as the events which send the public into shriek-
ing excitement, and will participate in the huge “ cates ”
which the pro. cracks attract. The public has never
taken a cent’s worth of interest in trophy races, and, as
far as the amalgamation of the two classes is concerned,
it shows the League to be an excellent philanthropist,
but a very had business-man. Its last act amounts to
little more than making itself into a benevolent asylum.
* * •
From accounts to Viand of the Corbett-Sharkey
fight it would seem that Corbett’s ring-days have de-
parted. His perfect science gave him a lead for the first
few rounds, but he soon tired, and in the ninth round
one of his seconds rushed into the ring, and the fight was
awarded to Sharkey, a second entering the ring whilst a
fight is in progress constituting a foul under Queensberry
rules. That it was pre-arranged that the second should
so act in the event of Corbett being bested was so pal-
pable that the referee declared all bets off.
r • • * *
Australian Bob Fitzsimmons, the elongated red-
headed champion bruiser of the world, is engaged in liti-
gation just now. He is suing theproprietor of ttic Gilsey
House, New York, for refusing to supply him with a meal
—said proprietor having, some week or two previously,
intimated, on account of a brawl which took place in his
hotel between those two champion talkers, “ Kid ” M‘Coy
and Jim Corbett, that no pugilists need apply. Bob con-
tends that the action of the Gilsey House people was
“greatly” to his “discredit, humiliation and mortifica-
tion, and to his great and irreparable damage.” The
balm Fitz. seeks is a trifle of 60,000d015.
* * • •
From far-off Capetown comes news that Jack
Valentine, erstwhile champion amateur boxer of Aus-
tralia, was beaten in a contest for the championship of
South Africa by a Dutchman named Holloway after 20
rounds of very severe work, throughout the whole of
which —right up to a moment or two before the knock-
out—the Sydneyite showed best. Jack is said to be
making plenty money at Capetown with a boxing-hall
and school.
* • * •
The long-distance jawing-mateh between Jim
Corbett and “Kid” M’Coy came to an end recently at
the Gilsey House, New York, and red war took its place.
Corbett began pleasantries by calling M’Coy “Monkey.”
M’Coy said Corbett was a “ Cur.” This started an argu-
ment in which both reflections were hurled back in the
teeth of the respective calumniators. Hands were put
up. M’Coy promptly found Corbett’s jaw with a half-
arm uppercut. Corbett retaliated by leaving his mark
on M’Cov’s cheek, and M’Coy plumped another fist into
the ex-champion’s startled and somewhat broken-up
features. At this critical moment bystanders took a
hand and separated the raging pugs. This little affair
settles one vital question as important in its way as the
much-disputed championship. Corbett has long been
known as “Gentleman Jim ’’—even before he proved his
right to the title by spitting in Fitzsimmons’ face. M’Coy,
a few months since, published “a card” stating that he
was an American gentleman “and no mistake, and
pointed with pride to the fact that he could trace his
ancestors in gentility back to 1667. And now the little
affair at Gilsey House proves that they are both gentle-
men.
• * • •
To so low an ebb has the noble art fallen in
Melb. of late that recently the suburbs 'have witnessed
the spectacle of “ champions ” getting into a street alter-
cation for the sole purpose of giving the boxing booth
proprietor who employs them the opportunity of calling
on them to •• come inside and have it out and don’t waste
your science in a rough and tumble.” The crowd of
spectators which previously looked askance at paying
their sixpences for an exhibition spar now fight for places
to witness a real blood-and-fire go.

The bitter cry of a tongue-lashed husband
made itself heard in Melb. Divorce Court
lately. Chief Justice Madden, having duly de-
dared that the last male respondent on the list
was a “ monster, ** likewise “ a filthy and detest-
able beast,” &e., went on to mention that a pre-
vious respondent, who had been summed up by
the learned divorcer as a fiend incarnate, or words
to that effect, was now writing to protest against
his Honor’s abusive language. It had deprived
him of his means of livelihood, and he felt it all
the more—said this aggrieved person —for the
reason that it was unfair abuse, which
would never have been uttered but for
Madden’s ignorance of the other side of
the case. In excuse for his reckless epithets the
Chief Justioe pleaded that a male respondent
must expect to get held up to execration if he
doesn’t enter an appearance at the court and dis-
count his wife’s “facts.” Sonah, on his own
showing, is always ready to believe a woman’s
statement, provided it is “clear.” Faith in the
truthfulness of the fair sex, together with his ten-
dency to bemoan Man’s inhumanity to Woman,
amounts to “ rats in the garret ” with his wigged
Waxellency. About 50 husbands already have
some oause for complaint. The Bulletin will
pass the office guitar to one of these degraded
miscreants :—

The “ beautiful Princess May ” of a few
years ago seems to have settled down into “ the
Duke of York’s rather plain wife,” and a Yankee
paper boldly alleges that York has fallen in love
with the Princess of Pless. The Princess is a
daughter of the old-time sassiety beauty, Mrs.
Cornwallis West. As a child, she sat for the
photographer around her mother’s shoulders —
pick-a-back fashion—and the picture had a large
sale in London. In those days, “York’s” father
adored Mrs. West.
I've held aloof from robbery, and forgery, and killin’,
I’ve always acted fairly by a pal,
Yet I’m blest if I'm not branded us an extra-special vill'ln
By a judge who puts belief in my “ old gal.”J
She pitched her little fable to that learn-ed sympathiser,
And told him she'd been cruelly forsook ;
But if his noble nibs had had to live along o’ ’User
He’d forgive me for a-slinging of my hook.
It was nag, nag, nag at breakfast, tea and dinner,
And she worried me the more 'cause I tried to hold
my tongue.
I reckoned her an angel when I started out to win
her,
But as soon as we got married all my hopes went
bung.
She made my life a burden till I smote her on the
“ smeller,”
Which hurt her, though I did it for the best:
So she went to live with mother, and she mashed another
feller,
And I departed, happy, to the West.
But, later on, I found she'd been protesting unto Madden
That I used to bite and kiok her like a 'orse ;
And 1 saw myself depicted as a truly awful bad ’un
In the papers whioh reported our divorce.
With my curse, curse, curse when she wanted to be
tender;
AVith my stoush, stoush, stoush on her body, limbs
and head !
My duty to the lady was to fondle and defend her,
But I'd been a “ human monster so the kind
Judge said.
Tue talp of my brutality comes rough on me, I'm thinkin’;
I’m pointed at by kiddies in the street;
I can't obtain employment, for my character is stinkin’ ;
I'm a “ dog " in search of anything to eat.
And all along o’ ’User swearing false upon the Bible,
And Madden being such a one to jaw.
Un doesn't stand to suffer heavy damages for libel—
All the tommyrot he utters is the Law.
Tommyrot, rot, rot! When petitioner's a woman,
And the husband isn't present her assertions to
dispute;
He’s the vilest thing in blackguards, and his con-
duct's so inhuman
That the learn-ed Judge regards him as a Loathsome
Brute.

“Hermes”: Melbourne Argus led the way
among the “ great organs of public opinion ” by
loudly denouncing Bishop Thornton’s recent
Ballarat address. When Thornton said we
were one of the strongest, most courageous,
capable, nimble-minded, progressive, and in-
teresting races the world has ever seen, and a
lot more nice things, the Australian flapped bis
ears and stretched himself out languorously for
some more, like a long pussy-cat reaching for a
fly on a hot afternoon. And according to the
great organs aforesaid any person who won’t
fall down and worship the huge idol of Aus-
tralian conceit is an immoral individual with
an unclean mind. Bishop Thornton didn’t say
we are any worse than other people—in fact, he
said we are rather better than most, but, that
with all our excellence, “ below the surface
Australian town life is stained deep with intem-
perance, profanity, and lust even, it is terrible
to record, among our children. . . . Theft is
incessant, and unchastity very lightly thought
of. Suicide is becoming not uncommon. . . .
The relations of the * boom ’ time in Victoria
quite forbid our holding up our heads in
righteous indignation at the Hooley intrigues
and scandals in England.” It is a true bill!
It doesn’t matter that other places are worse,
and that Sodom and Gomorrah had their draw-
backs. That doesn’t make us any better. In
N.S.W., during 1897, out of a population of
1,323,460 there were 3951 lunatics; 3709 chil-
dren were burdened on the State because their
parents couldn’t or wouldn’t support them;
45,578 people were convicted of various offences,
and 187 persons suicided. If the “ great Aus-
tralian dailies ” will carefully consider these
figures they may not do any good, but the
dailies may get into a less bumptious slate of
mind.
“Personal” journalism in polite France
from Lk Tour :—“ We have had the pleasure of
learning that the wound received yesterday by
Morel, the lawyer, is more serious than was
thought. Complicated by a dangerous disease,
there is all room to hope that this wound will
have consequences.

Government Notices.
Land and Income Tax Office, Custom House,
Sydney, 20th December, IS9B.
LAND AND INCOME TAX ASSESSMENT ACT
OF 1895.
NOTICE is hereby given, under section 30 of the
above Act, that Returns of Income received during
the year 1898 for the purposes of the assessment of income
Tax for the year 1899 are required to be furnished to the
Commissioners of Taxation, at their office, on or before
the Uth day of February, 1899, and all persons who are in
receipt of income, and all persons liable under the provi-
sions of the Act in any representative capacity to furnish
returns for the purposes aforesaid, are hereby informed
that the prescribed Forms of Returns are now available,
and may be obtained (except those for Companies) on ap-
plication at any Post Office, Police Office, Custom House,
Railway Station, and the office of any Clerk of Petty Ses-
sions in New South Wales, Stamp Duties Office, Phillip-
street, Sydney, or at the Taxation Office, Custom House,
Sydney. Forms for Companies can be obtained at the
Stamp Duties Office, Phillip-street, Sydney, or at the
Taxation Office, Custom House, Sydney. All persons car-
rying on any business, trade or profession on their own
account are required to furnish Income Returns irrespec-
tive of the amount of their income ; but no return of in-
come is required from any person in receipt of salary or
waqes only if the gross annual amount of such salary or
wages does not exceed £2OO. All employers of labour,
whether they themselves are liable to make Income Tax
Returns or not, are required to furnish to the Commis-
sioners of Taxation the name, occupation, and address of
all employees whose salaries or wages for the year 1898
aggregated £l6O and upwards. Public attention is called
to the fact that a penalty not exceeding twenty pounds
is imposed on any person neglecting to furnish the Return
required of him within the time abovementioned, and
that persons failing to furnish such Return are not re-
lieved from liability to the penalty by reason only that
they may not have received a notice to furnish the Re-
turn, or that the prescribed Form may not have been de-
livered to them. The attention of owners of taxable lands
subject to mortgage is invited to the necessity for furnish-
ing the requisite particulars in order that they may de-
rive the benefit of the deduction to which they are en-
titled in respect of the interest on the mortgage of their
lands, including improvements thereon. Without these
particulars the Commissioners will be unable to make the
statutory deduction from the tax on the unimproved
values. Forms of the prescribed Return may be obtained
on application at any of the abovementioned offices, and
should be furnished on or before the Uth day of February,
1899, before mentioned.
N. LOCKYER,
R. N. JOHNSON, V
L. S. SPILLER, J
Commissioner*
of
Taxation.
N.B. —Particular attention is called to the observations
and directions with the Forms ot Return.

During the Boxing Day crush, Fitzgerald’s circus in
Flinders-street, Melbourne, was incapable of accommodat-
ing all those who paid for admission. But the firm was
equal to the occasion : Brother Tom rushed into the
Australian Church next door and requested Pastor Strong
to oblige with the loan of a few pews. Much to his sur-
prise the good pastor acceded, whereupon half the fittings
of the church were promptly transferred to the tent, and
Dr. Strong and all his family even unto the fourth genera-
tion were placed “ on the door ” of the circus for ever.
The minister himself was one of the most amused spec-
tators around the ring that afternoon.
Many people attend Mdme. Heller's stances who have
never before visited a theatre. One night at Auckland
Opera House, Percy Abbott held out his hand to take
some tickets from a party of ladies whom the doorkeeper
had missed in the rush. Mr. Abbott’s frock-coat, white
tie, and benign clerical expression evidently reminded
them of the parson who stands at the door welcoming his
flock at a church function, for the ladies all shook him
cordially by the hand, and one of them remarked that it
was going to be “ quite a full meeting.” Another night
a party went to the circle and, never having seen a fold-
ing-seat, sat down on the floor between the arms of the
chairs, which came up to their ears. After a while they
went to the door and asked if they could go downstairs
where the seats were more comfortable, as the ones they
were in were so low they could not see what was going
on. Their surprise when the seat was unfolded was
intense.
“ S.S.” : Strong man Sampson only asks for sixpences
and “thrummers” to tear with his fingers. Even a
worn threepenny is a tough item. There was a
woman showing in Ballarat, over 20 years ago, whose
mo3t, surprising trick was drawing 4in. nails out of a
hardwood post’, about an inch of the nail being left, for a
grip. She drew the nails with her fingers, and then with
her teeth. This feat was as obviously genuine as any of
Sampson’s. Wonder if the Utter has ever wrestled with
it ? Would it be a soft snap to him ? Anyhow, it is a
taking item for show purposes.
Brough’s Napoleon would be more convincing if he
could overcome certain Brough-nesses. As, for instance,
standing about with his mouth wide open. One cannot
imagine Napoleon standing with his mouth wide open
while he was being spoken to. No great man ever stood
about with his mouth open. Decision of character tends
rather to a tight compression of the lips.
George and Henry Leopold, who engineered the
Alexandra Pantomime this Xmas, made their first Melb.
appearance in the harlequinade of “ Dick Whittington,”
Theatre Royal, 1857. Their brother Tom was clown.
“ Paradis” : Bland Holt introduces five bulls in “New
Babylon.” Not Irish ones. Joe Brown was never able to
stage his bullock-team drama. Bland is nothing, if not
circusy. He put on a pieoe without horses, but somehow
it didn’t quite gee.
“ Haresfoot ” : Apropos Madame Sans-GSne. Napoleon,
to get rid of his troublesome sister, Pauline—the lady in
the altogether—ordered her off to San Domingo with her
husband, General Leclerc, as Governor. Pauline wrought
on Leclerc’s fears by representing that his young sister
would be left defenceless in Paris. Leclerc spoke of this
to the Emperor. Napoleon: “ I’ll find her a husband.”
Shortly afterwards Marshal Davoust came in to obtain
the Emperor’s consent to his marriage with a rich widow.
He said : “Sire, 1 have to ask your consent to my mar-
riage with ” Napoleon : “ Oh ! yes, I know, Mdlle.
Leclerc.” Davoust: “ No, Sire 1” But he had to marry
her.
“M. 8.5.": A few weeks ago, Tub Bulletin remarked
that Henry Arthur Jones had, in “The Liars,” simply
repeated his own “ Case of Rebellious Susan ” with a few
variations. Now Nisbet, dramatic critic of the London
Times and of the Acadkmy, states that Jones’ last play,
“The Manoeuvres of Jane,” has simply repeated “The
Liars”: “ It is a piece written on the same lines and in
the same spirit,” says Nisbet. “ But whereas the lying
of Mr. Jones’ earlier piece was in some sort involuntary
and forced upon the liars by circumstances, in the
present instance it is deliberate and long continued and
so much the less excusable.”
Charles Itenningham has, it would seem, been telling a
Melb. interviewer of his achievements as a libretto “ im-
prover” as well as a musical composer. He was engaged
for a comic-opera which had a “ shockingly bad ” book-
awful stuff, doncherknow, written by .1. M. Barrie and
Conan Doyle. Kenningham couldn’t tolerate the “ tripe”
of such vain pretenders, so he and Rutland Barrington
(a somewhat aged baritone) set to work to rewrite the
libretto in a couple of hours. Kenningham didn’t tell
the interviewer whether the reconstructed opera was a
brilliant success, but, if it didn’t “ knock ” the house, the
failure was due to the weakness of the Barrie-Doyle plot,
no doubt. Some opera-books defy even the efforts of a
Kenningham to “lick them into shape.” The Bulletin
would advise manager Williamson to let his imported
tenor knock up an opera for production at the Princess’s
shortly—sav “ The Bounding Notion,” written and com-
posed by Kenningham, and introducing his immortal
ballad, “ O 1 Can it be Blow ? ”

living only a. few miles off at the time. The horse she
rode died in ’75, near Coombing Park, and was about 26
years of a^e.—Yours, Abercrombie. IPerhaps the “ old

Mrs. Brown-Potter, who had scored a big success as
Miladi*, in “The Three Musketeers ”—a part in which all
her dainty mannerisms count for histrionic virtues—was
laid up with pleurisy at latest dates. On the first night
of Mrs. P.'s illness the gap was filled by Gracie Warner.
The Firm, it seems, is not to have an Australian mono-
poly of the “ souvenir” system of advertising. At Melb.
Opera House, the other night, every feminine visitor was
supplied with a 36-page “ souvenir,” containing portraits
of the principal artists engaged by Rickards during the
past four years.
On a recent night a number of Sydney musicians fore-
gathered for a common beefsteak supper, but the popular
manager of a musical depot was unfortunate enough to
suggest that- fizz was better for the musicianly throat
than beer. The frolic only lasted an hour and a half, and
it cost him nigh on £2O.
“ Silas Snell" : It should be evident now even to the
most hopeful that Melbourne does not want good plays
well played. I find the Royal crowded night after night,
and the liijou almost desolate. At the Royal, Bland Holt
is producing “New Babylon ” ;at the Bijou, Robert Brough
supplies “ Madame Sans-Gene.” Bland Holt produces the
melodrama splendidly ; Robert Brough’s treatment of the
drama is susceptible of many improvements. Andjret at
the best the melodrama is only the same old garish, bald
and awful rushing to literary extremes, and the drama at
its worst is a good play, fairly artistic and full of incident.
The theatres are not - 50 yards apart, and prices to the
popular quarters are practically the same, and yet the
old familiar setting of the old, familiar incidents and the
crude contrasts of unmitigated virtue and perfect vice
draw enthusiastic pushes, and at the Bijou the voice of
Sardou is the voice of one crying in the wilderness. The
Melb. playgoer declares he wants to be amused. Plays
like “ The Liars,” “ Lord and Lady Algy," and “ Sans-
Gene ” should amuse him immensely, and do not; play*
like “ New Babylon ” should not amuse him and do.
There is the problem. “Teach the play-goer better,"
savs somebody. Humph ! That is how fortunes are ost.
Te'aohin-* the public better is about the most hopeless,
heart-breaking, and thankless task ever undertaken by
man and the teacher always dies before his pupils dis-
cover what the deuce he is doing. Try to teach the pub-
lic by giving it better '.—better give it best.
A specimen verse quoted in the Firm s advt. of “The
Geisha ” is not deeply impressive :
He called her the Jewel of Asia,
But she was the Queen of the Geisha.*
So she laughed : “ Though you’re ready to-day, Sir,
To flirt when I flutter my fan,
To-morrow you’ll go on your way, sir,
Forgetting the girl of Japan.”
“ Admirer ” : Wilson Barrett is sft. Bin. Anybody who
calls him 6ft. *in. has the jaundice. The profession re-
cognises that his stage figure has been the model of sym-
metry.
Ethel Haydon’s husband appears to be somewhat
“naughty” in his music-hall ditties. One of them relates
to the girls in the room above, who bore holes through
the ceiling and watch him as he undresses his manly pro-
portions at night.
News comes from London of the death, on Sept. 30, at
St. Mary’s Hospital, of Harry Power, Irish comedian,
aged 52 years. His real name was Alexander Morrison,
and he flourished at the old Vic. under John Bennett’s
management in the 70’s.
“Sector” : The late Johnson was architect of Melb.
Opera House, and Cornwell, Madame Midas’s father, was
contractor. Has she got to a nunnery V

Politics became of the bedstead variety on
the last night of the Vic. session. Dr. Carty
Salmon, MlL.A., held forth in this style (see
Hansard) on a subject he should know something
about:—
Young: mothers seemed to consider it unnecessary that
they should give to their children that sustenance which
Nature had provided for them. There were too many
j “bottle babies” in this community. It was very easy
* for the experienced person to observe, even after the
lapse of many years, the child which was brought up in
an unnatural fashion, and which was called a “ bottle
, baby.” He regarded these yountr women as being merely
incubators. They just performed the same functions as
a machine which was kept at a certain temperature for a
certain number of days for the purpose of incubation)
and they did little or nothing to keep alive the animal
which was produced from the time of incubation. (Mr.
Murray-“They are afraid of spoiling their figures. ’)
That seemed to be the case, although, if they only knew
it, the very action which they took was most destructive
to what they desired to attain.

The Vic. House of Rich Landlords gave a
dinner recently to their hero Sargood, whereat
brewer Fitzgerald did solemnly declare that Sar-
good's perseverance, industry, and peculiar genius
as an “improver” of legislation accounted for the
present popularity of the Hupper ’Ouse. The
country was never before so proud of its 48 bloated
obstructionists, said the unblushing beerist, and
Sargood is the draper who has made them famous
and beloved. To which, and ti lot more of it,
Sargood replied in a spirit of lofty patriot-
ism, disclaiming any special personal credit
for the Council’s misdeeds. “ The great secret
of the strength of the Legislative Council is
that they always hang together,” he reckons,
and The Buw.etin feels quite sure that Sargood
reckons rightly. It is in not criticising itself that
the Council names out strong. The Assembly
forms two or more parties for exposing one
another’s foolishness at times, but the Higher
Chamber (Lord bless it!) consists of only one
party —Fatrnan—whose solo object in life is to
hang together and resist encroachments upon the
Rights of Proputty. Patman doesn’t move,
neither does ho talk for the most part. Ho simply
hangs together, and smells unpleasantly, like a bit
of meat which has outlived its usefulness.

Mesmerised.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I went to Mr. Hirem’s entertainment, and got
mad on mesmerism. I came home and made
passes till the family thought it was St. Vitus.
In railway-carriages I stared young women out of
countenance. My sweetheart has broken it off
she says she wants a man who can do something
beside sta-are. I tried it on the cat. I inveigled
the office-boy home, at a cost of 55., and tried it
on him. He got fidgetty, but not mesmerised—
and the little brute went and told the clerks about
it. At pedestrians I darted glances like to wither
them, and even experimented on meek-looking
customers who came to the office. But all to no
purpose—l couldn’t mesmerise a living creature.
I got all the books out of the library, but could
make no progress. I was getting sane again, and
could eat a mutton-chop or so when this hap-
pened.
I was walking along Bourke-street and ap-
proached a man, travelling in the opposite direc-
tion, whose face seemed familiar. He was shabby
and dirty and beer-blossomy, and suddenly the
truth flashed upon me—he was one of Hirem’s
“ subjects,” the pianist. The man who played
rapturously on a deal-table, turned over imaginary
musio like an artist, thumped and pedalled and
wriggled, and who remained for 15 minutes like
an all-time statue—with his fingers raised ready
to strike the notes—the moment “the master”
took the influence off. He it was who had quaffed
the mustard-cnw-kerosene-vinegar-and-pepper and
sucked the candles, and before he oame a yard
nearer I had made up my mind. It was the
chanoe of my life. I strode up to him, made a
rapid pass, said in a commanding voice, “ Shut
your eyes!” and—joy I —he was mine. Yes,
mine.
He shut his eyes and assumed the un-spined
attitude I knew so well. We were close to the
Book Aroade ; there were dozens of people around,
and I was frightened at my success. I remem-
bered the formula, and said to him : “ When you
open your eyes your name is Mr. Lake ; you iive
at Warracknabeal; you are a customer of mine ;
you have come down to buy some barbed-wire
from me—open your eyes ! ”
He did, and then it dawned upon me that I was
in a mess. I had an engagement at the office
almost due—with the real Mr. Lake, too. I must
get rid of this idiot pretty soon, but I wasn’t quite
sure if I knew the way. I hadn’t paid much
attention to the bring-’em-out-of-it regulations. I
felt somewhat ashamed of his company as
we walked along, and the fool could talk
about nothing but barbed-wire. I took him in
to have a drink, and “suggested” that his
was brandy and whisky mixed. I was gratified
to find that he couldn’t resist the suggestion, and
felt my courage return. I knew now that I could
get rid of him at any moment. The offioe is in
King-street, and I walked him thitherward. I
planned to take him as far as William-street, and
un-mesmerise him in a quiet spot. Before we got
to the right place a friend passed, nodded to me,
and took stock of my disreputable-looking com-
panion. A little further on that office-boy came
strolling past, affecting not to see us, but com-
prehending the whole of the business as I thought.
These circumstances upset me a little, but near-
ing Menzies’ the coast was clear, and the moment
had arrived. I glanced round to make sure that
the office-boy wasn’t watching, and, in doing so,
had the misfortune to see Lake (the real one) com-
ing along on the other side of the street. I was
half-inclined to run, but was afraid the brute
would follow. I nerved up, put my hand on his
forehead, and said, “ Shut your eyes ! When you
open your eyes you will be a loafer again, and
will go about your usual business, if you have any.
Open your eyes ! ”
He didn’t, because he had never shut them ;
but he opened his mouth to some purpose. He
said, “Look here, mister, you’ve been havin’ a
game with me, and now yer wan ter get rid of me,
don’t yer ? Well, look here ; you give me ten bob
and I’ll slope, straight—see! ”
Lake was now right opposite ; saw me, and
angled across the street towards us. I took some
money out of my pocket, and said, “ Here’s five
shillings ; now you get! ”
“No yer don’t, mister,” he whined; “I never
do it under ten bob, ’cept it’s for a long engage-
ment—l ain’t a-goin’ ter go till I get my proper
fee, see ? ”
Lake was now at the kerb. I forked out two
more half-crowns with, “ Will you go now, you
cur ? ”
“All right, mister, don’t get waxy,” and he
slunk off.
Lake hoped I hadn’t been giving money to that
drunken-looking fellow—“ They invariably drink
it,” he said, “ and it only keeps them from trying
to earn an honest penny.” Whereupon I said it
was merely a trifle ; and then we got to business.
I have taken to amateur photography lately and
do a little at golf, but I leave mesmerism to “ the
master.”
DAEDALUS.
“ Mr. A. O. Sachse, M.L.0., who has been
on a visit to England, has started on his return to
Melbourne."— (Argus cablegram.) This is rather
less interesting than the ordinary cable nows that
John James Brownjones has achieved his B.A.
degree or become a duly qualified surgeon. Mel-
bourne knows nothing of Sachse, M.L.C., except
that he figured as somebody’s brother-in-law in a
family squabble case tried at Melb. Law Courts
some years back. Sachse was mentioned as the
funny man of the family, who had a way of pitch-
ing his hat into the room as a notification that he
was about to follow.
From North Coolgardie (W.A.) Herald’s
report of a local court-case: —
Prisoner: Your Worships, I would like to make a
statement.
Mr. Ballard, J.P.: You can make your statement when
the time oomes.
Witness (continuing) : Accused then said he would
either have to withdraw his words or take a d good
hiding.
Prisoner : I plead justification, your Worship, and ask
to be allowed to employ a solicitor.
Mr. Ballard : Shut up, man ; what do you want a solici-
tor for when you have already pleaded guilty ?
Prisoner continued addressing the Bench.
Mr. Ballard : By the Lord Harry, man, if you don’t
shut up I will send you up for 14 days more than you are
going to get on this charge.
Prisoner : I plead justification.
Mr. Ballard : Will you shut up, man 1
The dignity of the Sandgroper bench
The Drought at Mungerebar.—
A Fable.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Really, I can’t remember his name. There are
such a lot of them here in Heaven, you know, that
one gets a bit mixed. I, myself, perhaps, may
some fine morning—but, there, I won’t brag.
Anyhow, let’s call him Smith. He had been
dead a good few centuries. But only lately he got
canonised for some reason or other, and had been
duly invested with the title as soon as the appoint-
ment was known. He didn’t feel quite comfort-
able in his new dignity, and he said that the halo
was bad for the eyes. But that was mere pose.
His billet, however, was no sinecure. They
kept him busy with earthly affairs, intercessions
and appeals innumerable—for men rightly reasoned
that new brooms sweep clean ; and, when I come
to consider it, there isn’t much difference between
a just-elected M.P. and a recently-canonised saint
—bar on the point of veracity. And, with com-
mendable zeal, St. Smith transacted his agency
business, taking a real and personal interest in
every single case.
Thus it happened one day, just as I was putting
a new E string into my harp, that the saint hailed
me : “ I say, old man, you come from Queensland,
don’t you ? Where is that locality, at all ? You
will excuse my ignoranoe, but there are so very
few from that region in Heaven.”
He wasn’t strong on geography, and he had died
long before the discovery of Australia. So I ex-
plained as well as I could, and he continued :
“ Know a place called Mungerebar ? ”
I knew it, worse luck ! It had been a cattle-
station out West when I was in the flesh, and I
had been there myself. Moreover, that sojourn
had nearly cost me my pension up here, und I was
able to give detailed information.
“ Well, well! H’m, h’m ! ” muttered the saint.
He was very consequential in conversation with
his inferiors. “ Let’s see ! Humble petition—er—
gracious intervention—er—most holy and sinless
father—very good—the faithful of Mungerebar—
phew—no rain for seventeen years ! Humph ! You
say thev are an awful crowd. Very sad, very sad !
Earth lias gone to the dogs since I left—but, I
suppose we’ll have to humor them a bit, poor
mortals. Might teaoh them to regard the—er—
saints with more devotion. Yes, I’ll see to it at
once.”
Immeasurably far away, millions of light-years
beyond the farthest confines of the outermost
stardust, the Spirit of God was hovering over the
deep, in the awful silence of Nothingness. Not
even the searching flashes of revolving worlds
oould penetrate the darkness that had slept there
for untold reons, and as God lifted His eyes and
saw the loneliness and hopelessness, as He felt the
icy coldness of the night, He shuddered. “ More
worlds ! More suns ! More stars 1 ” He mur-
mured. He stretched forth His all-powerful
hand, and the shiver of creation stirred through
the emptiness. And God spoke: “There shall
bo ”
“May it please Your Majesty,” irrelevantly
squeaked a little voice at His side ; “ may it please
Your Majesty, Your servant humbly pleads for
mercy, for forgiveness for the undeserving ! ”
Frowningly the Lord turned. “ The people of
Mungerebar,’’ the voice continued, “have prayed
and asked of Thee to~ intervene in their behalf.
They have solemnly repented of their sins, and
their cries of anguish have penetrated through
the golden portal. For behold, they have had no
rain for 17 years and ’’
The Lord interrupted : “ Where is Mungere-
bar ? ’’
“ In—er—Western Queensland, I believe.”
“ And where is Western Queensland? ”
“In Australia—on the Earth,” explained St.
Smith.
Calmly, the Lord continued : “And where is
the Earth ? ”
The saintcollapsed. Oneof the Recording Angel’s
staff was ordered to look up “ Earth ” in the office-
directory.
“It will take some time, Your Majesty,” re-
monstrated the overworked clerk. “In the stellar
system Mr. Smith comes from there are alone
9537 million billions of ”
“ You’ll have to find it. Your books don’t
seem to be in the very best order. I’ll give you
a trillion centuries to look it up. Now, be off,
both of you 1 ” And God turned towards ex-
pectant space again.
St. Smith grinned hysterically (for swearing is
not allowed in Heaven) at the Recording-clerk,
and the latter, in appreciation of his task, picked
up a passing comet and Hung it viciously at the
Southern Cross. But as the missile only travels
at the fate of 1350 miles per second, it will take
some millions of years yet till that grand constel-
lation gets smashed to smithereens.
However, it is not likely that Mungerebar will
have any rain for another 200 years. Luckily,
most residents don’t expeot it till then, either.

“ 0.5." to The Bulletin :
“J.S.T.’B” comments (B. 12'll/’9B) on “T.’s” opinion
that social perambulation in the “altogether” would settle
the sexual question, do not seem to hit the bull’s eye.
“ J.S.T." reckons that general nakedness would largely
increase the birth-rate, and perhaps it might at first; hut
the next generation would certainly he less oonoupiscent.
Not so long ago Japanese men and women bathed to-
gether freely in public and the “ altogether," and plenty
of white me.n did ditto, with “ moosnnes” to towel them
afterwards. The Japs themselves, of course, took no
notice of each,other’s want of clothing, and though
the white man did at first, the novelty very soon
wore off and he became as inured as the natives to
the Adam and live business. Moreover, Jap. sexual
morality was in those days a long way better than
Sydney ditto. Adultery with a married woman was al-
most unknown (the penalty was extremely hideous) and if
prostitution was not considered disgraceful it was, at any
rate, severely restricted. Observation of both systems,
Jap. and Australian, in fact, convinces this scribe that
there’s little to choose as regards prevalence—while in all
other respects the Jap. custom is the less coarse
and sheeny animal. As for promiscuous indulgence,
the amorous Jap “ lady” is by no means al-fresco-ly im-
modest us her Sydney like. “ J.S.T s ” fears, therefore,
are not warranted by any known results elsewhere. Also,
when he talks of a municipal Flossie to, come some day,
he is merely reviving the old ideiv of a system of socialistic
soiled doves, sanctioned by the Church of the Middle
Ages. Prostitution at that time was placed upon a
guild-organisation ; brothels were Municipal, Crown, or
Church establishments, and enjoyed special protection,
whilst their principals swore to be faithful and friendly
to their respective towns.


A., B. and C. are intimates. B. is sus-
pected of too deep an admiration of A.’s wife.
The three are in a bar parlor, just about to
demolish long drinks, when Mrs. A. passes the
window. “ Excuse me,” says B., rising hastily,
and disappears. A. quaffs his glass serenely. 0.
(excitedly), “Why don’t you follow him, man?
He’s gone after your wife 1 ” A. (glancing round
eagerly and reaching over the table), “Has he?
By jove, I’ll drink his beer ! ” (Does so).

Healthy, Happy Grirls
often, from no apparent cause, become languid and despon-
dent in the early days of their womanhood. They drag
along, always tired, never hunger, breathless, and with a
palpitating heart after slight exercise, so that merely to walk
upstairs is exhausting. Sometimes a short, dry cough leads
to the fear that they are “ going into consumption.”
They are anaemic, doctors tell them, which means that
they have too little blood. Are you like that ? Have you
too little blood ?
More anaemic people have been made strong, hungry,
energetic men and women by the use of Dp- Williams’
Fink Pills for Pale People than by any other
means. They are the best Tonic in the world.
Miss Rose Kexnerdale, of Carnarvon-street, North Auburn, Sydney, had been a very healthy girl until
about 18 months ago, when she grew weak and pale. She lost her appetite, was as tired in the morning as on
retiring, and lost fh-sh until she became so emaciated that her friends hardly knew her. Her physician declared
the disease, anaemia, and his treatment did not appear to restore her. At last she tried Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
for Pale People. After the second box, her improvement was magical. She is now well and strong, and
endowed with sparkling eyes and rosy cheeks.
