Mariana in the Shingle Hut.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On my selection all is slow
And hot and dry and God-forsaken ;
The grass is done, the water’s low ;
All Hope is dead, all Faith is shaken;
The waterholes are choked with mud,
And lined with corpses—rotting—lying.
If I could see a good old flood
I think the sight would set me crying.
That barren spot before my place
That once, alas ! I called a garden,
Now mocks me with its sun-dried face,
And evermore doth waste and harden.
Y et here I’m tied and must abide
Po wait for Government inspection,
And yet once more I curse the law
That makes me live on my selection.
L ist night the air was deadly still;
Midst heavy heat my brain was burning !
1 could not sleep for hours—until
All wearied out with restless turning.
A nd in my dreams one came and stood,
Whose voice was sweet as seraph-singing—
Who said, “ The Marthayuy’s in Hood,
And all around the grass is springing / "
Yes, in my dreams the drought was broke,
And through the bark the rain was streaming.
The sun was up when I awoke !
The sun was up and I’d been dreaming /
O barren plains, and burning sky !
O bitterness of deep dejection !
Thrice cursed spot, thrice cruel lot,
Why must I live on my selection 1
I f I were rich, if I were left
Sole legatee of Jimmy Tyson,
Would I stay here, remote, bereft,
And gaze on this forlorn horizon !!
The stock might die, the tanks go dry,
The rotting sheep might spead infection
With noisome breath—and Grisly Death
Might hold his court on my selection 1
The skies might sodden be, or fair,
The glass might register 200°,
I should not care, I’d not be there,
My property and I’d be sundered.
O if you doubt my tale of drought,
Pray take a turn in this direction.
Just spend a week at Myall Creek,
Just stop a month on my selection !
JESSIE PATERSON.
“ The pitcher is broken at last.” Alexan-
der Murray, the South Australian whose name is
on millions of jam-tins scattered up and down
Australia, had ridden a favorite horse for twenty
years. But the ancient animal never took kindly
to the bicycle. One day it plunged at sight of one,
and a useful citizen was dead.
A Woman’s Love.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She took up the specialist’s reply. Her fingers
trembled as she opened it:
Yours of t he sth to hand. Prom your report of your
husband’s case, and from notes duly received from your
family doctor, I am strongly of opinion that your hus-
band's eyesight may be restored. Of course till I examine
the case myself I cannot be more explicit. If your hus-
band wishes to place himself in my hands, let me know
as soon as possible, as my stay in this city is limited.
The letter fell on the table, and through the
open window the sea breeze fluttered its pages.
But she clutched it back with a vague feeling
that with that scrap of paper would go all that it
represented, freedom from care, light, new life
for the one she loved best on earth. The sea-girt
landscape took on brighter colors as though she
were already watching it through her husband’s
opened eyes. The wonder and glory of it all.
The answer had come - so soon ! The little chink
of hope had widened into a beautiful probability.
A voice from the inner room. She rose, slip-
ping the letter into her pocket. She felt an im-
pulse to dally with her happiness—and his.
He was in the one-time studio, bare, dismantled;
yet he clung to it with all its associations. He
was sitting by the window, and, with brush up-
raised, was painting the air with long, sweeping
strokes.
“ Well, Maggie ! ” He turned at the rustle of
her dress.
They had been young when they married ten
years ago—twenty and twenty-five respectively.
He was young still; his oval face, the glow of gold
in hair and moustache, gave a brightness to his
face which even the vacancy of the blue eyes
oould not counteract.
She had been handsome, too, but the anxiety
born of her husband’s affliction had iuduced aii
illness the effects of which were still visible and
would, possibly, never pass away. She was only
thirty, but her face was haggard, her thin, yellow
hair coarsened with grey.
“Still painting!” She laid her hand on his
curling hair. She was feeling in anticipation the
joy that was to follow. “ You are always paint-
ing now.” She could be playful on the subject.
A few months, and he would be painting in
reality.
“ Always, my dear.” He put the brush down
with a little smile. “Do you know, when I have it
in my hands it sometimes seems to give a clair-
voyant sense of sight. I can see this room so
plainly now. The curtain blown Bideways, that
bust of Psyche, the easels—no, they are all away
now, with the pupils. It is very empty, this
studio ; I can see the emptiness though it did not
change —till afterwards. But there is no change
about you, Maggie. I can see you now ”
“ Can you, dear ? ” She spoke dreamily. She
was used to being “seen.” Besides, she was
thinking of the letter.
“Yes, I can see you now.” The blank blue
eyes looked at her with such an inner keenness
that she shivered—for a moment she could not tell
why. He leant forward. “ Your hair, so soft
and yellow ” he touched the grizzled locks
tenderly—“ I never let you do it in a tangled
fringe like other girls. You used to lament over
this, don’t you remember ? You said it looked so
untidy when the wind blew. But I don’t know—
it was like a field, at evening, when the wind is
blowing, and the sun slants across it with that
dazzling yellow gleam that yet never dazzles—
dear, pretty hair ! ”
The woman shrank away from his caress. There
was a sudden fear in her heart. His very finger-
tips seemed to have eyes in them.
“ And your eyes—blue, like mine ; but softer.
A bluish haze seemed to spread into the whites of
them—like a child’s eyes, you know. I see them
now, Maggie.” Involuntarily her dimmed eyes
closed. “ And your cheek —it seems a little
thinner now, but there is still that pure curve
from brow to chin. How I love that curve—see,
here it is.”
He drew it in the air, and she put her hands to
her haggard face. Her mind was in a whirl ;
then one thought came uppermost.
“ You love me, Arthur, you love me—for my
beauty! ”
“ Now, my dear,” he spoke with tender gaiety,
“ don’t be like the common woman and pretend
to misunderstand—stupidly. I do love you for
your beauty. I cannot separate the two. Your
beauty is you ; you are your beauty. An artist’s
love ! Ever hear of an artist marrying a plain
woman ? The musician may ; the poet does,
almost invariably. But an artist —never ! Beauty
is necessary for us. Other men see with their
eyes, we with our souls. Other men see the
whole, dimly, not knowing what they see ; we,
the different parts individually, the curve, the
shadow, the gleam, and the whole as well. Not a
touch of sensualism in this. Blind as I am,
Maggie, your beauty delights me as much as in
those other days, before this came upon me. I see
you still, Maggie ; thank God, I can see you still.”
“ You see me still! ” The words came softly,
as if without her consent. Her eyes were fixed on
the blue curve of the distant hills, fresh, fair
hills, whose beauty no sorrow ever impaired, “If
I were old and haggard, what then?”
“It would not be you,” he answered, simply,
“ but you will never grow old, Maggie. Yours is
a beauty that does not fade, only matures—a
steadier light in the eyes, a deeper shade in the
hair; that is all. Maggie ” —his voice changed to
a note of appeal—“in spite of this inner sight of
mine, I long to see you with the outer vision, to
see the world again, to go back to my old work ;
but, more thau all, to see you, Maggie, to see my
beautiful wife again. I suppose ” —he spoke
anxiously—“ it is too early yet for the doctor’s
answer? ”
Her gaze turned to the hills again. There was
no sorrow in her face now.
“ Not too early, Arthur ; the answer has come.”
“ And what is it, Maggie, dearest—the worst —
or best ? ”
There was silence a moment; then the sharp
sound of paper torn to fragments. She put her
hand on his shoulder.
“He says—there is no hope, Arthur. You will
—never see me again ! ”
CONSTANOE CLYDE.
“H.” : In a vol. of Edinburgh Review,
I find this vouched for—l put in a name for a
blank : Lord Devilham married a young lady, who
purported to be a widow. She died on the Con-
tinent, but directed her body to be buried at a
Dover church. Devilham took it, coffined, over
the Channel. The Dover Customs-people insisted
on inspection. The husband could only get out
by allowing the Dover clergyman to see the dead.
The clergyman found that it was his own wife.

KATI-KATI.


The Message of the Spear.
Away back in the year 54 b.®.— 1948 yf‘ ars a o°~
a little detatehment of Rowan soldiers was
beseiged by a host of Gauls at Cbaleroi, in the
north of what is now called France. Believing
that no help could possibly reach them, the
Romans expected to fight until they were a
striken down. One day a short spear came
whizzing over-the ramparts and stuck in one o
the wooden towers. Nobody paid any attention
to it at first. Tbev just went on slaughtering
Gauls and getting slaughtered themselves. At
lencfth an officer saw something fluttering from
that spear. It proved to be a note from Juluas
Cassar— written in Greek. He said he was on his
way with an army to relieve the garrison. that
note had been there unheeded for several days.
In due time Caesar came and made short work of
the Gauls.
That people should suffer and struggle hope-
lessly when help.is near at hand is sad to think of,
but they must know of the coming help before they
can draw courage from it. Take the torments and
dangers of disease for example. We fight them
with all the weapons we have. hit
on the right thing, and more often we don’t, even
when it is as dose as the spear in the tower was to
the exhausted (tarrison.
If Mr. Robert Lavis, postmaster at High Ham,
Langport, Somerset, could have found a remedy
for indigestion, dyspepsia and liver complaint, he
certainly would not have suffered from it, as he
did, for twenty years. Why, that is more than
half an average lifetime. A single day of illness
is always long enough, goodness knows. But
fancy such an experience stretching itself into
weeks, months, and years ! Common enough ?
Yes, dreadfully common. So is poverty; but
does that fact reconcile anybody to either V Mr.
Lavis is not a man to sit tamely down and b.rood
over a misfortune. Besides being postmaster he
is a grocer, with work enough to keep him busy.
Under what difficulties this'work was done he
tells us in a letter dated Nov. 8, 181)3.
“ I had a bad taste in the mouth,” ho says,
“particularly in the morning, and my mouth was
dry, and I spat up thick, tenacious phlegm. After
eating even the simplest food I had great pain in
the chest and around the heart. I suffered greatly
from sick headache and giddiness, and if I exer-
ciser! much 1 got out of breath.”
[This was asthma, caused by the stupefying
action of foul blood upon the nerves that move the
lungs; the impurities in the blood having come
from the festering mass of undigested food in the
stomach. 'I'lie heart trouble, the sick headache,
and the giddiness were symptoms of the same
thing.]
“As time went on,” continues Mr. Lavis, “I
became very low, weak, and mentally discouraged
and depressed.”
[A doctor, writing about this case for a learned
medical journal, would use many tough Latin
words, but he would not make the facts as plain
as Mr. Lavis himself has made them. Naturally
a man who can eat but little—-which little mostly
rots in his stomach—will lose’ flesh and strength
and come to be of small use to himself of to any-
one else. We can all see that even by candle-
light.]
“! tried many medicines,” he says finally,
“ but they did me no real good. At last I heard
of Mother Seigel's Curative Syrup, and used it. I
am happy to tell ypu that it soon relieved and
cured me. Whenever 1 feel a sign of rny old ail-
ment I resort to the Syrup at once, and the disease
gets no further hold upon me. Publish my state-
ment if you like, and believe me yours, &o.,
(Signed) Robert Lavis.”
The Roman soldiers left Caesar’s message unre-
garded for days. It was written on parchment and
fluttered from a spear, as I have said. But, see !
We live in the age of print. Are ypu. ill and in
pain as Mr. Lavis was? Every newspaper, every
magazine, almost every publication your eve falls
on, contains accounts of what Mother Seigel’s
Syrup has done, and daily does, for those who
suffer. Read the messages. This is one; and,
having read it, you know where to look for help.
